Refutation of Kawn Causality: The Dilemma
Razi refutes the later scholars' view that ka'iniyya is caused by a meaning (kawn).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the theory that spatial occurrence requires a separate cause (kawn), and the two impossible consequences he derives: circularity (dawr) and separation of cause from effect.

Core Idea
Postulating a hidden cause for spatial location leads to circularity or separation of cause and effect — both impossible.

Razi now tackles a specific theological theory: that an atom's being in a particular place is caused by a meaning called kawn. He shows that this theory collapses into one of two logical absurdities. By following his dilemma, you will see how kalam reasoning exposes hidden contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma
Read the first part where Razi sets up the two possibilities. Notice the logical structure: either the meaning depends on location or it does not.
This is the classic 'division' (qisma) technique in kalam.
2
Examine the first horn
See why circularity (dawr) follows if the meaning's subsistence depends on the substance being in that space. Trace the mutual dependency.
Circularity is impossible because it would mean each depends on the other.
3
Examine the second horn
If the meaning does not depend on location, then it could exist without the substance being there, meaning the cause exists without its effect — also impossible.
This is the principle that a cause cannot be separated from its effect.
4
Review the second argument and objection
Razi adds a second argument based on the nature of accident subsistence, then notes an objection that requires further investigation.
This shows Razi's intellectual honesty in acknowledging potential counterpoints.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الأولى وهذا القول عندنا باطل وذلك لأن المعنى الذي يوجب حصول الجوهر في حيز معين إما أن يتوقف صحة قيامه بالجوهر على كون ذلك الجوهر حاصلا في ذلك الحيز وإما أن لا يتوقف عليه. والأول باطل لأنا إذا عللنا حصول ذلك الجوهر في ذلك الحيز بذلك المعنى كان حصول ذلك الجوهر في ذلك الحيز محتاجا إلى قيام ذلك المعنى به وقيام ذلك المعنى به يكون محتاجا إلى حصول ذلك الجوهر في ذلك الحيز فيلزم الدور وهو محال. والثاني أيضا باطل لأن على هذا التقدير يجوز حصول ذلك المعنى عندما لا يكون ذلك الجوهر حاصلا في ذلك الحيز وذلك يقتضي جواز حصول العلة منفكة عن معلولها وهو محال. فتعين أن تعليل الكائنية بالمعنى يفضي إلى أحد هذين الباطلين. والدليل عليه أنا بينا أن كون العرض قائما بالجوهر معناه حصول ذلك العرض في ذلك الحيز تبعا لحصول محله فيه. فلو كان حصول الجوهر في الحيز معللا بمعنى قائم به لكان حصول ذلك الجوهر في ذلك الحيز تبعا لقيام ذلك المعنى من حيث أن المعلول تبع للعلة. فيلزم كون كل واحد منهما تبعا للآخر وهو محال. ولقائل أن يقول إن عنيت بالتوقف آخر الموقوف عليه لا يلزم وهذه الحجة فيها بحث.
The First Issue: This statement is, in our view, false. This is because the meaning that necessitates the occurrence of the substance in a particular space: either the validity of its subsisting in the substance depends on that substance being occurring in that space, or it does not depend. The first is false, because if we explain the occurrence of that substance in that space by that meaning, then the occurrence of that substance in that space would be in need of the subsistence of that meaning in it, and the subsistence of that meaning in it would be in need of the occurrence of that substance in that space—thus circularity (dawr) follows, which is impossible. The second is also false, because on this assumption it is possible for that meaning to obtain when that substance is not occurring in that space, and that would imply the possibility of the cause being separated from its effect, which is impossible. So it is determined that explaining ka'iniyya by the meaning leads to one of these two impossibilities. The proof of this is that we have shown that the accident's subsisting in the substance means that accident's occurrence in that space as a consequence of the occurrence of its locus in it. If, therefore, the occurrence of the substance in space were caused by a meaning subsisting in it, then the occurrence of that substance in that space would be a consequence of the subsistence of that meaning, since the effect follows the cause. This would entail each of them being a consequence of the other, which is impossible. And someone might say: 'If you mean by dependency [i.e., the last thing depended upon], it does not follow.' And this argument requires investigation.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the causal theory that ka'iniyya (spatial occurrence) is caused by a meaning called kawn. The author (Razi) presents a dilemma: either the meaning's subsistence in the substance depends on the substance being in that space, or it does not. If it does, circularity arises because the meaning's subsistence requires the substance's occurrence, and the substance's occurrence requires the meaning's subsistence. If it does not, then the meaning could exist without the substance being in that space, which means the cause could exist without its effect—also impossible. Thus both horns lead to absurdity. He adds a second argument: since an accident's existence in a substance means it follows the substance's location, if the substance's location were caused by a meaning, then the substance's location would follow the meaning's subsistence, and simultaneously the meaning's subsistence follows the substance's location (since it inheres), resulting in mutual consequence, which is impossible. The passage ends with an objection: perhaps the opponent means something else by 'dependency'. Razi acknowledges the argument requires investigation.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
دور
تعليل
كائنية
كون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation denies that spatial occurrence itself exists or that it can be caused.
Correction: Razi does not deny spatial occurrence; he only rejects the need to posit a separate 'meaning' as its cause. The theory being refuted is an unnecessary multiplication of entities.

Checkpoint
What are the two impossible consequences that Razi claims follow from the theory that ka'iniyya is caused by a meaning?
A) Either the cause exists without the effect or the effect exists without the cause.
B) Either the substance would not need a cause or the cause would be eternal.
C) Either circularity (dawr) or separation of cause from effect (in'ikak al-'illa 'an ma'luliha).
D) Either the meaning would be a body or it would be an accident.

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that attempting to explain spatial location by a hidden meaning (kawn) leads inevitably to an absurdity, whether circularity or separation of cause and effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself adding unnecessary hidden 'causes' to simple realities in your own life (like why someone acted a certain way), are you falling into the same trap as the later mutakallimun? How can you train yourself to accept what is clear without overcomplicating it?
?
This lesson shows that even great scholars like Razi note when their own argument needs further investigation. Where in your faith or daily life have you been too certain of a position, refusing to acknowledge its limitations? Will you commit to intellectual honesty even when it's uncomfortable?
?
The dilemma of circularity and separation is about logical consistency. Are there beliefs you hold that depend on each other in a circular way (e.g., 'I believe it because the text says so, and I trust the text because I believe it')? Have you ever examined whether your foundation is truly sound, or do you fear that questioning it might unravel everything?

Razi's Infinite Regress Argument Against Accidents
Razi presents a third argument with objection and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's infinite regress argument against the possibility of accidents inhering in bodies and why the objection that 'being a locus' is not a real attribute fails.

Core Idea
If a body becomes a locus for an accident, then its 'locus-ness' itself requires an accident, leading to infinite regress.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument that challenges the very notion of accidents inhering in bodies by showing that the attribute of 'being a locus' would itself be an accident, leading to an impossible infinite chain. Razi then answers a natural objection by distinguishing between the essence of an accident and its inherence. The argument is a classic example of reasoning about infinite regress (tasalsul) in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow: Razi posits a body becoming a locus for an accident, then shows that the 'being a locus' becomes another accident, leading to infinite regress.
Notice the structure: argument, objection, refutation.
2
Identify the infinite regress
Trace the chain: body is not a locus → accident occurs → body becomes locus → that locus-ness is a new accident → then its own locus-ness is another accident, and so on ad infinitum.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Examine the objection
The objection claims that 'being a locus' is merely the occurrence of the accident, not a separate attribute. Understand this counterpoint.
The objection tries to stop the regress by denying the reality of locus-ness.
4
Analyze Razi's refutation
Razi argues that one can know the accident's essence while being ignorant of its inherence, proving that inherence is an additional attribute. This undermines the objection.
This distinction is key: essence vs. attribute of inherence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : أن الجسم قبل حدوث العرض المعين فيه ما كان محلا لذلك العرض، ثم إذا حصل ذلك العرض فقد صار محلّا لذلك العرض فيلزم أن يكون كونه محلا لذلك العرض عرضًا آخر ثم إنه على هذا التقدير يصير محلا لذلك العرض الآخر، بعد أنه ما كان كذلك. فيلزم أن يكون كونه محلا لتلك المحلية عرضا آخر ويلزم التسلسل. لا يقال إنه لا معنى لكونه محلا لذلك العرض، وكون ذلك العرض حالاً إلا مجرد حصول ذلك العرض فيه. لأنا نقول : ليس كون الجسم محلّا لذلك العرض، وكون ذلك العرض حالا في الجسم، هو نفس ذلك العرض ولذاته ؛ لأنه يمكن العلم بذات ذلك العرض، وذات ذلك الشك في كون ذلك العرض حالا في ذلك الجسم، وكون ذلك الجسم محلا لذلك العرض والمعلوم مغاير لما ليس بمعلوم. فعلمنا أن المحلية والحالية صفتان مغايرتان لذات ذلك العرض، ولذات ذلك الجسم.
The third point: Before the occurrence of a specific accident in it, the body was not a locus for that accident. Then when that accident occurs, it becomes a locus for that accident. So its being a locus for that accident would be another accident. Then on this assumption, it becomes a locus for that other accident after not being so. So its being a locus for that locus-ness would be another accident, and an infinite regress follows. It cannot be said that there is no meaning to its being a locus for that accident and that accident's being inherent except the mere occurrence of that accident in it. For we say: The body's being a locus for that accident and that accident's being inherent in the body is not the essence of that accident itself; because one can know the essence of that accident and be ignorant of the accident's being inherent in that body and the body's being a locus for that accident. The known is distinct from what is not known. So we know that locus-ness and inherent-ness are two attributes distinct from the essence of that accident and the essence of that body.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the possibility of accidents inhering in bodies, taken from the perspective of the Ash'ari critique. The argument runs: If a body becomes a locus for an accident, then that 'being a locus' (mahalliyya) is a new attribute that itself must inhere in the body, making it a locus for that attribute, and so on ad infinitum. The objection that 'being a locus' is nothing but the mere occurrence of the accident is refuted: one can know the essence of the accident while being ignorant of its inherence, proving that inherence is an additional attribute. This infinite regress (tasalsul) is considered impossible, thus the initial assumption that accidents inhere in bodies is problematic.

Key Terms
عرض
محل
حال
المحلية
الحالية
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'being a locus' is nothing but the mere occurrence of the accident, so it adds no new attribute.
Correction: Razi shows that one can know the accident itself while being ignorant of its inherence, proving that inherence is a distinct attribute.

Checkpoint
What does Razi's third argument lead to if a body becomes a locus for an accident?
A) The accident ceases to exist.
B) The body becomes eternal.
C) An infinite regress of accidents.
D) The accident becomes a substance.

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that the concepts of locus-ness and inherent-ness are additional attributes beyond the accident itself, leading to an unacceptable infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that you can know a thing's essence without knowing its inherence. Is there a truth you believe you fully understand, but actually you are ignorant of how it relates to your life? Will you examine that gap today?
?
If you were Razi's opponent, would you accept the infinite regress or find a way to stop it? What does your answer reveal about your willingness to follow reason even when it leads to uncomfortable conclusions?
?
The objection that 'locus-ness is just the occurrence' is tempting because it simplifies things. Where in your faith or worldview do you simplify something that actually requires deeper analysis? Will you commit to probing one such assumption this week?

Motion as Transition: Razi's Refutation of Location-Based Definition
Razi refutes the definition of motion as mere location and defines it as transition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that motion is a transitional process rather than a static state of being in a location.

Core Idea
Motion is transition, not arrival.

Razi engages with an opponent who defines motion and rest merely as being in a location, differing only accidentally. The opponent concedes that change implies a positive state but questions why motion cannot be positive. Razi responds by rejecting the reduction of motion to location-occupation. He argues that motion is the transition from one location to another, which is prior to and distinct from arriving at the second location.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's position
The opponent concedes that 'it was not then it was' implies a positive state, and asks why motion cannot be positive and rest its absence. Then they define motion and rest both as 'being in a location', differing only accidentally.
Notice the opponent tries to reduce motion to a static state.
2
Follow Razi's proof that motion ends upon arrival
Razi rejects the definition. He argues that if motion were simply being in the second location, then once that location is reached, motion would cease. But the end of a thing is not the thing itself.
This is a key logical move: the endpoint ≠ the process.
3
Grasp the definition of motion as transition
Razi defines motion as 'being in transition from the first location to the second' (intiqal). This transition precedes arrival and is the essence of motion.
Motion is a process, not a state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا : أن صدق قولنا لم يكن ثم كان يقتضي كون إحدى الحالتين أمرًا ثبوتيا . فلم لا يجوز أن تكون الحركة أمرًا ثبوتيًا، وأن يكون السكون عبارة عن عدم الحركة عما شأنه أن يتحرك ؟ قوله : الحركة والكون كل واحد منهما عبارة عن الحصول في الحيز، ولا تفاوت بينهما إلا في وصف عرضي. قلنا : لا نسلم أن الحركة عبارة عن الحصول في الحيز الثاني. والدليل عليه أنه متى حصل في الحيز الثاني فقد انقطعت الحركة وانتهت ونهاية الشيء لا تكون نفس ذلك الشيء، بل الحركة عبارة عن كونه مستقلا عن الحيز الأول إلى الحيز الثاني. وذلك الانتقال أمر متقدم على الحصول في الحيز الثاني.
We concede that the truth of our statement 'it was not then it was' requires that one of the two states be a positive thing. So why is it not possible that motion be a positive thing, and that rest be the absence of motion from that which is apt to move? He said: Motion and rest are both expressions for being in a location, and there is no difference between them except in an accidental attribute. We said: We do not accept that motion is being in the second location. The proof is that when it has arrived at the second location, motion has stopped and ended, and the end of a thing is not that thing itself. Rather, motion is its being in transition from the first location to the second location. That transition is prior to arriving at the second location.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection about motion and rest. The opponent concedes that the phrase 'it was not then it was' implies a positive state, then asks why motion cannot be positive and rest merely its absence. The opponent then defines both motion and rest as 'being in a location' (husul fi al-hayyiz), differing only in an accidental quality. Razi rejects this definition. He argues that motion cannot be reducible to being in the second location because once the moving object reaches that location, motion has ceased; the end of motion is not motion itself. Rather, motion is the transition (intiqal) from the first location to the second, which precedes arrival. This clarifies that motion is a process, not a state.

Key Terms
حركة
سكون
حيز
انتقال
ثبوتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is simply a succession of static locations (being at point A then at point B).
Correction: Razi shows that motion is not the state of being at a location but the transition between locations, which is a continuous process distinct from the endpoints.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is motion?
A) Being in the second location.
B) The absence of rest.
C) The transition from the first location to the second.
D) An accidental attribute of location.

Key Takeaway
Motion is the transition between locations, not the state of being in a location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often measure your life by the moments you 'arrive'—a degree, a job, a milestone. But Razi says motion is the transition, not the arrival. How much of your life do you actually spend in transition? Are you neglecting the process because you're fixated on the endpoint? What would change if you truly believed that the transition itself is the real motion—the positive, substantial thing?
?
The opponent thought motion and rest were just accidental variations of location. Are you sometimes like that? Do you reduce your spiritual acts—prayer, charity, seeking knowledge—to mere 'being in a place' (performing the action) without recognizing the transition happening inside you? What is that transition, and what does it mean for you to be in it right now?
?
Razi's proof shows that the end of a thing is not the thing itself. When you look at your death, do you think of it as the end of your life, or do you realize that your life is the transition, and death is just the arrival? How does that reshape your daily choices? Will you live today as transition or as waiting for arrival?

The Possibility of Necessarily Stationary Bodies
Razi objects that necessary rest in bodies could allow eternal bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi challenges the proof that all bodies are created by suggesting that some bodies might be necessarily at rest in specific places.

Core Idea
A body that cannot move could be eternal, undermining creation proofs.

In this passage, Razi presents a critical objection to the kalam cosmological argument that all bodies are created. He concedes a premise but then questions the assumption that rest is always possible. By raising the possibility of bodies with necessary rest, he forces the opponent to strengthen their proof. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the rhythm of the argument: a concession, a question, an observation, a counter-possibility, and a conclusion.
2
Identify the concession
Razi begins by saying 'سلمنا' (we concede). What principle does he accept? This sets the stage for his objection.
He concedes that an eternal positive does not cease.
3
Understand the question and response
The opponent asks why every rest must be possible. Razi rejects this by pointing to observed motion. But then he pushes further with a counter-possibility.
The observation of motion does not prove that rest is always possible; some bodies might be necessarily stationary.
4
Grasp the counter-possibility
Razi asks: why can't some bodies be necessarily tied to specific places? If this is possible, such bodies could be eternal, defeating the proof that all bodies are created.
This is the core of the objection.
5
Connect to the larger argument
Reflect on how this objection challenges the kalam proof. The opponent must refute this possibility to maintain their conclusion.
This shows the dialectical nature of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا : أن الثابت الأزلي لا يزول. فلم قلتم بأن كل سكون فإنه ممكن؟ وهذا باطل. لأنا نشاهد بعض الأجسام متحركة. ولكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون بعض الأجسام مختصا بأحياز معينة، على سبيل الوجوب، بحيث يمتنع خروجها عن تلك الأحياز؟ فإنكم ما لم تبطلوا هذا الاحتمال لا يتم لكم إثبات أن كل جسم محدث.
We concede that the eternal positive does not cease. So why do you say that every rest is possible? This is false, for we observe some bodies moving. But why may it not be that some bodies are specific to certain places by way of necessity, such that their departure from those places is impossible? For unless you refute this possibility, you cannot prove that every body is created.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and response regarding the argument that bodies are created. The opponent concedes the principle that an eternal positive does not cease, then asks why rest must always be possible (i.e., why every body can potentially move). The author (Razi) responds that this is false because we observe motion, but then raises a counter: what if some bodies are necessarily tied to specific places, unable to leave? This possibility would undermine the proof that all bodies are created (since a body always in one place could be eternal). The opponent must first refute this possibility to uphold their argument.

Key Terms
سكون
وجوب
حيز
محدث
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that all bodies are capable of motion or change, but Razi suggests some may be necessarily stationary.
Correction: The passage shows that the possibility of necessary rest must be addressed; it cannot be dismissed simply because we observe motion.

Checkpoint
What possibility does Razi raise to challenge the claim that all bodies are created?
A) All bodies are moving.
B) Some bodies could be necessarily at rest in specific places.
C) Bodies are composed of atoms.
D) Motion is an eternal attribute.

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that unless the possibility of necessarily stationary bodies is refuted, the argument that all bodies are created remains incomplete.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief so firmly that you assumed it was universal, only to realize later that a counter-possibility existed? How did that affect your certainty? Will you now examine that belief more critically?
?
This lesson shows that even a powerful proof can be challenged by a mere possibility. What is one core conviction in your faith that you have never seriously questioned? Are you willing to honestly consider objections to it today, not to weaken your faith, but to strengthen it?
?
Razi demands that the opponent refute the possibility of necessarily stationary bodies. What possibility in your own life have you been avoiding because it would require you to change? Will you confront it today, or continue to let it remain unexamined?

The Argument from Two Sides
Razi argues that any extended body must have two sides, implying motion is possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that any extended body must have two sides equal in essence, and that this implies motion is possible.

Core Idea
If a body has two identical sides, motion from one to the other is possible.

This lesson unpacks a concise but powerful argument from Imam al-Razi. At first glance, the idea that every extended thing must have two sides seems obvious, but Razi uses it to build a rational proof for the possibility of motion. By the end, you'll see how this argument fits into larger kalām debates about the nature of bodies and temporal origination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of Razi's argument.
2
Identify the two premises
The passage presents two premises: (a) every extended thing has two sides equal in complete essence, and (b) if two things are equal in essence, whatever is valid for one is valid for the other. Find each one and note how they are stated.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that motion is valid for that extended thing. Ask yourself: why does this follow? The commentary will help you connect the premises.
Think about what 'valid' (صحة) means in this context.
4
Reflect on the significance
This argument shows that motion is not inherently impossible for bodies. Consider why Razi might need to prove this — what opposing view is he countering?
This connects to the broader topic of the temporal origination of bodies.
5
Test your understanding
Complete the checkpoint quiz to check if you have grasped the key conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : كل متحيز فإنه لا بد وأن يفترض فيه جانبان متساويان في تمام الماهية. وكل شيئين متساويين في تمام الماهية فإنه يصح على كل واحد منهما ما يصح على الآخر، وذلك يقتضي صحة الحركة على ذلك المتحيز.
He said: Every extended thing (mutahayyiz) necessarily has two sides that are equal in complete essence. And for any two things equal in complete essence, whatever is valid for one is valid for the other. That entails the validity of motion for that extended thing.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents an argument for the possibility of motion in bodies. The opponent (or the argument itself) states that any extended being (mutahayyiz) must have at least two sides (janiban) that are equal in essence. If two things are equal in essence, then what is possible for one is possible for the other. Therefore, if one side can be the location of the body, the other side can also be the location, implying that the body can move from one side to the other. This proves that motion is possible for any extended body, which is used to argue that bodies are not eternal (since if they were eternal, motion would not be possible, but here it is).

Key Terms
متحيز
جانبان
تمام الماهية
حركة
صحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sides of a body are merely conceptual partitions with no real significance.
Correction: Razi treats the sides as real aspects of the extended thing; their equivalence in essence grounds the possibility of motion.

Checkpoint
What does the argument from two sides prove about an extended thing?
A) It is composed of atoms.
B) Motion is possible for it.
C) It must be eternal.
D) It has a creator.

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument from two sides proves that motion is possible for any extended body, countering the idea that bodies are inherently static.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Razi argues that any extended thing with two identical sides can move, then consider your own spiritual state: Are you convinced that moving from a lower level of faith to a higher one is truly possible for you, or do you treat your current state as fixed and unchangeable?
?
You often face two equally valid choices in life—like pursuing a good deed or remaining idle. Do you use the excuse that both are 'equal' to avoid moving? How does Razi's reasoning that motion is valid challenge your hesitation to change?
?
This argument shows that motion is metaphysically possible for every body. What does that say about your own potential for change? Are you willing to act on that possibility today, or do you remain static out of habit?

Refuting the Premise That Every Extended Body Has Two Sides
Razi refutes a premise about extended bodies and motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the opponent's argument that every extended body must have two sides, leading to motion, by showing it implies infinite divisibility.

Core Idea
Infinite divisibility is impossible, so the premise of two sides fails.

Razi engages with a key premise in the argument for motion. The opponent claims that every extended body must have two equal sides, which would allow motion. Razi counters by pointing out the logical consequence of this premise—infinite divisibility—which contradicts the established existence of the indivisible atom.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the opponent's premise and conclusion carefully to understand the claim that every extended body has two sides and that this implies motion.
The opponent uses the principle that two equal things share the same valid judgments.
2
Identify the refutation
Notice how Razi points out that assuming two sides in every extended body leads to infinite divisibility, which is impossible.
3
Understand the atom
Recognize that the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) is a key concept here; its existence disproves infinite divisibility.
This is a foundational atomist view in kalam.
4
See the structure
This passage is an objection-and-answer format: first the opponent's argument, then Razi's rebuttal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : كل متحيز فإنه لا بد وأن يفترض فيه جانبان متساويان في تمام الماهية. وكل شيئين متساويين في تمام الماهية فإنه يصح على كل واحد منهما ما يصح على الآخر، وذلك يقتضي صحة الحركة على ذلك المتحيز. قلنا : لو افترض في كل متحيز جانبان لزم كون الجسم قابلا لانقسامات لا نهاية لها وذلك محال بالدلائل الدالة على إثبات الجوهر الفرد ولما بطل ذلك فسدت هذه المقدمة، وبالله التوفيق.
He said: Every extended thing (mutahayyiz) must have two sides equal in complete essence. And any two things equal in complete essence, whatever is valid for one is valid for the other, and that necessitates the validity of motion for that extended thing. We said: If in every extended thing two sides are assumed, it would necessitate that the body is divisible into infinite divisions, and that is impossible due to the proofs establishing the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). And since that has been invalidated, this premise is corrupted. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage quotes an opponent's argument: every extended body (mutahayyiz) must have two sides of the same nature, and since one side can move, the other can too, so motion is possible. Razi refutes by pointing out that assuming two sides in every extended thing leads to infinite divisibility, which is impossible given the existence of the atom (jawhar fard). Thus, the premise is false.

Change Implies a Positive State
Razi argues that change necessarily implies a positive state and dismisses objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why any change from one state to another necessarily involves a positive state, based on Razi's logical reasoning.

Core Idea
Every change results in a positive state, either directly or by implication.

In this passage, Razi tackles a fundamental objection to the claim that change requires a positive state. He offers a crisp two-case argument and then dismisses further objections as challenging a self-evident premise. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and shows how it defends the necessity of positive states in any change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Let Razi's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice the objection and the concise answer.
2
Identify the opponent's question
The opponent asks why change must involve a positive state. Locate this question and Razi's immediate response.
The question is in the first line.
3
Follow the two-case analysis
Razi considers two possibilities for the new state: positive or negative. Trace how each case leads to the conclusion that a positive state is involved.
Pay attention to the second case where the negative state is removed by a positive remover.
4
Understand the dismissal of objections
Razi dismisses further objections as attacks on an intuitive premise. Reflect on what this tells you about the strength of his original argument.
Think about why certain premises are accepted as self-evident in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «لِمَ قلتم بأنه لما صدق على الشيء أنه لم يكن كذا ثم صار كذا، فإنه يقتضي كون إحدى الحالتين أمرًا ثبوتياً؟». قلنا : إحدى الحالتين إن كانت ثبوتية فهو المقصود، وإن كانت عدمية فالحالة الثابتة رافعة لها ورافع العدم ثبوت فالحالة الثابتة يجب أن تكون ثبوتية. وأما النقوض التي ذكرتموها فهي نقوض على مقدمة بديهية، فلا تستحق الجواب.
He said: 'Why do you say that when it is true of a thing that it was not such-and-such and then became such-and-such, that necessitates that one of the two states is a positive matter (thubuti)?' We said: One of the two states: if it is positive, then that is the intended point; and if it is negative, then the established state is removing it, and the remover of non-existence is positive, so the established state must be positive. As for the objections you have mentioned, they are objections against an intuitive premise, so they do not deserve an answer.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage deals with a question about change. The opponent asks why change must involve a positive state. Razi answers: the new state either is positive, in which case it's proven, or it is negative, but in that case the positive state removes that negative, and the remover of non-existence is positive, so the new state is positive. He dismisses further objections as attacking a self-evident premise.

Key Terms

رافع العدم
مقدمة بديهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a negative state can be the direct result of change without involving any positive reality.
Correction: Razi shows that even if the resulting state is described as negative, the very change involves a positive remover of that non-existence, so a positive state is still implied.

Checkpoint

C) Because change always involves motion.
D) Because only positive things exist.

Key Takeaway
Every change necessarily results in a positive state, either directly or because the remover of non-existence is positive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Defining Motion as Instantaneous Attainment
Razi defines motion as attainment of second contiguity at first instant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's definition of motion in kalam as the instantaneous attainment of the second location, not a continuous process.

Core Idea
Motion is the attainment of the second location at the instant the first is lost.

Imam Razi here engages with a key theological definition: what exactly is motion? An opponent claims motion is a transitional state between locations. Razi rejects this and offers a precise alternative rooted in the atomic conception of time. This short passage demonstrates the kalam method of sharp definition and logical rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to grasp the flow of argument without analysis.
Notice the compact style; Razi is responding to an opponent.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Find the statement attributed to 'he' (the opponent) about motion. What does the opponent think motion is?
The opponent says motion is a state prior to arriving at the second space.
3
Understand Razi's response
Razi argues that when the body leaves the first location, at the first instant of that absence it must already be in another location. So motion is simply that attainment.
The key concept is 'the now' (al-aan) as the first time of non-existence of the first contiguity.
4
Reflect on the atomistic framework
Note how this definition fits with the kalam atomistic view of time as discrete instants. Motion is not a process across time but an instantaneous event.
This connects to broader theological discussions about causality and divine action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : "الحركة ليست عبارة عن حصول الجوهر في الحيز الثاني، بل عن حالة متقدمة على هذا الحصول، وهو انتقاله من الحيز الأول إلى الحيز الثاني " . قلنا : إن الحصول في الحيز الأول لما عدم بقي "الآن" الذي هو أول زمان ذلك العدم ولا بد وأن يكون الجسم قد حصل في حد آخر من حدود المسافة، فإذن لا معنى للحركة إلا حصول المماسة الثانية في "الآن" أول زمان عدم المماسة هو الأولى.
He said: 'Motion is not expressed as the occurrence of the substance in the second space, but rather as a state preceding this occurrence, namely its transit from the first space to the second space.' We said: When the occurrence in the first space ceases, there remains the 'now' which is the first time of that non-existence, and necessarily the body has occurred in another boundary of the distance. Therefore, there is no meaning to motion except the occurrence of the second contiguity in the 'now' which is the first time of the non-existence of the first contiguity.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage addresses the definition of motion. The opponent claims motion is a transitional state between locations. Razi responds: when the body leaves the first location, at the first instant of absence, it must already be in another location. Thus motion is simply the attainment of the second location at that instant, not an intermediate state.

Key Terms
الحركة
الحصول في الحيز
الآن
المماسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is a continuous process or transitional state between two locations.
Correction: Razi argues that at the first instant of leaving the first location, the body is already in another location, so motion is simply the attainment of the second location at that instant.

Checkpoint

A) As a continuous transition between spaces.
B) As the attainment of the second contiguity at the first instant of the absence of the first.
C) As the potential to move.
D) As the change of place over time.

Key Takeaway
Razi defines motion as the instantaneous attainment of the second location at the first moment of absence from the first, rejecting the view of motion as a continuous transitional state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your heart wavers between obedience and disobedience. At this very instant—this 'now'—which contiguity are you attaining? Do you see that there is no gentle slide, only a decisive shift?
?
You think repentance requires a process of gradual change. But Razi strips motion of any transition. If turning to Allah is an instantaneous attainment at the first 'now' of leaving sin, what are you waiting for? Is your delay itself a denial of the discrete nature of time?
?
The body cannot inhabit two spaces at once, nor can it drift in between. Your soul, too, cannot be halfway in submission to Allah. In this breath, are you at the first contiguity of heedlessness or at the second contiguity of awareness? Name the instant of your choice.

Why a Non-Existent Condition Cannot Affect the Cause
Razi refutes the objection that a non-existent condition could affect the cause's influence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that a condition for the cause's influence cannot be non-existent, and how this preserves the necessary causality of the self-necessary cause.

Core Idea
Non-existence cannot cause existence; therefore a non-existent condition for causal influence is impossible.

In this passage, Razi addresses an objection that tries to allow for change in an eternal effect by positing a non-existent condition. He carefully examines the logic of conditions and causality to show why such a condition is self-defeating. Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping the Islamic theological position on divine causality and the nature of eternal entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Razi's response
First, read the Arabic and translation to understand the structure: an objection proposing a non-existent condition, followed by Razi's refutation.
Notice that the objection assumes an eternal cause and an eternal effect, and suggests a non-existent condition that when removed, ends the effect.
2
Analyze Razi's dilemma
Razi presents a two-part argument: either the cause remains effective before the condition or not. If it remains effective, the condition is irrelevant. If not, then non-existence causes effectiveness, which is absurd.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Note the additional rebuttal about relations
Razi also addresses other objections by clarifying that change occurs only in relations and attributes, which are not real existents. This reinforces the immutability of eternal entities.
This part shows how he defends the principle that the eternal does not cease.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this argument is important: it upholds the logical consistency of divine causality and the distinction between necessary existence and contingent change.
Non-existence cannot be a cause of anything existential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : "لم لا يجوز أن يكون تأثير العلة الواجبة لذاتها في وجود ذلك المعلول الأزلي، كان موقوفا على شرط عدمي أزلي، فلما زال ذلك الشرط الأزلي زال ذلك المعلول الأولي ؟ قلنا : شرط التأثير لا يمكن أن يكون عدمياً ؛ لأن قبل حصول ذلك الشرط، إن بقيت العلة مؤثرة لم يكن ذلك الذي فرضناه شرطًا لذلك التأثير شرطا له، وإن لم تبق العلة مؤثرة فحينئذ تكون مؤثرية تلك العلة في ذلك المعلول معلولة بذلك العدم، فيكون العدم علة لتلك المؤثرية، فيكون العدم علة للأمر الوجودي، وهو محال، وأما النقوض التي ذكرتموها على قولنا : الأزلي لا يزول فهي غير واردة، وذلك لأن الذي وقع فيه التغير هو النسب والإضافات والنسب والإضافات لا وجود لها في الأعيان.
His statement: 'Why is it not permissible that the effect of the cause necessary by itself (al-ʿilla al-wājiba li-dhātihā) in the existence of that eternal effect (al-maʿlūl al-azalī) is dependent upon an eternal non-existent condition (sharṭ ʿadamī azalī), so when that eternal condition ceases, that primal effect ceases?' We said: The condition (sharṭ) of influence cannot be non-existent, because before that condition occurs, if the cause remains effective, then that which we assumed as a condition for that influence is not a condition for it; and if the cause is no longer effective, then the effectiveness of that cause for that effect becomes caused by that non-existence, so non-existence becomes a cause for that effectiveness, thus non-existence becomes a cause for an existent thing, and that is impossible. As for the objections you raised against our statement 'the eternal does not cease', they are not valid, because that in which change occurs is relations (nisab) and additions (iḍāfāt), and relations and additions have no existence in external things.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage handles an objection concerning the effect of a self-necessary cause. The objector suggests that the eternal effect might depend on an eternal non-existent condition, which when removed causes the effect to cease. Razi refutes this by analyzing the logic of conditions: a non-existent condition cannot be a genuine condition because if the cause were still effective before the condition, the condition is irrelevant; if the cause were not effective, then the non-existence would be causing the cause's effectiveness, which is absurd as non-existence cannot bring about existence. Additionally, he addresses related objections about the eternity of entities, explaining that changes occur only in relations and attributes, which are not real existents in external things.

Key Terms
علة واجبة لذاتها
معلول أزلي
شرط عدمي
مؤثرية
نسب وإضافات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A non-existent condition can legitimately influence the effectiveness of an eternal cause.
Correction: Non-existence cannot be a cause of existence, as Razi shows through logical analysis.

Checkpoint
What is the main problem with a non-existent condition for the cause's influence, according to Razi?
A) It contradicts the eternity of the cause
B) It would make non-existence a cause of existence
C) It is impossible for a condition to be non-existent
D) It would make the effect non-eternal

Key Takeaway
A condition for causal influence must be an existent; a non-existent condition leads to the absurdity of non-existence causing existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation where you believed that a certain condition which doesn't exist yet (like perfect circumstances, a specific feeling, or someone else's approval) was necessary for something good to happen. How does Razi's argument challenge that belief? What would it take for you to let go of that non-existent condition and act now, trusting in God's existent power?

In your prayer life, do you ever feel that God's response depends on you reaching a certain state of sincerity or knowledge that you haven't yet attained? How does Razi's refutation of non-existent conditions free you from that burden, and how can you pray with greater confidence in God's direct action?

Razi proves that non-existence cannot cause existence. Where have you been passively waiting for an absence—of hardship, of a bad habit, of a certain person—to change your situation? What is one concrete step you can take today to rely on God's existent help rather than on the removal of something non-existent?

Refutation of an Eternal Effect Depending on a Non-Existent Condition
Razi refutes the objection that an eternal effect may depend on a non-existent condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Razi, an eternal effect cannot depend on a non-existent condition without leading to the impossible consequence that non-existence causes existence.

Core Idea
Non-existence cannot be a cause for existence.

This lesson examines a subtle objection concerning eternal effects and conditions. The objector suggests that an eternal effect might depend on a non-existent condition. Razi refutes this by showing that it would entail non-existence causing an existent thing, which is impossible. We'll work through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Consider the scenario where an eternal effect is dependent on an eternal non-existent condition, and when that condition ceases, the effect ceases.
The objector is trying to find a loophole in causal necessity.
2
Grasp Razi's key insight
Razi asserts that the condition for effectiveness cannot be non-existent. He then presents a dilemma.
The dilemma examines two possibilities before the condition occurs.
3
Recognize the absurd consequence
If the cause remains effective without the condition, the condition is irrelevant; if the cause becomes ineffective, then non-existence would cause effectiveness, which is absurd.
Non-existence causing existence is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأزلي، كان موقوفا على شرط عدمي أزلي، فلما زال ذلك الشرط الأزلي زال ذلك المعلول الأولي ؟ قلنا : شرط التأثير لا يمكن أن يكون عدمياً؛ لأن قبل حصول ذلك الشرط، إن بقيت العلة مؤثرة لم يكن ذلك الذي فرضناه شرطًا لذلك التأثير شرطا له، وإن لم تبق العلة مؤثرة فحينئذ تكون مؤثرية تلك العلة في ذلك المعلول معلولة بذلك العدم، فيكون العدم علة لتلك المؤثرية، فيكون العدم علة للأمر الوجودي، وهو محال.
The eternal was dependent on an eternal non-existent condition; when that eternal condition ceased, that first effect ceased? We said: The condition of effect cannot be non-existent; because before the occurrence of that condition, if the cause remained effective, then that which we assumed as a condition for that effect would not be a condition for it; and if the cause did not remain effective, then the effectiveness of that cause for that effect would be caused by that non-existence, so non-existence would be a cause for that effectiveness, thus non-existence would be a cause for an existent thing, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector presents a scenario: suppose an eternal effect is dependent on an eternal non-existent condition, such that when that non-existent condition ceases (i.e., when the condition comes into existence), the effect ceases. The response points out a fundamental impossibility: a condition for effectiveness cannot be non-existent. Razi argues: before the condition occurs, either the cause remains effective or not. If the cause remains effective, then the supposed condition is not actually a condition. If the cause ceases to be effective, then its effectiveness would be caused by the non-existence, meaning non-existence (a non-entity) becomes a cause for an existent thing (the effectiveness), which is absurd. Thus, the scenario is impossible.

Key Terms
شرط عدمي
علة
معلول
مؤثرية
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A non-existent condition can serve as a condition for an eternal effect without absurdity.
Correction: Razi shows that if a non-existent condition is allowed, it leads to either the condition being irrelevant or non-existence causing existence, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason Razi rejects the idea that an eternal effect can depend on a non-existent condition?
A) Because non-existence cannot be a condition for existence as it would make non-existence a cause for an existent.
B) Because eternal things never cease.
C) Because conditions must be eternal.
D) Because the effect would then be contingent.

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that positing a non-existent condition for an eternal effect leads to the impossible consequence that non-existence causes an existent thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage proves that non-existence cannot be a cause for existence. In your life, are you putting your hope in something that does not yet exist? How does this truth challenge you to rely only on what is actually present and effective?
?

?
If non-existence cannot cause anything, then every real cause comes from the One who truly exists. Does this fact make you turn to Allah with more sincerity, or do you still find yourself depending on imaginary circumstances? What is one step you can take today to abandon false dependencies?

Refutation of Counterexamples to 'The Eternal Does Not Cease'
Razi argues that apparent changes in eternal things are only in relations, not in essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why apparent changes in eternal things do not violate the principle that the eternal does not cease, because the change is only in relations, not in the essence.

Core Idea
Change in relations does not change the eternal essence.

Opponents of the Ash'ari principle that the eternal does not cease sometimes raise objections based on observed changes in eternal entities. Razi responds by distinguishing between the eternal essence and its accidental relations. This passage presents his concise refutation, showing that only relational changes occur, which have no external reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a very direct and concise style here.
2
Identify the principle and the counterexamples
The principle is 'al-azali la yazul' (the eternal does not cease). The counterexamples are objections that seem to show eternal things changing. Identify what the opponents argue.
3
Understand Razi's distinction
Razi argues that the change is only in relations (nisab) and additions (idafat), which have no existence in external entities (a'yan). The eternal essence remains unchanged. Ask yourself: why does this distinction save the principle?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النقوض التي ذكرتموها على قولنا : الأزلي لا يزول فهي غير واردة، وذلك لأن الذي وقع فيه التغير هو النسب والإضافات والنسب والإضافات لا وجود لها في الأعيان.
As for the counterexamples you mentioned against our statement: 'The eternal does not cease', they are not valid, because that in which change occurred is relations and additions, and relations and additions have no existence in external entities.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of counterexamples. The opponents had raised objections to the principle 'The eternal does not cease' (al-azali la yazul), citing cases where something eternal appears to change. Razi responds by distinguishing between the eternal essence and its accidental relations. He states that the change occurs only in relations (nisab) and additions (idafat), which have no real existence in external entities (a'yan). Therefore, the eternal substance itself remains unchanged, and the principle is safe.

Key Terms
الأزلي لا يزول
نقوض
نسب
إضافات
أعيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any change in an eternal thing proves that the eternal thing itself changes and thus is not eternal.
Correction: Razi clarifies that change in relations does not affect the eternal essence; only change in the essence itself would violate eternity.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say the counterexamples to 'the eternal does not cease' are invalid?
A) Because the change happens in relations which have no external existence.
B) Because eternal things are absolutely immutable.
C) Because the counterexamples are about created things.
D) Because the eternal never has any relations.

Key Takeaway
The eternal does not cease because change occurs only in relations, which have no external reality, not in the eternal essence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in an unchanging God, yet how often do you let your changing circumstances—your relationships, your moods, your successes—define your certainty in Him? What would it look like for you to anchor your faith in the eternal essence rather than in these fleeting relations?
?
Consider a relation you strongly identify with—like your job, your family role, or your reputation. If that relation changed drastically, would you feel like you lost something eternal? How does Razi's argument challenge you to distinguish between what is eternal and what is merely relational?
?
When your connection with Allah feels distant or different, do you assume that He has changed? Or do you remember that only your relation (your state) has changed, while His essence remains the same? What practical step can you take today to realign your focus from changing relations to the unchanging Reality?

The Geometric Proof that All Bodies Are Finite
Razi presents a geometric proof that all bodies are finite in magnitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a geometric proof used by Imam al-Razi to demonstrate that every body is finite in magnitude.

Core Idea
A geometric argument shows that infinite magnitude leads to contradiction.

This passage presents one of the foundational proofs in kalam cosmology: that all bodies must be finite in size. Razi uses a thought experiment with two lines, one longer than the other, to show that assuming an infinite line leads to an impossible situation. This proof is crucial for establishing that the universe had a beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own flow come through.
Note the use of 'خط' for line and 'تطبيق' for correspondence.
2
Understand the setup of the two lines
Razi describes two lines: line (j) with one finite side and one infinite side, and line (d a) similarly structured, but line (j a) is shorter by one span. Visualize this geometric arrangement.
The shorter line is represented as having a finite end and an infinite extension, but its length is less than the longer line.
3
Follow the argument about correspondence
Razi considers a one-to-one correspondence between the parts of the two lines from the finite side. If the number of parts were equal, a contradiction arises. If the shorter line has fewer parts, the deficiency must come from the infinite side, forcing the shorter line to terminate and thus the longer line to also be finite.
The key insight: the finite sides are matched, so any mismatch must occur on the infinite side, which is impossible for an actual infinite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة
فنقول : خط (ج) (۱) من جانب (ج) متناه، ومن جانب (۱) غير متناه . وخط د ا أيضا كذلك، ولكن خط (ج) (ا) أقل من خط (د (ا) بمقدار خط (جد) وليكن ذلك المقدار شبرا واحدا.
وإذا تلخص هذا فنقول : عدد الأشياء الموجودة في الخط الناقص، وإن كانت مساوية لعدد الأشياء الموجودة في الخط الزائد كان الشيء مع غيره كهو لا مع غيره، وهذا محال، وإن كانت أنقص منه فذلك التفاوت إما أن يظهر من الجانب المتناهي أو من الجانب الغير المتناهي، والأول محال؛ لأنا فرضنا التطبيق بحسب مراتب الأعداد في هذا الجانب المتناهي، فوجب أن يظهر التفاوت من الجانب الآخر. ومعنى فرض التطبيق بحسب مراتب الأعداد أن الشبر الأول هذه الجملة من مقابل بالشبر الأول من تلك الجملة الثانية والشبر الثاني من هذه الجملة مقابل بالشبر الثاني من تلك الجملة ولتكن هذه الدقيقة معلومة حتى يتم البرهان، فإذا حصل التطابق من هذا الجانب وجب أن لا يظهر التفاضل إلا أن لا يظهر التفاضل إلا من الجانب الآخر، وحينئذ تنقطع الجملة الناقصة من ذلك الجانب والزائد زاد عليه بشبر واحد، فتكون الجملة الزائدة أيضا متناهية، وهو المقصود.
قثبت بهذين البرهانين أن كل جسم فهو متناء في المقدار.
The third introduction: We say: Line (j) has one side finite and one side infinite. Line (d a) is also like that, but line (j a) is less than line (d a) by the amount of line (j d), and let that amount be one span. When this is summarized, we say: The number of things existing in the deficient line—if it equals the number of things existing in the excess line, then a thing with its other would be like it without its other, which is impossible. And if it is less than it, then that disparity must appear either from the finite side or from the infinite side. The first is impossible because we have assumed correspondence according to the orders of numbers on this finite side, so the disparity must appear from the other side. The meaning of assuming correspondence according to the orders of numbers is that the first span of this set corresponds to the first span of that second set, and the second span of this set corresponds to the second span of that set. Let this subtle point be known so that the proof is complete. When correspondence is achieved on this side, the difference must not appear except from the other side. Then the deficient set terminates on that side, and the excess is one span more, so the excess set is also finite. That is the intended conclusion. So it is established by these two proofs that every body is finite in magnitude.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents a geometric proof that every body is finite in magnitude. The author, Imam al-Razi, begins by describing two lines, one (j) with one finite and one infinite side, and another line (d a) similarly structured, but line (j a) is shorter by a specific amount. He then argues that if we suppose a one-to-one correspondence between the parts of the two lines, then if the number of parts were equal, a contradiction follows. If the smaller line has fewer parts, the deficiency must come from the infinite side because the finite sides are matched. Consequently, the smaller line terminates, implying both lines are finite. Thus, all bodies are finite. This proof is foundational for establishing the temporality (huduth) of bodies.

Key Terms
جسم
متناهي
غير متناهي
تطبيق
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodies can be infinite in magnitude
Correction: Razi proves that assuming an infinite body leads to a contradiction via geometric correspondence, so every body must be finite.

Checkpoint
What do the two proofs in the third introduction conclude?
A) That some bodies are infinite
B) That every body is finite in magnitude
C) That lines can be infinite
D) That magnitudes are relative

Key Takeaway
Every body must be finite in magnitude, as shown by geometric proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If every body is finite, then everything you rely on in this world—your strength, your wealth, your relationships—has a limit. Does your heart truly rest on the only Being who has no limit? What would it take for you to transfer your ultimate trust from the finite to the Infinite today?

The proof shows that any finite magnitude cannot be infinite. Your lifespan is finite. Are you living each day as if it is a precious, limited gift from Allah? What one change can you make this week to ensure you are using your finite time for what truly matters?

Razi's proof is a reminder that everything in creation is bounded. What in your life are you treating as if it is infinite—your time, your health, your youth? How can you realign your priorities today to acknowledge that only Allah is without limit and to prepare for the meeting with Him?

The Second Premise: Finitude Implies Origination
Razi proves that finitude in magnitude implies temporality through the need for a specifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that anything finite in magnitude must be temporally originated because it requires an external specifier.

Core Idea
Finite things need a selector; that's why they are temporal.

Razi's second premise is a cornerstone of his proof for the existence of a creator. He argues that if something has a finite size, one can imagine it being slightly larger or smaller. Since these three possibilities are equally conceivable, the actual size must have been chosen by a specifier. This shows the thing's dependence on a cause, proving it is not eternal but temporal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
2
Identify the three magnitudes
Razi mentions the actual magnitude, a slightly larger, and a slightly smaller. Note that they are all equally possible in reason.
3
Understand the need for a specifier
Because all three are equally possible, one cannot be actual without a cause that specifies it. This specifier is the creator.
4
Connect to temporality
The requirement of a cause means the thing is not self-sufficient or eternal; it is temporally originated (hadith).
5
Reflect on the misconception
Consider that this argument applies not only to physical objects but to any finite magnitude, including durations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثانية وهي في بيان أن كل ما كان متناهيا في المقدار فهو محدث . والدليل عليه أن كل ما كان متناهيا في المقدار فإنه لا يمتنع في العقل فرض وجوده أزيد من ذلك المقدار بذرة أو أنقص منه بذرة، والعلم بجواز ذلك ضروري، وإذا استوت المقادير الثلاثة أعني حصول ذلك المقدار وحصول ذلك الأزيد منه وحصول الأنقص منه استحال رجحان ذلك القدر على ما أزيد منه أو أنقص منه، إلا بتخصيص مخصص، وإيجاد قادر، وإلا فقد ترجح أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر من غير مرجح، وهو محال.
As for the second premise, which is the explanation that everything that is finite in magnitude is temporal (hadith). Its proof: Every finite thing in magnitude is such that the intellect does not prevent supposing its existence larger than that magnitude by a small amount or smaller than it by a small amount, and knowledge of the permissibility of that is necessary. When the three magnitudes are equal—namely, the attainment of that magnitude, the attainment of a larger one, and the attainment of a smaller one—it is impossible to prefer that particular magnitude over what is larger or smaller than it except by a specification from a specifier and the bringing into existence of a powerful agent. Otherwise, one of the two sides of a possible thing would be preferred over the other without a preponderator, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage defines the second premise: every finite thing in magnitude is temporal (hadith). The proof proceeds by noting that for any finite magnitude, one can conceive it as larger or smaller by a negligible amount. The three possibilities—actual, larger, smaller—are equally possible. For one to be actualized over the others, a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) and a powerful agent must choose. Without such, there would be a preference without a preponderator, which is impossible. Hence, the finite thing is contingent and requires a creator, implying it is temporally originated.

Key Terms
متناهٍ
مقدار
محدث
مخصص
مرجح
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only applies to physical objects, not to abstract or temporal magnitudes.
Correction: The argument applies to any finite magnitude, including durations of time, as the same reasoning of equal possibilities and need for a specifier holds.

Checkpoint
What is the key step in proving that a finite thing is temporal?
A) It is impossible for it to be larger
B) The three magnitudes are equal and need a specifier
C) It is eternal by nature
D) It is necessarily infinite

Key Takeaway
Anything finite in size could have been different, so its actual size must have been chosen by a cause, proving it is not eternal but temporally originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the finite magnitude of your own lifespan. If it could have been longer or shorter by a 'small amount', what does the need for a specifier imply about the One who chose its exact duration? Are you living with the awe that your every moment is a deliberate act of creation?
?
The passage equates finitude with temporality. Look at something finite you rely on—your health, wealth, or knowledge. Do you treat it as if it were self-sufficient? How does the fact that it required a specifier to bring it about change your sense of dependence on the Creator?
?
If preference without a preponderator is impossible, then the fact that you exist in this specific circumstance—rather than any other—obliges you to acknowledge a Chooser. Have you ever truly confronted the weight of that choice? What would it mean to live every day as a chosen creature rather than an accidental one?

Third Proof: Eternal Body Cannot Move
Razi presents a conditional argument showing eternality implies impossibility of motion, which is false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that an eternal body cannot move, and how this proves bodies are temporal.

Core Idea
If eternal, a body could never move, but motion is real, so bodies are not eternal.

Razi offers a sharp logical argument: if a body were eternal, it would be stuck in one place forever, making motion impossible. But we see motion all around us, and even for unseen bodies motion is possible. Therefore, no body can be eternal. This lesson unpacks that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the full proof
Read the Arabic passage once with its translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...'.
The proof has three parts: the conditional chain, the defense of the first premise, and the conclusion.
2
Identify the three premises
Break down the argument: (1) If eternal, body is in a specific place from eternity. (2) If in a specific place eternally, it cannot leave. (3) Therefore, motion is impossible. Then motion is not impossible, so the eternal assumption fails.
3
Examine the first premise
Razi says the first premise is self-evident: any physical thing (mutahayyiz) must be in a specific location. Understand what 'mutahayyiz' means.
This connects to the definition of body from earlier lessons.
4
Understand the eternal principle
The proof uses the earlier established principle: the eternal cannot cease. So if a body's location from eternity is eternal, its departure would be its ceasing, which is impossible.
Review the first proof if needed.
5
Apply to unseen bodies
The proof works for all bodies—observed and unseen. For unseen bodies, the possibility of motion is established by prior reasoning.
The argument is not limited to what we see.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثالث في حدوث الأجسام : أن الجسم لو كان أزليا لكان في الأزل حاصلًا في حيز معين، ولو كان في الأزل حاصلا في حيز معين لامتنع خروجه عنه ، فكان يلزم أن يمتنع على كل واحد من أفراد الأجسام كونه متحركاً، ولما كان هذا الثاني باطلا كان القول بكون الجسم أزليا باطلا.
أما المقدمة الأولى وهي قولنا : إن الجسم لو كان أزليا لكان في الأزل حاصلا في حيز معين فهي مقدمة بديهية؛ لأنا قد ذكرنا أن المراد من المتحيز ما يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية بأنه هنا أو هناك، وكل موجود هذا شأنه وصفته فإنه لا بد وأن يكون مختصا بجهة معينة ومكان معين، فثبت أن الجسم لو كان أزليا لكان في الأزل حاصلا في حيز معين ولو كان كذلك لكان حصوله في ذلك الحيز المعين أزليّا وقد بينا في البرهان الأول أن الأزلي لا يزول، فكان يمتنع عقلا زوال ذلك الحصول، وإذا امتنع زواله امتنعت الحركة عليه، فثبت أن الجسم لو كان أزليا لكانت الحركة ممتنعة عليه، لكن الحركة غير ممتنعة عليه، أما في الأجسام المشاهدة فالحس دال على جواز الحركة عليها، وأما في الأجسام الغائبة فالبرهان عليه هو الذي ذكرناه في البرهان الأول، فظهر أن الجسم يمتنع أن يكون أزليا.
The third proof on the temporality of bodies: If a body were eternal, it would in pre-eternity be situated in a specific place. And if it were in pre-eternity situated in a specific place, its departure from it would be impossible. Consequently, it would be impossible for any individual body to be in motion. Since this second [consequence] is false, the statement that a body is eternal is false. As for the first premise, which is our saying 'If a body were eternal, it would in pre-eternity be situated in a specific place,' it is a self-evident premise. For we have stated that what is meant by 'occupying place' (al-mutahayyiz) is that which can be pointed to by sensory indication as being 'here' or 'there'. Every existent that has this characteristic and attribute must be particularized to a specific direction and a specific place. So it is established that if a body were eternal, it would in pre-eternity be situated in a specific place. And if it were so, its being in that specific place would be eternal. And we have shown in the first proof that the eternal does not cease to exist. Therefore, its cessation is intellectually impossible, and if its cessation is impossible, motion upon it is impossible. So it is established that if a body were eternal, motion would be impossible for it. But motion is not impossible for it. As for observed bodies, sense perception indicates the permissibility of motion upon them. As for unseen bodies, the proof for it is that which we mentioned in the first proof. Hence it is clear that a body cannot be eternal.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the temporality of bodies. It begins with a conditional: if a body were eternal, it would have been in a specific place from eternity. Since it would be eternally in that place, it could never leave it, rendering motion impossible. But motion is observed in sensible bodies, and for unseen bodies it is proven possible by earlier reasoning. Therefore, the assumption of eternality is false, and bodies are temporal. The argument relies on the self-evident fact that any physical thing occupies a specific location, and on the principle that the eternal cannot cease.

Key Terms
أزلي
حيز
حركة
متحيز
بديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the proof only works for bodies that are actually observed moving, but Razi covers unseen bodies via reasoning.
Correction: The proof applies to all bodies, not just those observed. The first proof establishes that motion is possible for all bodies in principle.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Razi uses the impossibility of motion for an eternal body to prove that all bodies are temporal, as motion is undeniably possible.
