Need for an Effecter Does Not Imply Origination
Response to the claim that need for effecter implies origination, denying the second premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the need of a contingent thing for an effecter does not necessarily imply that it is originated, and how the response uses the example of the caused thing (ma'lul) remaining in need of its cause during endurance.

Core Idea
Need for a cause does not require temporal origination; it can persist during existence.

Imam Razi responds to the claim that every possible thing needing an effecter must be originated. He denies the second premise of the opponent's argument: that every need for an effecter implies origination. Through a key counterexample—the caused thing remaining in need of its cause during its endurance—he shows that need can coexist with permanence.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim and response
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the structure of the argument and counterargument.
2
Identify the two premises of the opponent
The opponent argues: (1) every possible needs an effecter, (2) every need for effecter implies origination. Notice which premise is challenged.
3
Examine the counterexample of the caused thing
The response uses the ma'lul (caused thing) which remains in need of its cause during its continued existence (baqa'), not just at origination. This shows that need does not imply temporality.
4
Consider the further discussion on endurance
The passage also argues that endurance (baqa') is not an additional attribute but the very essence of the thing, preventing an infinite regress. This reinforces the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أن كل سوى الواحد ممكن لذاته، فلم قلتم : إن كل ممكن لذاته محدث ؟ ما : قوله : لأن كل ما كان ممكنا لذاته فإنه محتاج إلى المؤثر، وكل محتاج إلى المؤثر فهو محدث قلنا : أما المقدمة الأولى وهي أن كل ما كان ممكنا لذاته فإنه محتاج إلى المؤثر فسيجيء البحث عليها في مسألة إثبات الصانع إن شاء الله تعالى. وأما المقدمة الثانية وهي قوله : كل ما كان محتاجًا إلى المؤثر فهو "محدث" فهذا ممنوع" وقوله : "حاجة الأثر إلى المؤثر إما أن تحصل حال البقاء أو حال الحدوث أو حال العدم قلنا : الكلام عليه من وجهين : الأول: أنه منقوض بالمعلول، فإنه يبقى مفتقرًا إلى العلة حال بقائه، وكذلك المشروط يبقى مفتقرًا إلى الشرط حال بقائه، وكذلك عالمية الله تعالى معللة بعلمه، مع كون كل واحد منهما أزليا. ومن النقوض اللازمة : إن أريد بقاء الشيء إما على وجوده أو على عدمه، فبقاء الشيء على وجوده يمتنع أن يكون صفة زائدة على ذاته ؛ لأنه لا معنى للبقاء إلا نفس حصوله في الزمان الثاني وحصول الشيء في الزمان الثاني لو كان زائدا عليه لكان ذلك الزائد حاصلا في ذلك الزمان، ويلزم التسلسل، وهو محال، وأما بقاء الشيء على عدمه فيمتنع أن يكون وصفًا ثبوتيًا زائدا عليه، وإلا لزم قيام الموجود بالمعدوم، فلما ثبت أن الإنسان قد يريد بقاء الشيء وثبت أن بقاء الشيء نفس ذات الشيء ثيت أن الإرادة قد تتعلق بالشيء حال بقاء الشيء.
We concede that everything other than the One is possible in itself. So why do you say that every possible in itself is originated? [The opponent] says: because everything possible in itself is in need of an effecter, and everything in need of an effecter is originated. We say: As for the first premise, that everything possible in itself is in need of an effecter, its discussion will come in the issue of proving the Maker, God willing. As for the second premise, his statement that everything in need of an effecter is 'originated', this is denied. And his statement: 'the need of the effect for the effecter either occurs at the time of endurance (baqa') or at the time of origination (huduth) or at the time of non-existence (adam)'. We say: The discussion on it is from two aspects: First: It is rebutted by the caused thing (al-ma'lul), for it remains in need of the cause during its endurance. Similarly, the conditioned thing remains in need of the condition during its endurance. Similarly, the omniscience of God is caused by His knowledge, while each of them is eternal. And from the necessary rebuttals: If by endurance of a thing is meant either upon its existence or its non-existence, then the endurance of a thing upon its existence cannot be an additional attribute to its essence, because there is no meaning of endurance except the very occurrence in the second moment, and the occurrence of a thing in the second moment if it were additional to it, then that additional would be occurring in that moment, leading to infinite regress, which is impossible. As for the endurance of a thing upon its non-existence, it cannot be a positive attribute additional to it, otherwise it would entail the existence of an existent in a non-existent. So since it is established that man may will the endurance of a thing, and it is established that the endurance of a thing is the very essence of the thing, it is established that the will may attach to a thing during its endurance.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage begins with the opponent's challenge: why do you say every possible thing is originated? The opponent's proof is then quoted: every possible needs an effecter, and everything needing an effecter is originated. The response first postpones discussion of the first premise to a later section. The focus is on the second premise, which is denied. The opponent had specified that the need of the effect for the effecter occurs either at endurance, origination, or non-existence. The response gives two objections. The first objection (al-awwal) is a rebuttal by counterexample (naqd): the caused thing (ma'lul) remains in need of its cause during its endurance, not just at origination. Similarly, the conditioned remains in need of the condition, and God's knowledge is caused by His knowledge eternally. This shows that need for an effecter does not imply temporality. The passage then discusses the nature of endurance (baqa'): if endurance means the thing's occurrence in a second moment, it is not an additional attribute. If it were, infinite regress would follow. Endurance is the essence itself. So the need for an effecter can persist during endurance without implying origination.

مؤثر

بقاء

علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if something is in need of an effecter, it must be temporal (originated).
Correction: This passage shows that the caused thing remains in need of its cause during its endurance, so need does not imply origination.

Checkpoint
How does the response refute the claim that need for an effecter implies origination?
A) By arguing that the first premise is false
B) By showing that the caused thing remains in need of the cause during endurance
C) By denying that possible things need effecters
D) By asserting that need only occurs at origination

Key Takeaway
The need of a contingent being for an effecter does not require it to be originated; the caused thing remains in need during its endurance.

?

Prior Non-Existence Is Not a Condition
First aspect of counter-argument: incompatibility of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why prior non-existence cannot be a condition for an act or agent, according to this counter-argument.

Core Idea
Incompatibility precludes conditionality.

This lesson unpacks the first of two counter-arguments against the claim that need for an effecter implies origination. It argues that prior non-existence cannot be a condition for an act or agency because it is incompatible with them. If something is incompatible, it cannot be a condition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the logical flow: non-existence is incompatible with existence, so it cannot be a condition.
The argument is concise; make sure you follow the reasoning.
2
Understand the concept of incompatibility
Identify what is incompatible: non-existence of the act is incompatible with its existence. Similarly, non-existence of agency is incompatible with agency occurring.
Incompatibility means they cannot coexist.
3
Connect to the conclusion
See how the author concludes that since prior non-existence is incompatible, it cannot be a condition. Therefore, the act and agent can exist without it, contradicting the opponent's view.
This is a counter-argument (mu'arada) aiming to refute the opponent's premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: من الكلام على ذلك الدليل : أنا نعارضه بوجوه أخر من الدلائل :، الوجه الأول: أن عدم الفعل ينافي وجوده، وعدم الفاعلية ينافي حصولها، ومنافي الشيء يمتنع أن يكون شرطا له فالعدم السابق يمتنع أن يكون شرطا لكون الفعل فعلاً، ولكون الفاعل فاعلاً فإذن كون الفعل فعلاً، وكون الفاعل فاعلا ممكن التحقق بدون العدم السابق، وذلك هو المطلوب. الوجه الثاني : أن الأثر حال بقائه ممكن الوجود فلا بد له من مؤثر . فالشيء حال بقائه مفتقر إلى المؤثر، إنما قلنا إن الأثر حال بقائه ممكن الوجود لأن المراد من الإمكان كون تلك الماهية في نفسها قابلة للعدم ،والوجود وكونها في نفسها غير متأبية من الأمرين فتلك الماهية من حيث هي هي إن كانت متابية من قبول العدم وجب أن تكون كذلك أبداً، وإن كانت هويتها من حيث هي هي، لا تتأبى عن قبول العدم كانت هذه الحالة حاصلة أبداً، فكانت ممكنة أبدا وأما أن الممكن لا بد مؤثر فهو ظاهر. من له وإذا ظهرت المقدمتان ثبت أن الممكن حال بقائه مفتقرًا إلى المؤثر، وذلك
The second aspect: The discussion on that proof: We counter it with other proofs: The first aspect: The non-existence of the act is incompatible with its existence, and non-existence of agency is incompatible with its occurrence. That which is incompatible with a thing cannot be a condition for it. So the prior non-existence cannot be a condition for the act being an act, nor for the agent being an agent. Therefore, the act being an act and the agent being an agent are possible to realize without the prior non-existence, and that is the desired conclusion. The second aspect: The effect during its endurance is possible in existence, so it must have an effecter. So the thing during its endurance is in need of the effecter. We say the effect during its endurance is possible in existence because the meaning of possibility is that the essence in itself is receptive to non-existence and existence, and is not averse to either. So that essence, considered in itself, if it were averse to accepting non-existence, it would necessarily be so always; and if its identity in itself is not averse to accepting non-existence, then this state would be always present, so it would be always possible. And that the possible must have an effecter is obvious. So when these two premises are established, it is established that the possible during its endurance is in need of the effecter.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage contains the second objection (al-wajh al-thani) against the opponent's argument that need for an effecter implies origination. It is a counter-argument (mu'arada) with two aspects. The first aspect argues that prior non-existence cannot be a condition for an act or an agent because it is incompatible with them. Incompatibility precludes conditionality, so the act and agent are possible without prior non-existence. The second aspect argues that the effect during its endurance is possible in existence (mumkin al-wujud), and since every possible needs an effecter, the effect during endurance needs an effecter. This is proven by analyzing possibility: an essence that is not averse to either existence or non-existence remains always possible. Thus the possible always needs an effecter, not only at origination. Therefore, need for an effecter does not imply temporality.

Key Terms
عدم
فعل
فاعل
شرط
منافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prior non-existence is a necessary condition for an act to be an act.
Correction: The passage argues that non-existence is incompatible with existence, so it cannot be a condition. An act can be an act without prior non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the first aspect, why can't prior non-existence be a condition for the act?
A) Because it is a necessary cause
B) Because it is incompatible with the act's existence
C) Because it is the same as possibility
D) Because it occurs after the act

Key Takeaway
Prior non-existence cannot be a condition for an act or agency because incompatibility precludes conditionality.

Effect Needs Effecter During Endurance
Second aspect: effect endures as possible, needs effecter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an effect requires an effecter not only at origination but also during its endurance, based on possibility.

Core Idea
A possible thing always needs an effecter.

This lesson presents the second counter-argument. It argues that the effect, while it endures, remains possible in existence, and every possible needs an effecter. Therefore, the effect's need for an effecter continues as long as it exists, not just at the moment of origination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the second aspect carefully. Notice the structure: it states a premise, then proves it, then concludes.
The argument has two premises: the effect during endurance is possible, and every possible needs an effecter.
2
Understand the definition of possibility
The passage defines possibility (imkan) as the essence being receptive to both existence and non-existence, not averse to either. This state is constant.
If an essence is not averse to non-existence, it remains always possible.
3
See the implication
Since the effect during endurance is possible, and possibilities always need an effecter, the effect needs an effecter continuously, not only at origination.
This refutes the idea that need for an effecter implies temporality.

Counterexamples to the Causal Premise
Razi enumerates three counterexamples to the premise that only originated things need a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses counterexamples (al-manqud) to refute the premise that only originated things need a cause.

Core Idea
Counterexamples show that even eternal things can depend on a cause.

Razi opens his first objection by presenting three counterexamples to the opponent's premise. Each example demonstrates a case where a thing is in need of a cause or condition despite not being originated. These examples target the heart of the causal premise and force the opponent to reconsider its scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three counterexamples
Read the Arabic and translation to see the three instances: the effect (ma'lul), the conditioned (mashrut), and divine omniscience ('alamiyya).
Notice that all three are eternal yet dependent.
2
Understand why they refute the premise
The opponent claimed only originated things need a cause. These examples show non-originated things also need a cause or condition.
3
Reflect on the theological import
Razi uses divine attributes to show that even God's attributes (omniscience) are caused by His knowledge, yet both are eternal.
This defends Ash'ari theology against objections about eternal attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الأول: أنه منقوض بالمعلول، فإنه يبقى مفتقرًا إلى العلة حال بقائه، وكذلك المشروط يبقى مفتقرًا إلى الشرط حال بقائه، وكذلك عالمية الله تعالى معللة بعلمه، مع كون كل واحد منهما أزليا. ومن النقوض اللازمة : إن أريد بقاء الشيء إما على وجوده أو على عدمه، فبقاء الشيء على وجوده يمتنع أن يكون صفة زائدة على ذاته ؛ لأنه لا معنى للبقاء إلا نفس حصوله في الزمان الثاني وحصول الشيء في الزمان الثاني لو كان زائدا عليه لكان ذلك الزائد حاصلا في ذلك الزمان، ويلزم التسلسل، وهو محال، وأما بقاء الشيء على عدمه فيمتنع أن يكون وصفًا ثبوتيًا زائدا عليه، وإلا لزم قيام الموجود بالمعدوم، فلما ثبت أن الإنسان قد يريد بقاء الشيء وثبت أن بقاء الشيء نفس ذات الشيء ثبت أن الإرادة قد تتعلق بالشيء حال بقاء الشيء.
The first aspect: that it is refuted by the effect, for it remains in need of the cause (al-illa) during its state of remaining (baqa'), and likewise the conditioned (al-mashrut) remains in need of the condition (al-shart) during its state of remaining, and likewise the omniscience (al-'alamiyya) of God Most High is caused by His knowledge, while each of them is eternal. And among the necessary refutations: if the remaining of a thing is intended either as to its existence or its non-existence, then the remaining of a thing in its existence is impossible to be an attribute additional to its essence; because the meaning of remaining is nothing but its very occurrence in the second time, and the occurrence of the thing in the second time, if it were additional to it, then that additional would be occurring in that time, and infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow, which is impossible. As for the remaining of a thing in its non-existence, it is impossible for it to be a positive attribute additional to it, otherwise it would necessitate the existence of a being in a non-existent. Thus, since it has been established that a human may will the remaining of a thing, and that the remaining of a thing is identical to the thing's essence, it is established that the will (al-irada) may relate to the thing during the state of the thing's remaining.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is the first of two major refutations (al-wajh al-awwal) of the opponent's premise that only originated things (muhdath) need a cause (mu'aththir). Razi begins by stating that the premise is refuted (manqud) by counterexample. He gives three examples: (1) an effect remains dependent on its cause during its continued existence (baqa'), so an effect is not originated at that time yet still requires a cause; (2) a conditioned thing (mashrut) remains dependent on its condition (shart) during its continued existence; (3) God's attribute of omniscience (al-'alamiyya) is caused by His eternal knowledge, yet both are eternal (azali), so a non-originated thing can still be caused. Then Razi discusses a potential response about the meaning of 'remaining' (baqa'). He argues that if someone says that remaining either refers to existence or non-existence, then remaining in existence cannot be an additional attribute (sifa za'ida) because that would lead to infinite regress. Remaining in non-existence cannot be a positive attribute because that would require a being to subsist in a non-existent. Therefore, remaining is identical to the essence of the thing. Since a human can will the remaining of a thing, and remaining is the thing itself, it follows that will can relate to a thing in its state of continuing existence. This shows that the need for a cause (or in this case the relation of will) does not require origination (huduth).

Key Terms
الوجه
منقوض
المعلول
العلة
المشروط
الشرط
عالمية
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that only things that begin to exist need a cause.
Correction: Razi shows that even eternal things like an effect in its state of remaining, a conditioned thing, and God's attribute of omniscience are dependent on a cause or condition.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of Razi's counterexamples in this passage?
A) An effect remaining in need of its cause
B) A conditioned thing remaining in need of its condition
C) God's omniscience being caused by His knowledge
D) The will of a human being needing a cause

Key Takeaway
Eternal things can be causally dependent, refuting the claim that only originated things need a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Analysis of Remaining and Its Implications
Razi analyzes the concept of remaining (baqa') to refute the idea that it is an additional attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's analysis of 'remaining' (baqa') and how it shows that remaining is identical to the essence, not an additional attribute.

Core Idea
Remaining is not an attribute; it is the thing's existence in the second time.

Razi proceeds to analyze the concept of 'remaining' (baqa') to address a potential response from the opponent. He considers two ways to understand remaining—as existence in the second time or as non-existence—and argues that neither interpretation allows remaining to be an additional attribute. This leads to the conclusion that remaining is identical to the essence, which then supports his argument about the will relating to a thing during its remaining.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two interpretations of remaining
Razi divides remaining into two possibilities: remaining in existence and remaining in non-existence. He then argues against both being additional attributes.
Focus on the logical argument against additional attributes.
2
Follow the argument against remaining as an attribute
If remaining in existence were an attribute, it would require an infinite regress. If remaining in non-existence were an attribute, it would require a positive quality in a non-existent, which is absurd.
3
Connect to the conclusion about will
Since remaining is the essence itself, a human willing the remaining of a thing means the will relates to the thing itself during its continued existence, not at origination only.
This supports the idea that need for a cause can extend beyond origination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'remaining' is an additional quality that a thing acquires at the second moment of existence.
Correction: Razi proves that remaining is not an additional attribute; it is simply the thing's existence in the second time, which is identical to the thing's essence.

Checkpoint
What does Razi conclude about the relationship between remaining (baqa') and the essence of a thing?
A) Remaining is an attribute additional to the essence
B) Remaining is the existence of the thing in the second time, identical to the essence
C) Remaining is a condition that must be fulfilled for existence
D) Remaining is a state of non-existence

Key Takeaway
Remaining is identical to the essence, so a cause or will can relate to a thing during its continued existence, not only at origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi strips away the illusion that your persistence from moment to moment requires a new attribute or cause each second. When you consider your own daily existence—waking up, breathing, thinking—do you truly feel that each moment is a fresh act of God's preservation, or have you fallen into the comfortable assumption that you simply 'continue' on your own? What would change if you literally believed that your remaining is identical to your essence and completely dependent on God at every instant?
?
The passage shows that will can persist in relation to a thing beyond its first moment. Where in your life have you treated obedience as a one-time decision, forgetting that your will must continuously relate to the act of worship or repentance? What one area of your spiritual practice do you need to 're-will' today, not as a restart but as an ongoing commitment in the state of remaining?

Razi's First Objection: Non-existence Cannot Be a Condition
Razi introduces the second refutation and gives the first sub-objection against the premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the idea that preceding non-existence is a condition for an act being an act.

Core Idea
Non-existence contradicts existence, so it cannot be a condition for existence.

Razi now launches the second major refutation of his opponent's proof. He presents several objections, the first of which targets the claim that only originated things need a cause by showing that non-existence cannot serve as a condition. In this passage, he lays out a logical argument that the act and agent can exist without any prior state of non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: Razi first announces his opposition, then gives the argument.
The passage is dense but follows a clear logical chain.
2
Identify the two contradictions
Find where Razi states that non-existence of the act contradicts its existence, and non-existence of agency contradicts its occurrence. These are the key premises.
These contradictions are the foundation of the argument.
3
Understand the general rule
Razi says that what contradicts a thing cannot be a condition for that thing. Grasp this logical principle.
This rule applies universally.
4
Follow the conclusion
Since preceding non-existence contradicts the act and agency, it cannot be a condition. Therefore, the act and agent can exist without preceding non-existence.
This directly refutes the opponent's premise.
5
Reflect on the refutation
Ask yourself: Why is this argument effective against the opponent's proof? How does it undermine the need for a cause for originated things?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: من الكلام على ذلك الدليل : أنا نعارضه بوجوه أخر من الدلائل : الوجه الأول: أن عدم الفعل ينافي وجوده، وعدم الفاعلية ينافي حصولها، ومنافي الشيء يمتنع أن يكون شرطا له فالعدم السابق يمتنع أن يكون شرطا لكون الفعل فعلاً، ولكون الفاعل فاعلاً فإذن كون الفعل فعلاً، وكون الفاعل فاعلا ممكن التحقق بدون العدم السابق، وذلك هو المطلوب.
The second aspect: from the discussion on that proof: we oppose it with other aspects from the proofs: The first aspect: that the non-existence ('adam) of the act contradicts its existence, and the non-existence of agency contradicts its occurrence, and what contradicts a thing is impossible to be a condition for it; so preceding non-existence (al-'adam al-sabiq) is impossible to be a condition for the act being an act, and for the agent being an agent. Therefore, the act being an act and the agent being an agent are possibly realizable without preceding non-existence, and that is what is required.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the second major refutation (al-wajh al-thani) of the opponent's proof. Razi states that he will oppose the opponent with several other arguments. The first of these (al-wajh al-awwal min hadhihi al-wujuh) is: the non-existence of an act (adam al-fi'l) is the opposite of its existence; similarly, the non-existence of agency is the opposite of its occurrence. Something that opposes a thing cannot be a condition for that thing. Therefore, the preceding non-existence (a state of non-entity before the act) cannot be a condition for the act being an act or for the agent being an agent. Consequently, the act and the agent can be realized without any preceding non-existence. This refutes the idea that only originated things (which require a preceding non-existence) need a cause.

نعارضه (nu'ariḍuhu/we oppose it)
العدم (al-'adam/non-existence)
ينافي (yunafi/contradicts)
الشرط (al-shart/condition)
العدم السابق (al-'adam al-sabiq/preceding non-existence)
الفعل (al-fi'l/act)
الفاعل (al-fa'il/agent)
المطلوب (al-matlab/what is required)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence is a necessary prerequisite for an act to come into being.
Correction: Razi argues that non-existence contradicts existence, so it cannot be a condition; therefore the act can exist without it.

Checkpoint

A) Preceding non-existence is a necessary condition for an act to be an act.
B) An act and an agent can be realized without preceding non-existence.
C) The act and agent are impossible without non-existence.
D) Non-existence is the cause of the act coming into being.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first objection shows that preceding non-existence cannot be a condition for an act, because it contradicts the act's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If non-existence cannot be a condition for an act, then what excuses do you make for not fulfilling your obligations, waiting for an ideal circumstance that may itself be contradictory?

When you think about your own acts, do you assume that your previous failure to act is necessary for your current act? How does Razi's argument challenge that assumption?

The Effect in its State of Remaining is Possible and Requires a Cause
Razi argues that the effect in its state of remaining is possible and thus needs a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the effect, even in its state of remaining, is a possible existent and thus requires a cause, refuting the claim that causal need is limited to origination.

Core Idea
Possibility is a permanent property of the essence, not a temporary state.

Razi presents the second sub-objection against the view that the effect only needs a cause at the moment of origination. He argues that the effect in its state of remaining is still a possible existent, and possibility is intrinsic to the essence. Therefore, it always requires a cause. This challenges the notion that causal need is limited to the moment of coming into existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim being refuted
Identify the view that Razi is arguing against: that the effect needs a cause only at the moment of origination, not after.
This is the backdrop for the sub-objection.
2
Analyze Razi's definition of possibility
Read how Razi defines possibility (imkan) as the essence being equally receptive to both existence and non-existence, not averse to either.
This definition is crucial.
3
See why possibility is permanent
Razi argues that if an essence is not averse to non-existence, it remains so forever, so possibility is an unchanging property.
This counters the idea that possibility changes over time.
4
Connect to the need for a cause
Accept that the possible must have a cause (evident), combine with the permanence of possibility to conclude that the effect always needs a cause.
This directly refutes the original claim.
5
Reflect on the refutation
Understand how this argument refutes the idea that need for a cause is only at origination.
This is a key move in the larger debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : أن الأثر حال بقائه ممكن الوجود فلا بد له من مؤثر . فالشيء حال بقائه مفتقر إلى المؤثر، إنما قلنا إن الأثر حال بقائه ممكن الوجود لأن المراد من الإمكان كون تلك الماهية في نفسها قابلة للعدم والوجود وكونها في نفسها غير متأبية من الأمرين فتلك الماهية من حيث هي هي إن كانت متابية من قبول العدم وجب أن تكون كذلك أبداً، وإن كانت هويتها من حيث هي هي، لا تتأبى عن قبول العدم كانت هذه الحالة حاصلة أبداً، فكانت ممكنة أبدا وأما أن الممكن لا بد له من مؤثر فهو ظاهر. وإذا ظهرت المقدمتان ثبت أن الممكن حال بقائه مفتقرًا إلى المؤثر، وذلك يقدح في أن الافتقار إلى المؤثر لا يحصل حال الحدوث.
The second aspect: that the effect (al-athar) during its state of remaining is possible of existence (mumkin al-wujud), so it must have an efficient cause (mu'aththir). So the thing during its state of remaining is in need of the efficient cause. We only said that the effect during its state of remaining is possible of existence because what is meant by possibility (al-imkan) is that that essence (al-mahiyya) in itself is receptive to non-existence and existence, and that it in itself is not averse to either of the two matters. So that essence, considered purely in itself, if it is averse to the reception of non-existence, it would necessarily be so forever; and if its identity, considered in itself, is not averse to the reception of non-existence, this state would obtain forever, so it would be possible forever. And as for the fact that the possible must have an efficient cause, it is evident. And when the two premises become clear, it is established that the possible in its state of remaining is in need of the efficient cause, and that refutes the claim that the need for the efficient cause only occurs at the time of origination (huduth).
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This is the second sub-objection within the second major refutation. Razi argues that the effect (athar) in its state of remaining (baqa') is still a possible existent (mumkin al-wujud), and therefore it requires a cause (mu'aththir). The reasoning: possibility (imkan) is defined as the essence's being equally receptive to existence and non-existence, not being averse to either. This property of being 'possible' is intrinsic to the essence (dhat) and does not change with time. If an essence is always receptive, it remains possible eternally. Since it is evident that every possible needs a cause, the effect during its remaining is still in need of a cause. This directly refutes the claim that the need for a cause only arises at the moment of origination (huduth).

البقاء

قابلة
متأبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility only applies before the effect exists, not after it has come into being.
Correction: Razi shows that possibility is a property of the essence itself and does not change; it is permanent. Therefore, the effect remains possible in its state of remaining.

Checkpoint

A) That the thing actually exists
B) That the essence is receptive to both existence and non-existence
C) That the thing has a beginning in time
D) That the thing requires a creator only at the moment of creation

Key Takeaway
Possibility is an intrinsic property of the essence, so the effect always requires a cause, not only at origination.

Why Origination Cannot Cause Need
Razi proves that origination cannot be the cause of the effect's need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that origination (huduth) cannot be the cause of the effect's need for a cause, due to logical circularity.

Core Idea
Origination depends on existence; if it caused need, existence would need itself first.

Razi presents a tight logical refutation against those who think the temporal origination of a thing is what makes it need a cause. He shows that this leads to an impossible circularity where the effect would have to exist before its own cause. This passage demonstrates the precision of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for rhythm, then the literal translation. Focus on the definitions of huduth and masbuqiyya.
Note that Razi is defining terms precisely.
2
Trace the dependency chain
Identify each link in the chain: origination depends on existence, existence depends on the cause, the cause depends on need, need depends on its own cause/parts/conditions.
Write down the chain to visualize dependencies.
3
Identify the absurd consequence
See that if origination were the cause of need, then the thing would be delayed behind itself—an impossibility. This proves origination is irrelevant to need.
This is the key logical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : أن الحدوث عبارة عن كون وجود الشيء مسبوقا بعدمه، وهذه المسبوقية صفة من صفات الشيء، ونعت من نعوته وصفة الشيء مفتقرة الثبوت إلى تحقق الموصوف، فحدوث الشيء مفتقر إلى وجوده، ثم إن وجوده مفتقر إلى تأثير المؤثر فيه الذي هو مفتقر إلى احتياج الأثر إلى المؤثر، الذي هو مفتقر إلى علة ذلك الاحتياج، وإلى جزء تلك العلة، وإلى شرط تلك العلة، فلو كان الحدوث علة للحاجة أو جزءاً من تلك العلة أو شرطا لتلك العلة، لزم تأخر الشيء عن نفسه بمراتب، وهو محال، فعلمنا أنه لا عبرة بالحدوث في حصول احتياج الأثر إلى المؤثر.
The third aspect: that origination (huduth) is an expression of the thing's existence being preceded by its non-existence, and this precedence (al-masbuqiyya) is an attribute (sifa) of the thing and a characteristic (na't) of it, and the attribute of a thing depends for its establishment (thubut) on the realization (tahaqquq) of the described (al-mawsuf). So the origination of a thing depends on its existence. Then its existence depends on the influence (ta'thir) of the efficient cause (mu'aththir) in it, which depends on the effect's need (ihtiyaj) for the efficient cause, which depends on the cause (illa) of that need, and on a part (juz') of that cause, and on a condition (shart) of that cause. So if origination were the cause of the need, or a part of that cause, or a condition of that cause, then the thing would be delayed (ta'akhkhur) behind itself by degrees, which is impossible (muhal). Thus we know that origination has no consideration (i'tibar) in the occurrence of the effect's need for the efficient cause.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This is the third sub-objection. Razi defines origination (huduth) as existence preceded by non-existence. That precedence (masbuqiyya) is an attribute of the thing. An attribute depends on the existence of the thing it qualifies. Therefore, the origination of a thing depends on the thing's existence. But the thing's existence depends on the efficient cause's influence, which in turn depends on the effect's need for the cause. That need itself has a cause, its parts, and its conditions. If we were to say that origination (huduth) is the cause (illa) of the need, or a part or condition of that cause, then the need (and therefore the existence of the thing) would depend on origination, but origination itself depends on existence. This causes a circularity or infinite regress, specifically the thing would be 'delayed behind itself' — the effect would need to exist before its cause. This is absurd (muhal). Therefore, origination cannot be a factor in the effect's need for a cause.

Key Terms
الحدوث
مسبوقا بعدمه
المسبوقية
الصفة
الموصوف
المؤثر
الاحتياج
العلة
الجزء
الشرط
تأخر الشيء عن نفسه
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that a thing's temporal origination (huduth) is the reason it needs a cause.
Correction: Razi shows that this would lead to circularity because huduth itself depends on the thing's existence, which already presupposes the need for a cause.

Checkpoint
If huduth (origination) were the cause of the effect's need, what would be the result according to Razi?
A) The effect would be eternal
B) The thing would be delayed behind itself
C) The cause would be unnecessary
D) The need would disappear

Key Takeaway
Origination cannot be the cause or part of the cause of the effect's need because it would create an impossible circular dependency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Have you ever assumed that because something has a beginning (huduth), it automatically needs a cause—without considering that the beginning itself depends on the thing's existence? How does this lesson force you to reexamine the logical order of your beliefs about creation?

Razi argues that attributing need to origination leads to an impossible circular dependency. In your own life, do you ever rely on circular reasoning to justify your actions or beliefs, avoiding the real foundation? What is one assumption you hold that might collapse if you traced its dependencies honestly?

Fourth Sub-Objection: The Argument from Special Possibility
Razi uses special possibility to argue need extends beyond origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the concept of special possibility to argue that the need for a cause is not limited to origination but extends to continuity.

Core Idea
Special possibility shows that need for a cause applies to both existence and non-existence.

In this fourth sub-objection, Razi introduces a refined notion of possibility—special possibility—where both existence and non-existence are equally possible. He then builds a dilemma: either possible non-existence needs a cause or it does not. The implications challenge the common view that only origination requires a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the concise style typical of kalam arguments.
2
Understand 'special possibility'
Razi defines special possibility (al-mumkin al-khass) as that which can both exist and not exist. This is the foundation of the argument.
Contrast with absolute possibility if needed.
3
Follow the dilemma
The passage presents two options: either possible non-existence requires a cause (murajjih) or it does not. Examine both horns and their consequences.
The first horn leads to need during continuity; the second leads to absurdity.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Razi concludes that the first horn is necessary: possible non-existence itself needs a cause, implying that need is not restricted to origination.
This refutes the opponent's claim that only originated things need a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع وهو أن الممكن الخاص هو الذي يصدق عليه أنه يمكن وجوده، ويمكن عدمه، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : كون العدم ممكنا إما أن يكون محوجًا إلى المؤثر ولا يكون كذلك، فإن كان الأول كان العدم الممكن المستمر من الأزل إلى الأبد، محتاجًا إلى المرجح ، فثبت أن الشيء حال استمراره قد يفتقر إلى المؤثر، وإن كان الثاني فهو باطل؛ لأن ماهية الإمكان الخاص واحدة في جانبي الوجود والعدم، فإن لم يكن في جانب العدم محوجًا إلى المرجح فكذا في جانب الوجود يحوج إلى المؤثر، وحينئذ يلزم استغناء الممكن عن المؤثر، وهو محال.
The fourth aspect: that special possibility (al-mumkin al-khass) is that which it is true of that its existence is possible and its non-existence is possible. And when this is established, we say: the fact that non-existence is possible either necessitates need for the efficient cause or it does not. If the first, then the non-existence that is possible and continuous from eternity to eternity (al-azal ila al-abad) would be in need of a preponderator (murajjih), so it is established that the thing during its state of continuity (istimrar) may be in need of the efficient cause. And if the second, it is false; because the quiddity (mahiyya) of special possibility is one in both sides of existence and non-existence. So if on the side of non-existence it does not necessitate need for the preponderator, then likewise on the side of existence it would not necessitate need for the efficient cause, and then it would follow that the possible is independent of the efficient cause (istighna'), which is impossible (muhal).
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This is the fourth sub-objection. Razi defines 'special possibility' (al-mumkin al-khass) as an entity for which both existence and non-existence are equally possible. Then he presents a dilemma: the possibility of non-existence either necessitates a cause (mu'aththir or murajjih) or it does not. If it does, then the continuous non-existence (from eternity to eternity) would itself need a preponderator, which proves that something in a state of continuity (not just at origination) may need a cause. If it does not necessitate a cause, then since possibility is the same for both existence and non-existence, existence would also not need a cause, and the possible would be independent of a cause (istighna'), which is impossible (muhal). Therefore, the first horn is accepted: non-existence, if possible, does require a cause. Thus, need is not restricted to origination.

Key Terms
الممكن الخاص (al-mumkin al-khass)
يمكن وجوده (yumkin wujuduhu)
يمكن عدمه (yumkin 'adamuhu)
العدم الممكن (al-'adam al-mumkin)
المرجح (al-murajjih)
الاستمرار (al-istimrar)
الماهية (al-mahiyya)
جوانب الوجود والعدم (janibay al-wujud wa al-'adam)
الاستغناء (al-istighna')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only things that begin to exist need a cause; continuous things do not.
Correction: Razi shows that possible non-existence itself, if continuous, would need a cause, so need is not restricted to origination.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth sub-objection, what happens if possible non-existence does not need a preponderator?
A) Then existence would also not need a cause, leading to the impossible independence of the possible.
B) Then existence would need a cause, but non-existence would not.
C) Then the possible would be self-subsistent.
D) Then origination would be the only cause of need.

Key Takeaway
Need for a cause applies to possible non-existence itself, not just to origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that even your possible non-existence requires a preponderator from eternity. When you feel like you are 'just existing' without actively needing Allah, are you forgetting that your very possibility demands a cause at every instant? How can you make the awareness of your constant need for Allah a reality in your daily life?

The symmetry of possibility shows that if non-existence didn't need a cause, existence wouldn't either. Do you sometimes live as if your existence is self-sufficient, independent of Allah? The Eternal Cause and Its Eternal Effect
Razi presents a dilemma regarding the Necessary Being's eternal causal qualifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the chain of contingent beings must end in a Necessary Being, and that this Being's eternal causality does not require its effects to be originated in time.

Core Idea
An eternal cause can have an eternal effect—dependence does not require temporal origination.

In this passage, Razi presents a critical argument about the relationship between the Necessary Existence and its effects. He asks whether the Necessary Being possesses all causal qualifications from eternity or acquires them later. The answer leads to a conclusion that challenges the assumption that all effects must be temporal. Let's follow his reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Razi's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise and logical style typical of kalam arguments.
2
Identify the dilemma
Find the two horns: either the Necessary Being has all causal conditions from eternity, or it does not. Understand each possibility clearly.
3
Examine the consequences
What follows if the conditions are eternal? What if they are not? Razi explains both branches.
Pay attention to the term 'perpetuity of effects' (dawam al-athar).
4
Reflect on the impossibility of regress
Why is infinite regress impossible according to Razi? This is a key move in the argument.
Regress (tasalsul) is considered muhal (impossible) in classical kalam.
5
Connect to the learning objective
How does this dilemma show that dependence does not require temporal origination? Summarize in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : أن الممكنات لا بد من انتهائها في سلسلة الحاجة على واجب الوجود، فنقول : واجب الوجود إما أن يكون مستجمعا لجميع تلك الأمور المعتبرة في المؤثرية في الأزل أو ما كان مستجعًا لتلك الأمور في الأزل، فإن كان الأول لزم من دوامه مع دوام جميع تلك الأمور المعتبرة في المؤثرية، دوام آثاره، وعلى هذا التقدير ثبت أن استناد الأثر إلى المؤثر لا يتوقف على كون الأثر حادثاً، وأما إن قلنا بأنه تعالى ما كان مستجمعًا في الأزل لجميع الأمور المعتبرة في المؤثرية، فحينئذ يكون حصول تلك الأمور المعتبرة في المؤثرية حادثاً، ثم الكلام في كيفية حدوثها كما في الأول، ويلزم التسلسل، وهو محال.
The fifth aspect: that the contingent things (al-mumkinat) must end in the chain of need (silsilat al-haja) at the Necessary of Existence (wajib al-wujud). We say: the Necessary of Existence either possesses all those considerations (al-umur al-mu'tabara) for efficient causality (al-mu'aththiriyya) from eternity (al-azal) or it did not possess those considerations in eternity. If the first, then from its perpetuity (dawam) together with the perpetuity of all those considerations for efficient causality, the perpetuity of its effects (athar) follows. On this assumption, it is established that the dependence (istinad) of the effect on the efficient cause does not depend on the effect's being originated (hadith). And if we say that He Most High did not possess from eternity all the considerations for efficient causality, then the attainment of those considerations for efficient causality would be originated (hadith), then the discussion about how they occur is like the first, and infinite regress (tasalsul) follows, which is impossible (muhal).
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This is the fifth sub-objection. Razi builds on the principle that the chain of need in contingent things must terminate in a self-necessary being (wajib al-wujud). He then presents a dilemma regarding the Necessary Being's qualifications for causality (al-umur al-mu'tabara fi al-mu'aththiriyya). Either the Necessary Being possesses all these qualifications eternally, or they come to be at some point. If He possesses them eternally, then since He is perpetual, and the qualifications are perpetual, His effects would also be perpetual — meaning the effect does not need to be originated (hadith) to depend on the cause. If the qualifications are not eternal but originated, then they themselves require an explanation, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. Thus, the first horn is necessary, showing that an effect can be dependent on a cause without being originated.

Key Terms
الممكنات (al-mumkinat)
سلسلة الحاجة (silsilat al-haja)
واجب الوجود (wajib al-wujud)
الأمور المعتبرة (al-umur al-mu'tabara)
المؤثرية (al-mu'aththiriyya)
الأزل (al-azal)
الدوام (al-dawam)
الآثار (al-athar)
الاستناد (al-istinad)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All effects must be originated in time to be dependent on a cause.
Correction: Razi shows that if the cause is eternal and its conditions are eternal, the effect can also be eternal while still being dependent. Dependence does not entail temporal origination.

Checkpoint
What follows if the Necessary of Existence possesses all considerations for efficient causality from eternity?
A) Its effects must be originated.
B) Its effects may be perpetual along with it.
C) Infinite regress becomes necessary.
D) The effects become independent of the cause.

Key Takeaway
Dependence on a cause does not require the effect to be temporal; if the cause is eternal, the effect can be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every effect depends on an eternal cause, what does that imply about your own actions and their consequences? How does this reshape your understanding of personal responsibility?
?
Razi argues that God's causality conditions are eternal. If God's causal power is not bound by time, how should that change your daily reliance on Him?
?
The passage shows that dependence does not require temporal origination. In what area of your life do you assume that 'new' things must have a temporal origin, and how might you now see them as eternally dependent on God?

Sixth Sub-Objection: Self-Preponderating vs. Enduring Possible
Razi contrasts a self-necessitating agent with a continuing possible to show that need depends on contingency, not origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the need for an external cause depends on a thing's essential contingency, not on whether it is originated or enduring.

Core Idea
Need for a cause comes from contingency, not from coming into existence.

Razi presents a thought experiment that separates the need for a cause from the fact of being originated. By comparing a self-necessitating originated thing with an enduring possible, he shows that what truly demands a cause is the possibility of non-existence, not temporal origination. This passage, though incomplete, lays out the key contrast.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the text cuts off at the end.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage contrasts two cases: an originated thing that is necessary by its own essence, and an enduring existent for which existence is like non-existence. Find each one and note their different conclusions regarding the need for a preponderator.
3
Understand the key terms
Grasp the meaning of 'self-preponderating' (mutarajjih li-dhatihi) and 'external preponderating efficient cause' (al-mu'aththir al-murajjih al-khariji). Recognize how they relate to the argument.
4
Consider the incomplete conclusion
The text ends with 'And this indicates...' Think about what conclusion Razi is likely drawing: that the need for a cause is based on contingency, not origination.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس : أنا لو تصورنا محدثًا حدث مع وجوب أن يحدث لذاته، قطعنا بأنه لا حاجة به في حدوثه إلى المرجح ؛ لأنه لما كان مترجحًا لذاته فالمترجح لذاته وبذاته يمتنع استناده إلى المؤثر المرجح الخارجي، وأما إذا تصورنا موجودا باقيًا واعتقدنا أن الوجود بالنسبة إليه كالعدم قطعنا بأنه لا بد له من المرجح، وهذا يدل على
The sixth aspect: if we imagine an originated thing that came to be with a necessity that it comes to be by its essence, we decide that it has no need in its origination for a preponderator (murajjih); because since it is preponderated (mutarajjih) by its essence, then what is preponderated by itself and its essence is impossible for it to be dependent on an external preponderating efficient cause (al-mu'aththir al-murajjih al-khariji). But if we imagine an enduring existent (mawjud baqi) and we hold that existence is like non-existence relative to it, we decide that it must have a preponderator. And this indicates...
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This is the sixth sub-objection, though it is incomplete. Razi presents a thought experiment: imagine an originated thing that necessarily occurs by its own essence (i.e., its occurrence is necessary from its own nature). In that case, it does not need an external preponderator (murajjih) because it is self-preponderating. On the other hand, consider an enduring existent (mawjud baqi) for which existence is like non-existence (i.e., it is equally possible that it exist or not). In that case, we judge that it must have a preponderator to tip the scales towards existence. This shows that the need for a cause is not tied to origination (huduth) but to essential contingency. The argument is cut off, but the intended conclusion is that the enduring possible still requires a cause.

Key Terms
محدث (muhdath/originated)
وجوب (wujub/necessity)
لذاته (li-dhatihi/by its essence)
المرجح (al-murajjih/preponderator)
مترجح لذاته (mutarajjih li-dhatihi/preponderated by itself)
المؤثر المرجح الخارجي (al-mu'aththir al-murajjih al-khariji/external preponderating efficient cause)
موجود باق (mawjud baqi/enduring existent)
الوجود كالعدم (al-wujud ka al-'adam/existence like non-existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only originated (created) things need a cause; eternal things do not.
Correction: Razi argues that even an enduring (eternal) possible needs a cause because it is contingent (existence like non-existence). The need is for a preponderator, not for a creator in time.

Checkpoint
According to the incomplete sixth sub-objection, what is true of an enduring existent for which existence is like non-existence?
A) It has no need for a preponderator.
B) It must have a preponderator.
C) It is essentially necessary.
D) It cannot be imagined.

Key Takeaway
The need for an external cause is based on a thing's essential contingency, not on whether it is originated or eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is as fragile as a scale where non-existence is equally possible, what external preponderator are you relying on to keep you existing right now? Have you ever truly thanked that preponderator for this moment?
?
Razi contrasts a self-necessitating thing with a contingent enduring being. You are not self-necessitating. Does your soul feel the weight of absolute dependence every second? If not, what keeps you from feeling that fragility?
?
You know you could cease to exist at any instant. If existence and non-existence are equal for you, what tips the scale? Would you live differently if you felt the tipping factor—Allah's will—with every breath? What change will you make today because of this?

Third Proof: Temporality Does Not Cause Need for a Cause
Imam Razi presents the third proof that need for a cause is due to possibility, not temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why temporality (ḥudūth) cannot be the cause of an effect's need for a cause, based on Imam Razi's dependency argument.

Core Idea
Temporality depends on existence, so it cannot cause the need for a cause.

Imam Razi presents a third proof that the need of an effect for a cause is not due to temporality. He defines temporality as the state of being preceded by non-existence, then shows that this state depends on the existence of the thing itself, which in turn depends on a chain of causation. If temporality were the cause of need, an infinite regress would follow—an impossibility. This passage sharpens our understanding of causality in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source and its English translation. Focus on the logical flow, not every detail at first.
The argument is dense; read twice.
2
Identify the definition of temporality
Find how Razi defines temporality: it is the existence of a thing being preceded by its non-existence. This is a crucial starting point.
3
Trace the dependency chain
Follow the sequence: temporality depends on the thing's existence, existence depends on the cause, cause depends on need, need depends on its own cause, etc. Notice each link.
Each step is a condition or part.
4
Understand the absurd consequence
If temporality were the cause of need, then the need would depend on temporality which depends on existence, etc., leading to an infinite regress of posteriorities. This is the core of the proof.
Consider why this regress is impossible.
5
Formulate the conclusion
Conclude that temporality has no role in causing the effect's need for a cause. The need must be due to something else—namely, possibility (imkān).
This connects to broader kalām discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : أن الحدوث عبارة عن كون وجود الشيء مسبوقا بعدمه، وهذه المسبوقية صفة من صفات الشيء، ونعت من نعوته وصفة الشيء مفتقرة الثبوت إلى تحقق الموصوف، فحدوث الشيء مفتقر إلى وجوده، ثم إن وجوده مفتقر إلى تأثير المؤثر فيه الذي هو مفتقر إلى احتياج الأثر إلى المؤثر، الذي هو مفتقر إلى علة ذلك الاحتياج، وإلى جزء تلك العلة، وإلى شرط تلك العلة، فلو كان الحدوث علة للحاجة أو جزءاً من تلك العلة أو شرطا لتلك العلة، لزم تأخر الشيء عن بمراتب، وهو محال، فعلمنا أنه لا عبرة بالحدوث في حصول احتياج الأثر إلى المؤثر.
The third proof: That temporality is the expression of the existence of a thing being preceded by its nonexistence, and this precedentiality is an attribute of the thing and a quality of its qualities, and the attribute of a thing is dependent in its existence on the realization of the described. So the temporality of a thing is dependent on its existence, then its existence is dependent on the influence of the efficient cause in it, which is dependent on the need of the effect for the cause, which is dependent on the cause of that need, and on a part of that cause, and on a condition of that cause. So if temporality were the cause of need, or a part of that cause, or a condition of that cause, it would necessitate the thing being posterior by many degrees, which is impossible. Thus we know that temporality has no consideration in the occurrence of the effect's need for the cause.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof that the need of an effect for a cause is not due to temporality. The argument begins by defining temporality as the existence of a thing being preceded by its nonexistence. This precedentiality is an attribute of the thing, and an attribute depends for its existence on the existence of the subject (the described). Hence, the temporality of a thing depends on the thing's existence. But the thing's existence depends on the influence of the efficient cause, which in turn depends on the effect's need for the cause, which depends on the cause of that need, its parts, and its conditions. If temporality were the cause or part of the cause or condition of the need, then the effect would be posterior to itself by multiple steps—an infinite regress of posteriorities—which is absurd. Therefore, temporality is irrelevant to the need of the effect for the cause.

Key Terms
الحدوث
المسبوقية
الموصوف
المؤثر
الأثر
العلة
الحاجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporality itself is the reason an effect needs a cause.
Correction: Temporality is an attribute that depends on existence, so it cannot be the cause of the need for a cause; rather, the need arises from the possibility (imkān) of the effect.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, why can temporality not be the cause of the need for a cause?
A) Because temporality is a non-existent attribute.
B) Because if temporality caused need, the effect would be posterior to itself by many degrees, leading to an infinite regress.
C) Because temporality is identical to the existence of the thing.
D) Because temporality is a necessary attribute.

Key Takeaway
Temporality is an attribute dependent on existence, so it cannot serve as the cause of the effect's need for a cause; the need must be grounded in possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your temporality—being created in time—is not why you need Allah. According to this proof, your need is rooted in your very possibility—that you could not exist. Do you live as if you could be self-sufficient? When was the last time you truly felt your existence could vanish at any moment, and how did that shape your prayer?
?
The proof shows that attributing need to temporality leads to an absurd infinite regress. In your own life, do you sometimes base your reliance on Allah on temporary things—like past good deeds, a spiritual high, or a particular blessing—rather than on your constant, inherent need? What would change if you grounded your need on your very possibility?
?
Imam Razi argues that an attribute depends on its subject. Your temporality is an attribute of your existence; it does not stand alone. Does your awareness of being a contingent being—possible in itself—make you feel more dependent on Allah right now? How can you turn that awareness into a concrete act of reliance today?

The Fourth Proof: Possibility Demands a Cause
Imam Razi uses a dilemma to link possibility with causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the concept of specific possibility to prove that the need for a cause is due to possibility itself, not temporality.

Core Idea
Need for a cause stems from possibility, not from being temporal.

Imam Razi now presents his fourth proof, which refines the understanding of causality. He argues that if possibility of nonexistence requires a cause, then even a perpetual nonexistence would need a preponderator. Conversely, if not, then existence would be independent, which is absurd. The proof demonstrates that the need for a cause is intrinsic to the nature of possibility.

Guided Path
1
Define al-mumkin al-khass
Read the first sentence carefully: 'The specific possible is that of which it is true that its existence is possible and its nonexistence is possible.' This sets up the key concept.
Note that 'specific possible' refers to a thing that is equally capable of existing or not existing.
2
Follow the dilemma
The argument presents two possibilities: either the possibility of nonexistence requires a cause (preponderator) or it does not. Trace each branch and its consequences.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum structure.
3
Understand the rejection of the second branch
The second branch is rejected because it would lead to the possible being independent of a cause, which is impossible. Note the symmetry argument: possibility is the same for existence and nonexistence.
4
Conclude the lesson’s point
The accepted branch shows that even a persistent possible (nonexistence) needs a cause, proving that need is tied to possibility, not to temporality.
This refutes the view that only temporal things require a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع وهو أن الممكن الخاص هو الذي يصدق عليه أنه يمكن وجوده، ويمكن عدمه، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : كون العدم ممكنا إما أن يكون محوجًا إلى المؤثر ولا يكون كذلك، فإن كان الأول كانه العدم الممكن المستمر من الأزل إلى الأبد، محتاجًا إلى المرجح ، فثبت أن الشيء حال استمراره قد يفتقر إلى المؤثر، وإن كان الثاني فهو باطل؛ لأن ماهية الإمكان الخاص واحدة في جانبي الوجود والعدم، فإن لم يكن في جانب العدم محوجًا إلى المرجح فكذا في جانب الوجود يحوج إلى المؤثر، وحينئذ يلزم استغناء الممكن عن المؤثر، وهو محال.
The fourth proof: That the specific possible is that of which it is true that its existence is possible and its nonexistence is possible. And when this is established, we say: The fact that nonexistence is possible either necessitates the need for the cause or not. If the first, then the possible nonexistence continuous from eternity would be in need of a preponderator. Thus it is established that the thing during its continuance may be in need of the cause. And if the second, it is false, because the essence of specific possibility is one with respect to both existence and nonexistence. So if on the side of nonexistence it is not necessitating of the need for a preponderator, then likewise on the side of existence it would necessitate the need for the cause. And then the independence of the possible from the cause would follow, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof. It defines the specific possible (al-mumkin al-khāṣṣ) as that of which both existence and nonexistence are equally possible. Given that, we consider the possibility of nonexistence: either that possibility requires a cause or it does not. If it requires a cause, then even a thing's continuing nonexistence from eternity would need a preponderator, showing that need can exist without temporality. If it does not require a cause, then since possibility is the same for both existence and nonexistence, the possibility of existence would also not require a cause, which implies the possible is independent of a cause—an impossibility. Therefore, the need for a cause is not due to temporality but to possibility itself.

Key Terms
الممكن الخاص
الإمكان
الوجود
العدم
المرجح
المؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only temporal things (those that begin to exist) need a cause for their existence.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that even a possible nonexistence that never began would need a preponderator, because possibility itself—not temporality—is what necessitates a cause.

Checkpoint
In the fourth proof, what does Razi conclude about the need for a cause?
A) It is due to the temporality of the effect.
B) It is due to the possibility of the effect.
C) It is due to the actuality of the effect.
D) It is due to the nonexistence of the effect.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof establishes that the need for a cause is grounded in the possibility of a thing, not in its being temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence and every possible outcome depends on a preponderator, are you living as though you are constantly sustained by Allah's will, or do you take your possibilities for granted? What would change if you truly felt your need for Him every moment?
?
The proof shows that possibility cannot cause itself. Have you ever acted as if your own possibilities — your choices, your future — are self-sufficient? Where in your life do you need to acknowledge that only Allah tips the balance?
?
If the possibility of your own non-existence requires a cause, then your continued existence is not an accident but an act of continuous creation. Does this awareness make you more grateful and more dependent on the Creator? How will you respond to Him today?

Causality and Created Things

Fifth Proof: The Eternity of Effects from an Eternal Cause
A two-branch argument showing effects can be co-eternal with an eternal cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's argument that the dependence of an effect on a cause does not require the effect to be temporal, and that an eternal cause with eternal conditions yields eternal effects.

Core Idea
Effect dependence does not require temporality; an eternal cause yields eternal effects.

Imam Razi presents the fifth proof for the existence of the Necessary Being. He examines the relationship between cause and effect, arguing that if the cause is eternal and fully qualified, its effects can also be eternal. This challenges the assumption that effects must be temporal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then follow the literal translation. Focus on understanding the two possibilities Razi presents.
Pay attention to the terms 'mumkināt' (contingent things) and 'wājib al-wujūd' (Necessary Being).
2
Identify the two possibilities
Razi considers two cases: either the Necessary Being possessed all causative conditions from eternity, or He did not. Notice how each leads to a different conclusion about the effect's temporality.
3
Understand why the second possibility fails
If the conditions were not eternal, they must have come into being, leading to an infinite regress. Razi rejects this as impossible, leaving only the first option.
This shows that the effect's dependence does not require it to be temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : أن الممكنات لا بد من انتهائها في سلسلة الحاجة على واجب الوجود، فنقول : واجب الوجود إما أن يكون مستجمعا لجميع تلك الأمور المعتبرة في المؤثرية في الأزل أو ما كان مستجعًا لتلك الأمور في الأزل، فإن كان الأول لزم من دوامه مع دوام جميع تلك الأمور المعتبرة في المؤثرية، دوام آثاره، وعلى هذا التقدير ثبت أن استناد الأثر إلى المؤثر لا يتوقف على كون الأثر حادثاً، وأما إن قلنا بأنه تعالى ما كان مستجمعًا في الأزل لجميع الأمور المعتبرة في المؤثرية، فحينئذ يكون حصول تلك الأمور المعتبرة في المؤثرية حادثاً، ثم الكلام في كيفية حدوثها كما في الأول، ويلزم التسلسل، وهو محال.
The fifth proof: That contingent things must ultimately terminate in the chain of need to the Necessary of Existence. We say: The Necessary of Existence either possesses all those considered aspects of causation from eternity or does not possess those aspects from eternity. If the first, then from His perpetuity together with the perpetuity of all those considered aspects of causation, the perpetuity of His effects follows. On this estimation, it is established that the dependence of the effect on the cause does not depend on the effect being temporal. And if we say that He, exalted, did not possess from eternity all those considered aspects of causation, then the occurrence of those considered aspects of causation is temporal. Then the discussion about how they occur is as before, and an infinite regress follows, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof. It begins with the premise that contingent things (al-mumkināt) must ultimately terminate in a chain of need to the Necessary Being (wājib al-wujūd). Then we consider: either the Necessary Being possessed all the conditions required for causation (al-umūr al-mu‘tabara fī al-mu’aththiriyya) from eternity, or He did not. If He did, then since He is eternal and those conditions are eternal, His effects must also be eternal. This shows that the dependence of an effect on a cause does not require the effect to be temporal. If He did not possess those conditions from eternity, then those conditions themselves must have come into being, and we would have to explain their origination, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. Therefore, the only coherent option is that the effect can be co-eternal with the cause, so temporality is not necessary for dependence.

Key Terms
الممكنات
واجب الوجود
المؤثرية
الأزل
التسلسل
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Effects must be temporal because they depend on a cause.
Correction: Razi shows that if the cause is eternal with all its conditions, the effect can be eternal as well, so dependence does not require temporality.

Checkpoint
What does the fifth proof conclude about the relationship between an effect and its cause?
A) An effect must always be temporal.
B) An effect can be eternal if the cause is eternal and fully qualified.
C) An effect can never be eternal.
D) The cause must be temporal for the effect to be temporal.

Key Takeaway
An effect can be eternal if its cause is eternal and fully qualified — dependence does not entail temporality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine the causal chains in your daily life—your health, provision, relationships—do you genuinely trace your ultimate dependence to the Eternal Cause, or do you stop at secondary means? How would your actions change if you truly lived with the certainty that your entire existence is perpetually dependent on an eternal, timeless Cause?
?
The argument eliminates the possibility of infinite regress in causes. Are there areas in your life where you defer resolution—spiritual growth, repentance, or obedience—thinking 'I will deal with it later' as if the chain of procrastination has no end? What step can you take today to stop that regress and anchor yourself in the Eternal?
?
If the Necessary Being's effects can be eternal, your own life as an ongoing effect of His causation is a continuous manifestation of His power. Does your daily conduct reflect that you are constantly being sustained by the Eternal, or do you live as though you are self-sufficient? What specific attitude or habit must change to align with this truth?

The Sixth Proof: Need is Due to Possibility, Not Temporality
Imam Razi contrasts a self-necessitated agent with a contingent being to show that need is due to possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the need for a cause is rooted in possibility (imkan) rather than in temporal origination (huduth), and how this distinguishes the Necessary Being from contingent beings.

Core Idea
Possibility, not temporality, is the true cause of need.

Imam Razi presents a subtle argument that shifts the foundation of causality. By contrasting two hypothetical beings—one self-necessitated and one eternal but contingent—he shows that the need for an external cause arises from possibility, not from having a beginning in time. This argument strengthens the case for a self-sufficient Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two hypothetical cases
Read the passage and identify the two beings Razi asks us to imagine. Notice how each case leads to a different conclusion about the need for a preponderator.
The two cases are: a self-necessitated agent (مترجح لذاته) and a contingent eternal being (موجود باقي مع الإمكان).
2
Grasp the key distinction
Observe that the self-necessitated agent requires no external cause, while the contingent eternal being does. The difference lies in necessity vs. possibility.
3
Draw the theological conclusion
Recognize that this proves that possibility (imkan) is the cause of need, not temporal origination. This establishes that the Necessary Being is self-sufficient by essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس : أنا لو تصورنا محدثًا حدث مع وجوب أن يحدث لذاته، قطعنا بأنه لا حاجة به في حدوثه إلى المرجح ؛ لأنه لما كان مترجحًا لذاته فالمترجح لذاته وبذاته يمتنع استناده إلى المؤثر المرجح الخارجي، وأما إذا تصورنا موجودا باقيًا واعتقدنا أن الوجود بالنسبة إليه كالعدم قطعنا بأنه لا بد له من المرجح، وهذا يدل على أن علة الحاجة هي الإمكان لا الحدوث.
The sixth proof: That if we conceive of an agent that acts with the necessity that it act by its essence, we decide that it has no need in its action for a preponderator, because it is preponderated by its essence, and what is preponderated by its essence and by itself is impossible to depend on an external preponderating cause. And if we conceive of an existent that persists and consider that existence is like nonexistence to it, we decide that it must have a preponderator. This indicates that the cause of need is possibility, not temporality.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof. It contrasts two cases. First, imagine an agent that acts necessarily by its very essence (i.e., its action is necessitated by its own nature). In that case, we judge that it does not need an external preponderator to bring about its action, because it is already self-preponderated. Such a being is independent. Second, imagine a being that continues to exist but its existence is like its nonexistence—meaning it is contingent, equally possible to be or not be. For such a being, we judge that it must have a preponderator to tip the scale from possibility to actuality. This difference shows that the real cause of need is not temporality (since the second being is eternal but still needs a cause) but rather possibility (imkān). The self-necessitated agent is necessary, not possible, so it needs no cause.

Key Terms
محدث
المترجح لذاته
المرجح الخارجي
موجود باقي
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Everything eternal is self-sufficient and needs no cause.
Correction: Eternal contingent beings still need a cause; only the self-necessitated (the Necessary Being) is self-sufficient.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth proof, what is the cause of the need for a cause?
A) Temporality
B) Possibility
C) Eternity
D) Action

Key Takeaway
The need for a cause stems from possibility, not from having a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Are you living as if you need God only for the big moments of your life, or do you recognize that you need Him for every breath, even if you have been existing for years? What will you change to make this consciousness more real in your heart?
?
Imam Razi asks you to conceive of a being that exists eternally but still needs a cause. What does that say about your own dependence on God? How can you turn this awareness into a concrete act of worship today?
