The Seventh Proof: Three Aspects of a Temporal Thing
Imam Razi enumerates three aspects of a temporal thing and eliminates two to pinpoint the real need for a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi analyzes a temporal thing into three aspects and demonstrates that only possible existence, not temporality, necessitates a cause.

Core Idea
Temporality is incidental; possibility is the real ground for needing a cause.

Imam al-Razi presents his seventh proof for the existence of God, focusing on the nature of temporal things (al-muḥdathāt). He argues that the need for a cause is not due to the fact that something is created in time but due to its inherent possibility. This lesson walks through his precise analysis of the three aspects of a temporal thing—its present existence, prior nonexistence, and the relation between them—to isolate the true reason for causal dependence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: enumeration, elimination, conclusion.
2
Identify the three aspects
The passage lists three 'matters' of a temporal thing: present existence, prior nonexistence, and the fact of precedence. Understand each one clearly.
The third aspect is a relational property, not a thing itself.
3
Follow the eliminations
Razi eliminates the first two candidates for needing a cause. Ask yourself why each is eliminated: prior nonexistence is pure negation, and the fact of precedence becomes necessary once the thing exists.
The second elimination is subtle: what is necessary by itself cannot depend on an external cause.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
After eliminating two, only present existence remains. But absolute existence cannot need a cause (otherwise God would need one), so it must be possible existence. Thus temporality (ḥudūth) is irrelevant; possibility drives the need.
This shifts the kalam argument from temporality to possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السابع : أن المحدث له أمور ثلاثة : الوجود الحاصل في الحال، والعدم السابق، وكون هذا الوجود مسبوقاً بذلك العدم، فنقول : المحتاج إلى المؤثر ليس هو العدم السابق ؛ فإنه نفي محض، وهو مناقض لحصول التأثير، وليس هو أيضا كون ذلك الوجود مسبوقاً بالعدم، فإن كون هذا الوجود مسبوقاً بالعدم أمر واجب لذاته، ممتنع التغير، والواجب لذاته لا يحتاج إلى المؤثر، فلم يبق إلا أن يقال : المحتاج إلى المؤثر هو الوجود الحاصل في الحال، فنقول : لا جائز أن يكون المحتاج إلى المؤثر مطلق الوجود، وإلا لكان الواجب لذاته مفتقرًا إلى المؤثر، وهو محال، ولما بطل هذا القسم لم يبق إلا أن يقال : المحتاج إلى المؤثر هو الوجود الممكن، فإن الحدوث ساقط عن درجة الاعتبار بالكلية.
The seventh proof: That the temporal thing has three matters: the existence occurring in the present, the prior nonexistence, and the fact that this existence is preceded by that nonexistence. We say: The one in need of the cause is not the prior nonexistence, for it is pure negation, which is contrary to the occurrence of effect. Nor is it the fact that this existence is preceded by nonexistence, for this fact is necessary by itself, impossible to change, and what is necessary by itself does not need the cause. Nothing remains except to say: The one in need of the cause is the existence occurring in the present. We say: It is not permissible that the one in need of the cause be absolute existence, otherwise the Necessary Being would be dependent on the cause, which is impossible. And when this division is invalidated, nothing remains except to say: The one in need of the cause is possible existence. Then temporality falls completely from the degree of consideration.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof, which analyzes a temporal thing (al-muḥdath) into three aspects: (1) its present existence, (2) its prior nonexistence, and (3) the fact that its existence is preceded by that nonexistence. The argument examines each: The prior nonexistence is pure negation and cannot receive an effect, so it is not the needy one. The fact of being preceded by nonexistence is a necessary property of a temporal thing—once the thing exists, this fact is necessary and unchangeable, and what is necessary by itself does not need a cause. Thus, the only candidate is the present existence itself. But we cannot say that all existence needs a cause, because that would include the Necessary Being. So it must be possible existence (al-wujūd al-mumkin) that needs a cause. Therefore, temporality (al-ḥudūth) has no role at all; the need is due to possibility.

Key Terms
المحدث
الوجود الحاصل
العدم السابق
المسبوقية
الوجود الممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporality (being created in time) is the reason a thing needs a cause.
Correction: Razi argues that temporality (ḥudūth) is irrelevant; rather, the need for a cause stems from the possible nature of existence (imkān). The fact that a thing is temporal does not itself ground its need for a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh proof, which of the three aspects of a temporal thing is in need of a cause?
A) The prior nonexistence
B) The fact that existence is preceded by nonexistence
C) The existence occurring in the present, considered as possible existence
D) All three aspects together

Key Takeaway
The need for a cause is rooted in the possible nature of existence, not in its temporal origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you had always existed, would you still owe your being to God? Reflect: Is there any moment you feel self-sufficient? How can you remind yourself that your existence is possible at every instant?
?
Razi eliminates every candidate for needing a cause except possible existence. In what areas of your life do you still act as if you are necessary or self-sufficient? What would it look like to live with the constant awareness that you are contingent on God?
?
The passage says temporality is 'completely out of consideration.' This means your need for God is not about when you started but about what you are. Do you ever feel that God is only relevant for beginnings (birth, creation) but not for your ongoing existence? How would your daily reality change if you understood that you are always and fundamentally possible, needing God now as much as at the first moment?

Eighth Proof: Inseparable Attributes Show Effect Can Be Co-eternal
Imam Razi uses inseparable attributes like evenness to show that an effect can be perpetual with its cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses inseparable attributes to demonstrate that an effect can be eternal and simultaneous with its cause.

Core Idea
Inseparable attributes prove that dependence does not require temporal priority.

Imam Razi's eighth proof tackles a common assumption: that effects must come after their causes. By examining attributes that are inseparable from their essences—like evenness for the number four—he shows that an effect can be co-eternal with its cause. This lesson walks through that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the examples of evenness and oddness—they are key to the argument.
Pay attention to the terms 'extrinsic necessary attributes' (الصفات الخارجة اللازمة).
2
Trace the logical steps
Identify how Imam Razi moves from the nature of these attributes to the conclusion. He argues they are not independent, so they are possible in themselves and caused by the essence.
The phrase 'must be possible in itself and necessary through another' is central.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider what this means for causality. If the attribute is perpetually with the essence, then an effect can be eternal as long as its cause is eternal.
This challenges the idea that all effects are temporally posterior to their causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثامن : أن الصفات الخارجة اللازمة للماهية كالزوجية للأربعة، والفردية للخمسة لا بد وأن تكون ممكنة في نفسها؛ لأنه لا يعقل وجود هذه الصفات على سبيل الانفراد والاستقلال، بل لا يعقل ثبوتها إلا عارضة لهذه الماهيات وكل ما لا يكون مستقلا بنفسه ولا منفردًا بذاته فإنه لا بد وأن يكون ممكنا لذاته واجبًا لغيره فإذن هذه اللوازم معلولة بتلك الماهيات، ثم إن تلك الماهيات قط ما كانت خالية عن تلك اللوازم فإن الأربعة لا يعقل أن تكون منفكة عن الزوجية والخمسة لا تكون منفكة عن الفردية، فقد ثبت في هذه الصور استناد الشيء إلى غيره، مع كون الأثر دائما بدوام المؤثر.
The eighth proof: That the extrinsic attributes necessary to an essence, like evenness for four and oddness for five, must be possible in themselves, because it is not conceivable that these attributes exist in isolation and independence; rather, it is not conceivable that they are established except as accidents of these essences. And everything that is not independent by itself and not separate by its essence must be possible by itself and necessary through another. Thus, these necessary concomitants are caused by those essences. Then, those essences are never devoid of those necessary concomitants; for four is not conceivable as separated from evenness, nor five as separated from oddness. So it is established in these cases the dependence of a thing on another, while the effect is perpetual with the perpetuity of the cause.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof, which uses the example of inseparable attributes (al-ṣifāt al-lāzima) that are extrinsic to the essence but necessarily accompany it, such as evenness (al-zawjiyya) for the number four and oddness (al-fardiyya) for five. These attributes are not independent; they exist only as accidents of their essences. Since they are not self-subsistent, they must be possible in themselves and necessary through another—that is, they are caused by the essences they inhere in. Moreover, these essences (like four and five) are never without these attributes; four is always even, five always odd. So we have a clear case of an effect (the attribute) depending on a cause (the essence) while the effect is co-eternal and perpetual with the cause. This shows that dependence does not require the effect to be temporal; an effect can be eternal if its cause is eternal.

Key Terms
الصفات الخارجة
اللازمة
الماهية
الزوجية
الفردية
اللوازم
المعلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All effects must be temporally posterior to their causes.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that inseparable attributes like evenness are effects of their essences yet are co-eternal with them, proving that effects can be simultaneous and perpetual.

Checkpoint
What do the examples of evenness and oddness demonstrate in the eighth proof?
A) That attributes are always independent of essences.
B) That an effect can be eternal and simultaneous with its cause.
C) That temporality is necessary for all effects.
D) That only numbers have attributes.

Key Takeaway
Inseparable attributes demonstrate that an effect can be eternal and simultaneous with its cause, challenging the assumption that all effects are temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence is like the evenness of four—completely dependent on its cause—can you truly claim independence in any aspect of your life? What would it mean to live every moment aware that you are 'necessary through another'?
?
This proof shows that an effect can be eternal with its cause. How does this reshape your understanding of your own relationship with Allah? Are there areas where you still feel you are 'posterior' to Him, meaning you think you come later and therefore are less dependent?
?
The attribute of evenness is inseparable from four. Is your gratitude to Allah inseparable from your awareness of Him? If not, what would it take to make that connection as constant as evenness is to four?

Conclusion of the Eight Proofs: Dependence Does Not Imply Temporality
Imam Razi summarizes that the eight proofs show need for cause is not about temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the eight proofs establish that a thing's need for a cause is due to its possibility, not its temporality, so an effect can be co-eternal with its cause.

Core Idea
Dependence does not require temporality; effects can be eternal.

Imam Razi concludes his eight proofs with a decisive statement. He clarifies that when something depends on another for its existence, it does not automatically mean that thing is temporal (ḥādith). This is crucial for understanding the relationship between the Creator and creation, especially regarding the eternity of the universe argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Notice the concise concluding style and the closing supplication.
This passage is the summary of the entire section on the eight proofs.
2
Understand the key distinction
The passage explicitly denies a necessary link between dependence (iftiqār) and temporality (ḥudūth). Reflect on why this distinction matters: it allows an eternal effect that still depends on its cause.
This counters the common misconception that all effects must be temporal.
3
Connect to broader theological debates
Consider how this conclusion supports the Ashʿarī position that the world, though contingent, can be created ex nihilo without being temporally preceded by its Creator. Relate it to discussions on the eternity of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه الدلائل الثمانية دالة على أنه لا يلزم من افتقار الشيء في وجوده إلى غيره كون الأثر حادثاً وبالله التوفيق.
So these eight proofs indicate that it is not necessary from the dependence of a thing in its existence on another that the effect be temporal. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This short passage is the conclusion of the eight proofs. Imam Razi states that these eight proofs demonstrate that when a thing depends on another for its existence, it does not necessarily follow that the effect is temporal (ḥādith). In other words, an effect can be co-eternal with its cause. The phrase 'Bi-Llāh al-tawfīq' is a traditional closing asking for God's success.

Key Terms

Correction: Imam Razi explicitly denies this, stating that dependence does not necessitate temporality. An effect can be eternal yet still depend on its cause.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Objection: God's Attributes Challenge the Proof
Objection using God's attributes and beginning of answer about necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an objection based on God's eternal attributes poses a dilemma for the proof of the necessary being, and why Razi's answer begins with questioning the nature of necessity.

Core Idea
The attributes create a dilemma: either two necessary beings or an eternal effect.

The opponent in this passage attacks the eight proofs by focusing on God's attributes. If God is knowing through knowledge, then either knowledge is necessary like God, leading to multiple necessary beings, or it is possible but eternal, implying an eternal effect. Both options undermine the opponent's own premises. Razi's reply starts by asking what evidence shows that necessity is a positive attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the dilemma presented against the eight proofs.
Notice the two horns of the dilemma.
2
Identify the two premises being attacked
The first premise states only one necessary being exists; the second says an effect cannot be perpetual with its cause. See how the objection targets each.
3
See the beginning of the answer
Razi quotes the opponent's question about the evidence that necessity is a positive thing, and promises two proofs.
The answer is incomplete here, but sets up the next discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : ما ذكرتم من الدلالة منقوض يكون الله تعالى عالما بالعلم، قادرًا بالقدرة، فإن علم الله تعالى إن كان واجبًا لذاته وذاته واجبة لذاتها فقد حصل موجودان كل واحد منهما واجب لذاته، وذلك يبطل المقدمة الأولى من دليلكم، وإن كان علم الله تعالى ليس واجبًا لذاته بل ممكنا لذاته واجبًا بوجوب ذاته - كان ذلك اعترافًا بكون الأثر والمؤثر دائمين، وذلك يبطل المقدمة الثانية من مقدمات دليلكم، وبالله التوفيق. والجواب : قوله : " ما الدليل على أن الوجوب أمر ثبوتي؟". قلنا : يدل عليه وجهان
Then we say: What you have mentioned of proof is broken by the fact that God, exalted, is knowing through knowledge, powerful through power. For if God's knowledge is necessary by itself and His essence is necessary by itself, then there have occurred two existents, each one necessary by itself, and that invalidates the first premise of your proof. And if God's knowledge is not necessary by itself but rather possible by itself and necessary through the necessity of His essence, then that is an admission that the effect and the cause are perpetual, and that invalidates the second premise of your proof. And with God is success. And the answer: He said: "What is the evidence that necessity is a positive thing?" We say: Two proofs indicate it.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the eight proofs and the beginning of Razi's answer. The objection: The opponent claims that the eight proofs are broken by considering God's attributes: God is knowing through knowledge, powerful through power. If God's knowledge is necessary by itself (wājib li-dhātihi) and God's essence is also necessary by itself, then we have two necessary beings (God and His knowledge), which contradicts the first premise of the opponent's proof (that there is only one necessary being). If instead God's knowledge is possible by itself but necessary through God's necessity (i.e., it is an effect that is eternal), then that admits that an effect (knowledge) can be perpetual with its cause (God), which invalidates the second premise of the opponent's proof (that effects must be temporal). So either way, the opponent's argument fails. Then Razi begins his answer by quoting the opponent's question: 'What is the evidence that necessity (al-wujūb) is a positive thing?' He responds that there are two proofs, but the text cuts off. This is the beginning of the answer.

Key Terms
العلم
القدرة
الوجوب
الواجب لذاته
الممكن لذاته
الأثر والمؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's attributes are identical to His essence in all respects, so no dilemma arises.
Correction: The objection posits attributes as distinct causal principles, leading to the dilemma. Razi's answer challenges the nature of necessity itself, not the distinction or identity.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The objection uses God's attributes to expose a dilemma: either multiple necessary beings or an eternal effect, both undermining the proof of a single necessary being.

The Seventh Proof: Need Is for Possibility, Not Temporality
Al-Razi gives the seventh proof that need for an effect is due to possibility not temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi argues that a thing's need for a cause is due to its possibility (imkan) rather than its temporality (huduth).

Core Idea
Temporality is not the cause of need—possibility is.

Al-Razi presents a sophisticated logical argument to show that what makes a created thing dependent on a cause is not the fact that it came into existence after non-existence, but rather its inherent possibility. He analyzes the originated thing into three aspects and eliminates each one until only possible existence remains. This lesson walks through that elimination process.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then the English translation. Note the key terms: imkan, huduth, mu'aththir.
2
Identify the three aspects of an originated thing
Al-Razi breaks down an originated thing into three parts: its present existence, its prior non-existence, and the fact that its existence is preceded by non-existence. Find these in the passage.
3
Follow the elimination argument
The passage then eliminates the prior non-existence and the 'preceded-by' relation as candidates for needing an effect. Only present existence remains, but it cannot be absolute existence. Trace this reasoning.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that the needy existence is possible existence, and temporality (huduth) is irrelevant. Ask yourself why this matters for proving the existence of a necessary being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن علة الحاجة هي الإمكان لا الحدوث. الوجه السابع : أن المحدث له أمور ثلاثة : الوجود الحاصل في الحال، والعدم السابق، وكون هذا الوجود مسبوقاً بذلك العدم، فنقول : المحتاج إلى المؤثر ليس هو العدم السابق ؛ فإنه نفي محض، وهو مناقض لحصول التأثير، وليس هو أيضا كون ذلك الوجود مسبوقاً بالعدم، فإن كون هذا الوجود مسبوقاً بالعدم أمر واجب لذاته، ممتنع التغير، والواجب لذاته لا يحتاج إلى المؤثر، فلم يبق إلا أن يقال : المحتاج إلى المؤثر هو الوجود الحاصل في الحال، فنقول : لا جائز أن يكون المحتاج إلى المؤثر مطلق الوجود، وإلا لكان الواجب لذاته مفتقرًا إلى المؤثر، وهو محال، ولما بطل هذا القسم لم يبق إلا أن يقال : المحتاج إلى المؤثر هو الوجود الممكن، فإن الحدوث ساقط عن درجة الاعتبار بالكلية.
That the cause of need is possibility, not temporality. The seventh proof: The originated thing has three matters: the existence occurring in the present, the prior non-existence, and that this existence is preceded by that non-existence. We say: What needs the effect is not the prior non-existence, for it is pure negation and contradicts the occurrence of effect. Nor is it that this existence is preceded by non-existence, for that this existence is preceded by non-existence is necessary in itself, impossible to change, and the necessary in itself does not need an effect. So nothing remains except to say: What needs the effect is the existence occurring in the present. We say: It is not permissible that what needs the effect is absolute existence, otherwise the Necessary in itself would depend on an effect, which is absurd. When this division is invalidated, nothing remains except to say: What needs the effect is possible existence, for temporality is entirely removed from consideration.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof from Imam al-Razi on the topic of need for an effect. It is a definitional argument. He begins by stating the thesis: 'The cause of need is possibility, not temporality.' Then he lays out the proof. He identifies three aspects of an originated thing: its present existence, its prior non-existence, and the fact that its existence is preceded by that non-existence. He then examines which of these needs an effect (al-mu'aththir). The prior non-existence is pure negation, so it cannot receive effect. The fact that existence is preceded by non-existence is a necessary attribute of the originated thing; it is necessary in itself and immutable, so it does not need an effect. Thus only the present existence remains. But it cannot be absolute existence, because that would imply the Necessary Being depends on an effect. So it must be possible existence. Therefore, the need is due to possibility, not temporality. The conclusion: temporality (huduth) is irrelevant.

Key Terms
الإمكان (al-imkan: possibility)
الحدوث (al-huduth: temporality/origination)
المحتاج (al-muhtaj: the needy)
المؤثر (al-mu'aththir: the effecter/cause)
الوجود الممكن (al-wujud al-mumkin: possible existence)
الواجب لذاته (al-wajib li-dhatihi: necessary in itself)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that temporality (huduth) itself is the cause of a thing's need for a cause.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that temporality is irrelevant; the real cause is possibility (imkan).

Checkpoint
According to the seventh proof, what is the real cause of a thing's need for an effect?
A) Temporality (huduth)
B) Prior non-existence
C) Possibility (imkan)
D) Being preceded by non-existence

Key Takeaway
The need for a cause is due to a thing's possibility (imkan), not its temporality (huduth).

?

?
If need is rooted in possibility, and Allah alone is Necessary, then everything about you—your strengths, your intelligence, your health—is contingent. What would it look like to live today fully aware that you are nothing but a possible existence sustained by the Necessary?

The Eighth Proof: Necessary Attributes Show Eternal Effects
Al-Razi uses necessary attributes to show that an effect can be eternal with its cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses necessary attributes like evenness for four to demonstrate that a caused thing can be eternal as long as its cause is eternal.

Core Idea
An effect can be eternal if its cause is eternal.

In this lesson, we examine al-Razi's eighth proof, which uses necessary attributes—those that are inseparable from their essences—to challenge the assumption that all effects must be temporal. By analyzing examples like evenness for four, al-Razi shows that an effect can coexist perpetually with its cause. This argument reinforces the possibility of eternal divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the argument's flow. Note the examples of evenness and oddness.
The passage is concise; read it at least twice.
2
Understand the Concept of Necessary Attributes
Focus on the idea of 'extrinsic necessary attributes' (الصفات الخارجة اللازمة). These are attributes that are not independent but necessarily accompany an essence, like evenness for four.
They are 'extrinsic' because they are not the essence itself but depend on it.
3
Follow the Causal Reasoning
Al-Razi argues that these attributes are possible in themselves and necessary through another, making them effects of their essences. The fact that they are always present with the essence shows that the effect can be perpetual with the cause.
This is the key step: the effect does not need to be temporal.
4
Connect to the Broader Conclusion
Finally, note how this proof contributes to al-Razi's overall point: dependence on a cause does not necessitate temporal origination. The proof supports the eternity of divine attributes.
This is the eighth and final proof in the series.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثامن : أن الصفات الخارجة اللازمة للماهية كالزوجية للأربعة، والفردية للخمسة لا بد وأن تكون وأن تكون ممكنة في نفسها؛ لأنه لا يعقل وجود هذه الصفات على سبيل الانفراد والاستقلال، بل لا يعقل ثبوتها إلا عارضة لهذه الماهيات وكل ما لا يكون مستقلا بنفسه ولا منفردًا بذاته ؛ فإنه لا بد وأن يكون ممكنا لذاته واجبًا لغيره فإذن هذه اللوازم معلولة بتلك الماهيات، ثم إن تلك الماهيات قط ما كانت خالية عن تلك اللوازم فإن الأربعة لا يعقل أن تكون منفكة عن الزوجية والخمسة لا تكون منفكة عن الفردية، فقد ثبت في هذه الصور استناد الشيء إلى غيره، مع كون الأثر دائما بدوام المؤثر. فهذه الدلائل الثمانية دالة على أنه لا يلزم من افتقار الشيء في وجوده إلى غيره كون الأثر حادثاً وبالله التوفيق.
The eighth proof: The extrinsic attributes necessary to the essence, like evenness for four and oddness for five, must be and must be possible in themselves, because it is not conceivable for these attributes to exist in isolation and independence, rather it is not conceivable for them to subsist except as accidents to these essences. And everything that is not independent in itself nor separate in its essence must necessarily be possible in itself and necessary through another. So then these necessary concomitants are caused by those essences. Moreover, those essences were never devoid of those concomitants, for four cannot be conceived separated from evenness, and five cannot be conceived separated from oddness. So it is established in these cases the dependence of something on another, while the effect is perpetual with the perpetuity of the cause. So these eight proofs indicate that the dependence of a thing in its existence on another does not necessitate that the effect be originated. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof. Al-Razi argues using necessary attributes that are inseparable from their essences, such as evenness for four and oddness for five. These attributes are not independent; they exist only as accidents to their essences. Therefore, they are possible in themselves and necessary through another. They are caused by the essence. Importantly, these attributes are always present with the essence: four is always even. Thus, the effect (the attribute) is eternal as long as the cause (the essence) is eternal. This shows that an effect can be eternal without being originated. Al-Razi then concludes the entire set of eight proofs stating that needing something else does not imply the effect must be originated.

Key Terms
الصفات الخارجة اللازمة (al-sifat al-kharija al-lazima: extrinsic necessary attributes)
اللوازم (al-lawazim: necessary concomitants)
المعلول (al-ma'lul: the caused/effect)
بدوام المؤثر (bi-dawam al-mu'aththir: with the perpetuity of the cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All effects must be temporal (originated in time) if they depend on a cause.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that an effect can be eternal (non-originated) as long as its cause is eternal, using the example of necessary attributes that are always present with their essences.

Checkpoint
What does the eighth proof demonstrate about the relationship between an effect and its cause?
A) The effect must be temporal if it depends on a cause
B) The effect can be eternal if the cause is eternal
C) The effect is always independent of the cause
D) The effect must be possible in itself

Key Takeaway
The eighth proof shows that necessary attributes are eternal effects of their essences, demonstrating that dependence does not require temporal origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If they are effects caused by your existence, and your existence is caused by Allah, then your traits are effects upon effects. Does that make them less real? Or does this lesson teach you that they can be eternal in the sense that Allah sustains them continuously? How does this reshape your view of your own identity as entirely dependent on Him moment by moment?
?
This proof uses numbers to show that an attribute can be eternal with its cause. Now consider your own soul: if it is created and depends on Allah, does its existence have to be temporary? What does it mean for you that your soul's dependence on Allah does not force it to perish? How does that affect your urgency in seeking Him now?
?
You often think that needing something means you are weak or limited. But here, the effect (evenness) is completely dependent on its cause (four) yet is perpetual. Proving That Wajub Is a Positive Attribute
Al-Razi answers an objection by proving wajub is positive with two proofs and a general rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi defends the positive nature of divine necessity (wajub) against objections, using logical proofs based on the nature of contraries and existential affirmation.

Core Idea
Wajub (necessity) is a positive attribute, not a negation—otherwise nothing could be necessary.

In this passage, al-Razi confronts a dilemma about God's knowledge: if it's necessary in itself, there would be two necessary beings; if it's possible and made necessary by God, the cause and effect would be co-eternal. To resolve this, he must first prove that necessity itself is a positive reality. His two proofs—using the concept of affirmation and the principle of contraries—establish that wajub is a positive attribute, thereby safeguarding the foundation of natural theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and identify the dilemma
Read the opening objection carefully. Notice the two horns: either God's knowledge is necessary in itself (creating two necessary beings) or possible but made necessary by God (making effect co-eternal with cause). Each horn seems to refute the original proof.
This dilemma is used by the objector to challenge the distinction between God and His attributes.
2
Examine Al-Razi's two proofs that wajub is positive
Al-Razi provides two proofs. The first relies on the idea that necessity affirms existence—if it were negative, one contrary would affirm another, which is absurd. The second relies on the law of contraries: necessity and non-necessity are contraries; non-necessity is non-existent, so necessity must be existent.
Focus on how each proof uses logical principles exclusive to kalam.
3
Understand the general rebuttal
Al-Razi concludes that if necessity were purely negative, nothing could be described as necessary in external reality, which would negate the existence of the Necessary Being itself—an impossible consequence.
This rebuttal ties back to the central importance of necessity for theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : ما ذكرتم من الدلالة منقوض يكون الله تعالى عالما بالعلم، قادرًا بالقدرة، فإن علم الله تعالى إن كان واجبًا لذاته وذاته واجبة لذاتها فقد حصل موجودان كل واحد منهما واجب لذاته، وذلك يبطل المقدمة الأولى من دليلكم، وإن كان علم الله تعالى ليس واجبًا لذاته بل ممكنا لذاته واجبًا بوجوب ذاته - كان ذلك اعترافًا بكون الأثر والمؤثر دائمين، وذلك يبطل المقدمة مع أن علمه قديم الثانية من مقدمات دليلكم، وبالله التوفيق. والجواب : قوله : 'ما الدليل على أن الوجوب أمر ثبوتي؟'. قلنا : يدل عليه وجهان الأول : أن الوجوب تأكيد الوجود، فلو كان الوجوب عدميًا لكان أحد النقيضين سببًا لتأكد الآخر، وإنه محال. الثاني : أن الوجوب يناقض اللاوجوب، والداخل تحت اللا وجوب إما الممتنع وإما الممكن الخاص والممتنع معدوم، والممكن الخاص يجوز أن يكون معدوماً، فإذن اللا وجوب محمول على المعدوم فيكون معدوماً، وإن كان اللاوجوب معدومًا كان الوجوب موجودًا ضرورة أن أحد النقيضين لا بد وأن يكون موجوداً وأما المعارضات فإنها بأسرها معارضة بوجه واحد وهو أن الوجوب لو كان عدميًا محضا في الخارج لم يكن الشيء في الخارج موصوفًا بأنه واجب، فهذا يقتضي نفي واجب الوجود لذاته، وإنه محال.
Then we say: What you mentioned of the proof is refuted by that Allah Almighty is knowing through knowledge, powerful through power. For if Allah's knowledge is necessary in itself and His essence is necessary in itself, then two existents each necessary in itself have occurred, and that invalidates the first premise of your proof. And if Allah's knowledge is not necessary in itself but possible in itself and necessary through the necessity of His essence, then that is an admission that the effect and the cause are perpetual, and that invalidates the second premise of your proof while His knowledge is eternal. And with Allah is success. And the answer: He said: 'What is the proof that necessity (wajub) is a positive thing?' We say: Two proofs indicate it. The first: That necessity is an affirmation of existence. So if necessity were negative, then one of the two contraries would be the cause of the affirmation of the other, and that is absurd. The second: That necessity contradicts non-necessity. What falls under non-necessity is either the impossible or the particular possible. The impossible is non-existent, and the particular possible may be non-existent. So non-necessity is predicated of the non-existent and thus is non-existent. And if non-necessity is non-existent, then necessity is existent, necessarily because one of the two contraries must be existent. As for the counter-objections, they are all answered with a single point: that if necessity were purely negative in external reality, then a thing in external reality would not be described as necessary. This would necessitate the negation of the Necessary Being in itself, and that is absurd.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage begins with an objection. Someone says: 'Your proof is refuted by the fact that Allah is knowing through knowledge and powerful through power.' The objection argues that if God's knowledge is necessary in itself, then there would be two necessary beings (God and His knowledge). If it is possible but necessary through God, then the effect (knowledge) is eternal with the cause, which undermines the opponent's assumption that an effect must be temporal. So the objector claims a dilemma. Al-Razi then provides the answer. He quotes the objector asking: 'What is the proof that wajub (necessity) is a positive attribute?' Al-Razi gives two proofs. First: Necessity is an affirmation (ta'kid) of existence; if it were negative, it would cause the affirmation of its opposite, which is absurd. Second: Necessity contradicts non-necessity (la-wajub). Non-necessity applies to the non-existent, so it is non-existent; therefore necessity must be existent because contraries must have one positive. Then he addresses all counter-objections with one general rebuttal: if necessity were purely negative, nothing could be described as necessary, which would negate the Necessary Being itself—an impossible conclusion.

Key Terms
الوجوب (al-wajub: necessity)
الثبوتي (al-thubuti: positive/existent)
العدمي (al-'adami: negative/non-existent)
النقيضين (al-naqidayn: the two contraries)
الوجود (al-wujud: existence)
الممكن الخاص (al-mumkin al-khass: particular possible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessity (wajub) is a negative concept, like non-existence or absence.
Correction: Al-Razi proves necessity is positive: it affirms existence and is the positive contrary of non-necessity, which is non-existent.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what would happen if necessity were negative?
A) Existence would be impossible
B) One contrary would cause the affirmation of the other
C) Nothing would be necessary
D) It would be a contradiction

Key Takeaway
Necessity (wajub) is a positive attribute because it affirms existence and is the existent contrary of non-necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If wajub were merely a negation, your worship would be based on what Allah is not, not on what He is. Do you actively affirm His positive attributes in your daily life, or do you fall into thinking of Him only in terms of negation? What is one positive name of Allah you can meditate on today to change that?
?
Al-Razi argues that if necessity were negative, nothing could be described as necessary. Consider your own certainty about Allah's existence: is it a living, positive conviction that shapes your choices, or is it a vague absence of doubt? What concrete action would prove that you truly believe His necessity is real and demands your submission?
?
The proof shows necessity is an affirmation of existence. Reflect: Are you affirming Allah's existence through obedience, or are you living as if His necessity is merely theoretical? What is one relationship, habit, or thought you need to surrender to the reality that He alone is truly necessary?

The General Proof and First Proof That Determination Is Positive
Al-Razi presents the question, a general argument, and the first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial argument and the first proof that determination is a positive attribute beyond the essence.

Core Idea
Determination is a real, existent attribute that distinguishes individuals sharing an essence.

In this passage, al-Razi presents the question and then offers a general argument based on similarity and difference between two humans, followed by a direct proof from the part-whole relationship. This lays the foundation for understanding determination as a positive attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Identify the general argument
Notice how al-Razi uses the example of two humans: they share humanity but differ in being 'this' or 'that'. The distinguishing factor must be additional to the essence.
The conclusion is that this additional factor cannot be non-existent.
3
Examine the first proof
The first proof argues that determination is part of the essence of the determinate thing, and since the determinate thing exists, its part must exist.
This directly establishes determination as an existent attribute.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why al-Razi starts with these arguments: they establish that determination is a real attribute, not merely a mental concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ما الدليل على أن التعين أمر ثبوتي؟ قلنا : الدليل عليه أن هذا الإنسان يساوي ذلك الإنسان في كونه إنساناً، ولا يساويه في كونه هذا أو ذاك، وما به المشاركة غير ما به المباينة، فالتعين أمر زائد على الماهية ثم ذلك الزائد لا يجوز أن يكون عدميًا. ويدل عليه وجهان الأول : أن التعين جزء من ماهية المعين والمعين من حيث إنه ذلك المعين موجود، وجزء الموجود موجود فالتعين أمر موجود. الثاني : أن التعين لو كان عدما لم يكن عدم أي تعين اتفق، بل كان عبارة عن كون هذا الشيء ليس هو ذلك الشيء، فإذا فسرنا تعين هذا بعدم تعين إن كان عدما كان تعين هذا بعدم تعين ذاك، فتعين ذاك إن كان عدما كان تعين عدما للعدم، وعدم العدم وجود، فتعين هذا أمر موجود، وإن كان تعين ذاك أمرًا وجوديًا كان ذلك التعين أمرًا موجودًا ، فثبت أن أحد التعينين أمر موجود، وإذا كان وجب أن يكون كل تعين أمرًا موجوداً ؛ لأن حقيقة التعين حقيقة واحدة لا تختلف بصورة وصورة. وأما المعارضة الأولى فجوابها أن الماهية والتعين إذا اجتمعا فبانضياف التعين إلى الماهية صارت الماهية متعينة وبانضياف الماهية إلى التعين صار التعين متعينا، وبهذا الطريق ينقطع التسلسل. لا يقال : إنه لم يلزم التسلسل، لكنه يلزم الدور؛ لأنا نقول : الدور إنما يلزم لو كانت ماهية كل واحد منهما سببًا لماهية الآخر، لكننا لا نقول كذلك، بل نقول ماهية
He said: 'What is the proof that determination (ta'ayyun) is a positive thing?' We said: The proof is that this human equals that human in being human, but does not equal him in being this or that. What is shared is not what distinguishes. So determination is additional to the essence, and that addition cannot be negative. Two proofs indicate it: The first: That determination is part of the essence of the determinate thing, and the determinate thing qua that determinate thing is existent, and a part of the existent is existent, so determination is existent. The second: That if determination were non-existent, it would not be the non-existence of any determination whatever, but rather it would be an expression of that this thing is not that thing. So if we interpret the determination of this as the non-existence of that determination, if it were non-existent, then the determination of this would be the non-existence of the determination of that. So the determination of that, if it were non-existent, would be the non-existence of non-existence, and the non-existence of non-existence is existence, so the determination of this is existent. And if the determination of that is an existent thing, then that determination is existent. So it is established that one of the two determinations is an existent thing, and if so, then every determination must be existent, because the reality of determination is one reality that does not differ from one instance to another. As for the first counter-objection, its answer is that when the essence and determination come together, by the addition of determination to the essence, the essence becomes determinate, and by the addition of the essence to determination, determination becomes determinate, and in this way the infinite regress is cut off. It cannot be said that no infinite regress occurs, but rather a circularity occurs; for we say: Circularity would only occur if the essence of each were the cause of the essence of the other, but we do not say that. Rather we say that the essence...
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is an objection-answer pair regarding determination (ta'ayyun). The objector asks for proof that ta'ayyun is a positive attribute. Al-Razi first gives a general argument: two humans share humanity but differ in being 'this' or 'that'; the distinguishing factor must be additional to the essence and cannot be non-existent. Then he provides two detailed proofs. The first: ta'ayyun is part of the essence of the determinate thing; since the determinate thing exists, its part exists. The second: a logical proof. If ta'ayyun were non-existent, then saying 'the determination of this is the non-existence of the determination of that' would lead to either the determination of that being non-existent (making the determination of this the non-existence of non-existence, hence existence) or the determination of that being existent. Either way, one determination is existent, and since the nature of determination is uniform, all determinations must be existent. He then answers a counter-objection: if determination and essence mutually determine each other, there might be an infinite regress or circularity. The answer: no circularity because the relation is not causal in both directions; the essence does not cause determination in the same way determination causes the essence to be determinate. The text ends mid-sentence, likely continuing on the next page.

Key Terms
التعين (al-ta'ayyun: determination)
الماهية (al-mahiyya: essence)
المعين (al-mu'ayyan: the determinate thing)
الموجود (al-mawjud: existent)
العدم (al-'adam: non-existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Determination is merely a relational concept with no real existence.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that determination must be a positive attribute because it distinguishes individuals and is part of what makes a thing what it is.

Checkpoint
What does the general argument use to show that determination is additional to the essence?
A) The difference between existence and non-existence
B) The similarity and difference between two humans
C) The part-whole relationship
D) The concept of circularity

Key Takeaway
The first proof establishes that determination is a real, existent attribute because it is part of the existent determinate thing.

The Second Proof and the Answer to the Objection
Al-Razi gives the second proof and answers a counter-objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second logical proof that determination must be existent and how al-Razi answers the objection of circularity.

Core Idea
Logical analysis shows that at least one determination must exist, and mutual addition prevents infinite regress.

In this continuation, al-Razi presents a more intricate logical proof that determination cannot be non-existence, and then he addresses a potential objection about infinite regress or circularity. This solidifies the doctrine that determination is a positive attribute.

The objection concerns infinite regress or circularity if both determination and essence mutually determine each other. Al-Razi answers by describing mutual addition without bilateral causation.

قوله : ما الدليل على أن التعين أمر ثبوتي؟ قلنا : الدليل عليه أن هذا الإنسان يساوي ذلك الإنسان في كونه إنساناً، ولا يساويه في كونه هذا أو ذاك، وما به المشاركة غير ما به المباينة، فالتعين أمر زائد على الماهية ثم ذلك الزائد لا يجوز أن يكون عدميًا. ويدل عليه وجهان الأول : أن التعين جزء من ماهية المعين والمعين من حيث إنه ذلك المعين موجود، وجزء الموجود موجود فالتعين أمر موجود. الثاني : أن التعين لو كان عدما لم يكن عدم أي تعين اتفق، بل كان عبارة عن كون هذا الشيء ليس هو ذلك الشيء، فإذا فسرنا تعين هذا بعدم تعين إن كان عدما كان تعين هذا بعدم تعين ذاك، فتعين ذاك إن كان عدما كان تعين عدما للعدم، وعدم العدم وجود، فتعين هذا أمر موجود، وإن كان تعين ذاك أمرًا وجوديًا كان ذلك التعين أمرًا موجودًا ، فثبت أن أحد التعينين أمر موجود، وإذا كان وجب أن يكون كل تعين أمرًا موجوداً ؛ لأن حقيقة التعين حقيقة واحدة لا تختلف بصورة وصورة. وأما المعارضة الأولى فجوابها أن الماهية والتعين إذا اجتمعا فبانضياف التعين إلى الماهية صارت الماهية متعينة وبانضياف الماهية إلى التعين صار التعين متعينا، وبهذا الطريق ينقطع التسلسل. لا يقال : إنه لم يلزم التسلسل، لكنه يلزم الدور؛ لأنا نقول : الدور إنما يلزم لو كانت ماهية كل واحد منهما سببًا لماهية الآخر، لكننا لا نقول كذلك، بل نقول ماهية
He said: 'What is the proof that determination (ta'ayyun) is a positive thing?' We said: The proof is that this human equals that human in being human, but does not equal him in being this or that. What is shared is not what distinguishes. So determination is additional to the essence, and that addition cannot be negative. Two proofs indicate it: The first: That determination is part of the essence of the determinate thing, and the determinate thing qua that determinate thing is existent, and a part of the existent is existent, so determination is existent. The second: That if determination were non-existent, it would not be the non-existence of any determination whatever, but rather it would be an expression of that this thing is not that thing. So if we interpret the determination of this as the non-existence of that determination, if it were non-existent, then the determination of this would be the non-existence of the determination of that. So the determination of that, if it were non-existent, would be the non-existence of non-existence, and the non-existence of non-existence is existence, so the determination of this is existent. And if the determination of that is an existent thing, then that determination is existent. So it is established that one of the two determinations is an existent thing, and if so, then every determination must be existent, because the reality of determination is one reality that does not differ from one instance to another. As for the first counter-objection, its answer is that when the essence and determination come together, by the addition of determination to the essence, the essence becomes determinate, and by the addition of the essence to determination, determination becomes determinate, and in this way the infinite regress is cut off. It cannot be said that no infinite regress occurs, but rather a circularity occurs; for we say: Circularity would only occur if the essence of each were the cause of the essence of the other, but we do not say that. Rather we say that the essence...
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is an objection-answer pair regarding determination (ta'ayyun). The objector asks for proof that ta'ayyun is a positive attribute. Al-Razi first gives a general argument: two humans share humanity but differ in being 'this' or 'that'; the distinguishing factor must be additional to the essence and cannot be non-existent. Then he provides two detailed proofs. The first: ta'ayyun is part of the essence of the determinate thing; since the determinate thing exists, its part exists. The second: a logical proof. If ta'ayyun were non-existent, then saying 'the determination of this is the non-existence of the determination of that' would lead to either the determination of that being non-existent (making the determination of this the non-existence of non-existence, hence existence) or the determination of that being existent. Either way, one determination is existent, and since the nature of determination is uniform, all determinations must be existent. He then answers a counter-objection: if determination and essence mutually determine each other, there might be an infinite regress or circularity. The answer: no circularity because the relation is not causal in both directions; the essence does not cause determination in the same way determination causes the essence to be determinate. The text ends mid-sentence, likely continuing on the next page.

التعين (al-ta'ayyun: determination)
العدم (al-'adam: non-existence)
الموجود (al-mawjud: existent)
الدور (al-dawr: circularity)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul: infinite regress)

Responding to Arguments on Dependence During Continuance
Al-Razi begins a response to arguments about dependence during continuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi begins his response to arguments about dependence during the state of continuance, setting up a logical dilemma.

Core Idea
Dependence on the cause persists even when the thing continues to exist.

In this passage, Al-Razi responds to alternative views that dependence may occur in the state of continuance. He asserts that his proof is stronger and introduces a dilemma: when we ascribe the continuant to its efficient cause, that cause either produces an effect or it does not. When does an effect need its cause?
Razi argues that effects need causes only at origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that an effect only requires a cause at the moment of its origination, not during its continued existence.

Core Idea
An effect needs a cause only to begin, not to continue.

This lesson presents Imam Razi's incisive argument on causality. He addresses the view that an effect continuously depends on its cause. Razi uses a logical dilemma to show that need is only at origination. The passage is from his work Al-Arbaeen fi Usul al-Din.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a structured dilemma here.
2
Identify the dilemma's branches
The passage presents a two-part dilemma: if the cause produces no effect, it's not a cause; if it does produce an effect, the effect either existed before (unintelligible) or is new (originated). Find these branches in the text.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that the need for a cause applies only to the originated aspect, not to the persisting entity. Ask yourself why this matters for theology.
This conclusion supports the independence of the created world after its creation.
4
Note the scope
Razi mentions that detailed refutations of opposing arguments are in larger works. This signals that he is presenting a core argument, not the full debate.
If interested, consult the 'mutilat' (detailed works) mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجوه التي ذكرتموها في بيان أن الافتقار قد يحصل حال البقاء فنقول : إن دليلنا أقوى من الوجوه التي ذكرتموها وذلك لأنا إذا أسندنا الباقي حال بقائه إلى المؤثر فذلك المؤثر إما أن يقال: إنه لم يصدر عنه أثر أو يقال صدر عنه أثر، فإن لم يصدر عنه أثر لم يكن مؤثرًا، وإن صدر عنه أثر فذلك الأثر إما أن يصدق عليه أنه كان حاصلا قبل ذلك أو يصدق عليه أنه ما كان حاصلا قبل ذلك، فإن صدق عليه أنه كان حاصلا قبل ذلك فحينئذ يلزم أن يقال : المؤثر حصل في هذا الوقت شيئًا كان ذلك الشيء حاصلا قبل ذلك وهذا غير معقول، وأما أن يصدق عليه أنه ما كان حاصلا قبل هذا الوقت فهذا الأثر يكون حادثاً لا باقيا فكان المفتقر إلى المؤثر هو الحادث لا الباقي، فهذه نكتة جليلة قوية في بيان أن إسناد الأثر إلى المؤثر لا يحصل إلا حال الحدوث. وأما الجواب عن تلك الوجوه على سبيل التفصيل فمذكور في المطولات.
As for the arguments you mentioned in showing that need (for a cause) may occur during persistence, we say: Our proof is stronger than the arguments you mentioned. Because if we attribute the persisting state to the cause while it is persisting, then that cause either: it is said that no effect issued from it, or it is said that an effect issued from it. If no effect issued from it, then it is not a cause. And if an effect issued from it, then that effect is either truly described as having existed before that time, or truly described as not having existed before that time. If it is truly described as having existed before, then it would follow that the cause produced at this time something that already existed before, which is unintelligible. And if it is truly described as not having existed before this time, then this effect is originated, not persisting. So the one in need of the cause is the originated thing, not the persisting thing. This is a strong and subtle point in showing that attribution of the effect to the cause only occurs at the moment of origination. As for the detailed answer to those arguments, it is mentioned in the detailed works.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents a powerful argument that an effect does not require a cause for its continued existence, only for its origination. Razi addresses those who argue that the effect needs the cause during persistence. He presents a dilemma: if we consider a persisting entity and attribute it to a cause, either the cause produces an effect or not. If not, it's not a cause. If it produces an effect, that effect either previously existed or not. If it previously existed, production of something already existing is unintelligible. If it did not previously exist, then the effect is originated, not persisting, so it is not the persisting entity that needs the cause but rather the originated aspect. Thus, need for a cause is only at origination. The detailed refutations of opposing arguments are in larger works.

Key Terms

الباقي
الحادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An effect continuously requires a cause for its existence.
Correction: Razi argues that need is only at origination; once existing, the effect does not require the cause for persistence.

Causation Only at Origination: A Nukta from Imam Razi
A subtle logical argument about causal attribution and origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that an effect is attributed to its cause only at the moment of origination, and not to permanent existence.

Core Idea
Causation is only about bringing something new into existence, not about sustaining it.

This lesson presents a subtle but powerful point (nukta) from Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. He asks: when exactly does an effect depend on its cause? His answer cuts through a common misconception about how causation works. Let's follow his reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the logical structure: two exclusive possibilities are considered.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage considers whether the effect existed before the agent's action or not. Mark these two cases.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Understand why the first possibility is absurd
If the effect already existed, the agent would be producing something already present, which is unintelligible. Grasp why this is rejected.
Absurdity indicates impossibility.
4
Realize the conclusion
The second possibility (effect did not exist) leads to the effect being a new event (hadith). Thus, only the new event requires an agent.
This is the key insight: causation is tied to origination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أثر لم يكن مؤثرًا، وإن صدر عنه أثر فذلك الأثر إما أن يصدق عليه أنه كان حاصلا قبل ذلك أو يصدق عليه أنه ما كان حاصلا قبل ذلك، فإن صدق عليه أنه كان حاصلا قبل ذلك فحينئذ يلزم أن يقال : المؤثر حصل في هذا الوقت شيئًا كان ذلك الشيء حاصلا قبل ذلك وهذا غير معقول، وأما أن يصدق عليه أنه ما كان حاصلا قبل هذا الوقت فهذا الأثر يكون حادثاً لا باقيا فكان المفتقر إلى المؤثر هو الحادث لا الباقي، فهذه نكتة جليلة قوية في بيان أن إسناد الأثر إلى المؤثر لا يحصل إلا حال الحدوث.
An effect that is not an effect, and if an effect issues from it, then that effect either can be truly described as having been existent before that, or can be truly described as not having been existent before that. If it can be truly described as having been existent before that, then it would be necessary to say: The agent produced at this time something that was existent before that—and this is unintelligible. But if it can be truly described as not having been existent before this time, then this effect is originated, not permanent, so the one in need of an agent is the originated, not the permanent. This is a sublime and strong point in showing that attribution of the effect to the agent only occurs at the moment of origination.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents a precise point (nukta) about causality. It argues that an effect (athar) is only attributed to an agent (mu'aththir) at the moment of its origination (huduth). The reasoning: if an effect already existed before the agent's action, then the agent would be producing something already present, which is absurd. If it did not exist before, then the effect is a new event (hadith) and not a permanent one (baqi). Thus, only the new event is in need of an agent. This establishes that causal attribution occurs only when the effect is new.

Key Terms
أثر (effect)
مؤثر (agent/cause)
حدوث (origination/new event)
باقي (permanent/everlasting)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Causation applies continuously to sustain things in existence.
Correction: This passage shows that causation is only relevant at the moment of origination; once a thing exists, it does not need a cause to continue.

Checkpoint

A) The permanent thing
B) The new event
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Causal attribution is exclusively about bringing something new into existence, not about its continued existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of a habit or achievement you attribute to yourself—like a skill you've developed. According to this lesson, you are only the cause of its origination, not its continued existence. Does that make you feel less proud or more grateful? How might you change the way you acknowledge your own efforts?

If causality only applies at the moment of origination, then what does that mean for your relationship with God? Which aspects of your existence do you still imagine are self-sustaining? Take a moment to identify one thing you assume is 'permanent' in your life that you should instead see as constantly renewed by its Originator.

The argument shows that attributing an effect to an agent only makes sense at the moment it begins. When you look at your own role in creating positive change—in your family, work, or community—are you acting as if you alone sustain that change? What would shift if you recognized that your causal role was only at the start?

The Philosophers' Unified Rebuttal of the Theologians' Answers
Imam Razi presents the theologians' fifth and sixth answers and the philosophers' unified rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers reduce all six theological answers to a single logical flaw: each answer relies on a condition missing in eternity, leading to a dilemma that refutes them.

Core Idea
All six answers collapse into one: a missing condition, leading either to atheism or infinite regress.

Imam Razi presents two more theological answers and then the philosophers' powerful unified rebuttal. The philosophers show that despite their diversity, all answers share the same weakness: they depend on something absent in eternity, which leads either to denial of the Maker or infinite regress. This lesson unpacks that rebuttal and reveals its decisive force.

Guided Path
1
Read the two new answers
Read the fifth and sixth answers (the world was impossible then became possible; free-chooser prefers without reason) and note their apparent independence.
These are meant to avoid the original dilemma.
2
Identify the philosophers' core claim
The philosophers claim all six answers reduce to a single point: that something necessary for the world's existence was absent in eternity.
This is the key insight.
3
See how each answer becomes a condition
Review each answer and notice how the philosophers reinterpret it as positing a condition (sharṭ) that was missing in eternity, such as a specific time, known time, benefit-time, expiration of eternity, possibility-time, or act of preference.
All answers fit this pattern.
4
Understand the dilemma
The philosophers apply their original dilemma: if the missing condition occurs without a preponderator, atheism follows; if with a preponderator, infinite regress follows. Thus the answers are not genuine refutations.
This is the core logical move.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by asserting that these points are by no means answers to the proof. Think about why this rebuttal is considered decisive in the debate.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامسها : أن العالم قبل ذلك الوقت ما كان ممكنا بل كان ممتنعا ثم انقلب في ذلك الوقت. و سادسها : أن القادر المختار يمكنه أن يرجح أحد المقدورين على الآخر من غير مرجح كما أن الهارب من السبع إذا عَنَّ له طريقان متساويان من جميع الوجوه فإنه يختار أحدهما على الآخر من غير مرجح والعطشان إذا خير بين قدحين متساويين فإنه يختار أحدهما على الآخر من غير مرجح. قالت الفلاسفة : لو تأملتم حق التأمل في الحجة التي ذكرتموها لما أوردتم هذه الأجوبة، وذلك لأن حاصل هذه الأجوبة يرجع إلى حرف واحد وهو أن كل ما لا بد منه في إيجاد العالم ما كان حاصلا في الأزل. وأما الجواب الأول : فلأنكم قلتم بأن إرادة الله تعالى تعلقت بإيجاد العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، فإذن العالم إنما لم يوجد في الأزل لأن حصول ذلك الوقت المعين شرط، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، ففات الإيجاد لفوات شرطه. وأما الجواب الثاني: فهو أن الوقت الذي علم الله تعالى أن العالم يوجد فيه شرط لوجود العالم، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاضراً، والعالم لا يوجد لأن شرطه ما كان حاضرًا في الأزل. ذلك وأما الجواب الثالث فهو أن شرط دخول العالم في الوجود حضور الوقت المشتمل على تلك المصلحة، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاضرًا في الأزل فالعالم لم يحصل لأن شرط حصوله كان فائنا. وأما الجواب الرابع : فهو أن انقضاء الأزل شرط الإيجاد، وهذا الشرط ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، ففات الإيجاد لفوات شرطه. وأما الجواب الخامس فهو أن حضور الوقت الذي حدث فيه إمكان العالم شرط الإيجاد، وهذا الشرط ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، ففات الإيجاد لقوات شرطه. وأما الجواب السادس وهو أن القادر يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر لا المرجح فمعناه أن دخول العالم في الوجود يتوقف على ذلك الترجيح، ثم إن ذلك الترجيح ما كان حاصلا في الأزل ففات الإيجاد لفوات شرطه. فثبت أن هذه الأجوبة الستة كلها راجعة إلى حرف واحد، وهو اختيار أحد القسمين المذكورين في تلك الحجة، وهو أن كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية ما كان في الأزل، لكنا ذكرنا هذا القسم وأبطلناه بأن تلك الأمور لما لم تكن حاصلة في الأزل ثم حدثت، فإن كان حدوثها غنيا عن المرجح لزم نفي الصانع، وإن افتقر إلى المرجح عاد التقسيم الأول فيه، ولزم التسلسل، فهذه الوجوه ليست أجوبة عن تلك الحجة البتة أصلا.
And the fifth: that the world before that time was not possible but was impossible, then it changed to possible at that time. And the sixth: that the able free-chooser (al-qādir al-mukhtār) can prefer one of two possible things over the other without a preponderator (murjīḥ), just as the one fleeing from a lion, if presented with two equally balanced paths from all aspects, he chooses one over the other without a preponderator, and the thirsty person, if given a choice between two equal cups, he chooses one over the other without a preponderator. The philosophers said: If you truly reflected on the proof (al-ḥujja) you mentioned, you would not have presented these answers, because the sum total of these answers reduces to a single point: that everything necessary for bringing the world into existence was not present in eternity (al-azal). As for the first answer: you said that the will of God Most High was attached to bringing the world into existence at that specific time, and that time was not present in eternity, so the world was not brought into existence in eternity because the occurrence of that specific time is a condition, and that time was not present in eternity, so the bringing into existence was missed due to the absence of its condition. As for the second answer: it is that the time at which God Most High knew the world would come into existence is a condition for the world's existence, and that time was not present, and the world does not exist because its condition was not present in eternity. As for the third answer: it is that the condition for the world's entry into existence is the presence of the time containing that benefit (al-maṣlaḥa), and that time was not present in eternity, so the world did not occur because the condition for its occurrence was absent. As for the fourth answer: it is that the expiration of eternity is a condition for bringing into existence, and this condition was not present in eternity, so bringing into existence was missed due to the absence of its condition. As for the fifth answer: it is that the presence of the time at which the world's possibility occurred is a condition for bringing into existence, and this condition was not present in eternity, so bringing into existence was missed due to the absence of its condition. As for the sixth answer: and it is that the power-holder prefers one of his possible acts over the other without a preponderator, its meaning is that the world's entry into existence depends on that preference (tarjīḥ), then that preference was not present in eternity, so bringing into existence was missed due to the absence of its condition. So it is established that these six answers all reduce to a single point: the choice of one of the two divisions mentioned in that proof, namely that everything necessary for effective causality (al-muʾaththiriyya) was not in eternity, but we mentioned this division and refuted it by [arguing] that those things, since they were not present in eternity and then occurred, if their occurrence is independent of a preponderator, then the denial of the Maker (al-Ṣāniʿ) follows, and if they depend on a preponderator, the first division applies to it, and infinite regress (al-tasalsul) follows. So these points are by no means answers to that proof at all.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents two additional answers (the fifth and sixth) that theologians offered to the philosophers' argument for the eternity of the world, followed by the philosophers' unified rebuttal of all six answers. The fifth answer claims that the world was impossible before a certain time and then became possible at that time. The sixth answer appeals to the free-chooser's ability to prefer one of two equal options without a reason, citing analogies like a fleeing person choosing between two equal paths. The philosophers then respond by reducing all six answers to a single core claim: something necessary for the world's existence was not present in eternity. They review each answer, showing that each posits a condition (sharṭ) that was absent in eternity, such as a specific time, a known time, a time containing benefit, the expiration of eternity, the time of possibility, or the act of preference. Since all answers rely on a condition missing in eternity, they all fit into one of two divisions from the original proof: either the missing condition is uncaused, which denies the Maker, or it requires another preponderator, leading to infinite regress. Thus, the philosophers conclude that these alleged answers are not genuine responses to their argument.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The theological answers are independent refutations of the philosophers' argument.
Correction: The philosophers show that all six answers share a common structure: they each posit a condition absent in eternity, and this leads to a dilemma that undermines them.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is the common element in all six theological answers?
A) They all deny the principle of causality.
B) They all assert that God's will is timeless.
C) They all claim that something necessary for the world's existence was absent in eternity.
D) They all assume the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' unified rebuttal shows that all six theological answers collapse into a single dilemma, revealing their failure to refute the argument for the world's eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you delay an act of obedience because you are waiting for the 'right time', are you inadvertently adopting the theologians' reasoning that the philosophers dismantled? What condition are you waiting for, and does it truly depend on anything but your own hesitation?
?
If every condition you set for drawing closer to Allah leads either to a denial of His power or an infinite postponement, what is the one step you must take today, not because conditions are perfect, but because He alone is the Preponderator?
?
The philosophers argued that positing a missing condition either nullifies the Creator or leads to regress. In your own life, what habit or excuse have you been using to delay repentance that, upon reflection, fits this same logical trap? How will you break it before tomorrow?
