Causality and Created Things
intermediate
10 min
Refuting the First Answer on the Timing of Creation
Razi refutes the first answer via two aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that God's will was attached to a specific time for creation, by showing it leads to either infinite regress or the world's eternity.

Core Idea
Razi's refutation exposes that delaying creation by a specific time either entails a regress or forces the world to be eternal.

Imam Razi now turns to refuting the answers theologians gave for why the world was not created in eternity. The first answer claimed that God's will was attached to a specific time not yet present, thus delaying creation. Razi dismantles this from two angles, revealing that such an answer cannot coherently explain temporal creation while preserving God's free choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the overall argument. Note the two distinct aspects (wajh) Razi presents.
2
Analyze the first aspect of the refutation
Focus on the first aspect: the eternal will is either valid for multiple attachments or only one. Trace the consequences of each possibility.
Look for the terms 'ṣāliḥa' (valid) and 'tarjīḥ' (preponderance).
3
Analyze the second aspect of the refutation
Now examine the second aspect: is God's will conditioned by a specific time or not? Follow the branching logic to see how both options lead to problems.
Notice the concept of 'sharṭ' (condition) and its role.
4
Understand why the first answer fails
Reflect on why both aspects lead to an impossibility (regress) or an unwanted conclusion (eternity of the world). This shows the first answer is not a valid defense of creation in time.
The refutation is strong because it forces the opponent into a dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنا بعد هذا نبطل كل واحد من تلك الأجوبة على سبيل التفصيل : أما الجواب الأول : فهو ضعيف من وجهي : الأول : أن الإرادة القديمة المتعلقة بإيجاد العالم في الوقت المعين إما أن تكون صالحة التعلق بإيجاد العالم في وقت آخر، وإما أن لا تكون صالحة لذلك، فإن كان الأول كانت الإرادة صالحة لإيجاد العالم في هذا الوقت، وصالحة للتعلق بإيجاد العالم في سائر الأوقات، فترجيح بعض التعلقات على ما سواه، إن لم يفتقر إلى فقد المرجح ترجح الممكن لا عن مرجح وإن افتقر إلى المرجح كان الكلام فيه كما في الأول، ويلزم التسلسل، وإن كان الحق هو القسم الثاني، وهو أن تلك الإرادة المتعلقة بإيجاد العالم في ذلك الوقت ما كانت صالحة للتعلق بإيجاد العالم في وقت آخر فحينئذ لا يكون هذا المؤثر فاعلا مختاراً ، بل مؤثر موجبًا بالذات، وإذا كان موجبا امتنع تخلف المعلول عنه ، فهذا يقتضي القول بقدم العالم لا بحدوثه. والوجه الثاني : أن تعلق إرادة الله بإيجاد العالم إما أن لا يكون مشروطًا بوقت معين، وإما أن يكون مشروطًا بوقت معين فإن لم يكن مشروطًا بوقت معين فهذا معناه أنه تعالى في الأزل أراد إيجاد العالم من غير أن تكون تلك الإرادة مختصة بوقت معين وإذا كانت الإرادة غير مقيدة بوقت معين البتة وما كانت مشروطة بشرط فائت البتة وتتب حصول المراد مع هذه الإرادة، فكان التقدم لازما. وأما إن كانت الإرادة مشروطة بوقت معين مثل أن يقال إنه تعالى أراد إيجاد العالم في وقت معين فذلك الوقت إن كان حاضرًا في الأزل لزم حصول ذلك المراد في الأزل، وإن لم يكن حاضرًا في الأزل كان حادثاً ، فحينئذ ينتقل الكلام إلى كيفية إرادته لإحداث ذلك الوقت فإن كانت تلك الإرادة مشروطة بوقت آخر لزم التسلسل، وهو محال.
Then we, after this, refute each one of those answers in detail: As for the first answer: it is weak from two aspects: First: that the eternal will (al-irāda al-qadīma) that is attached to bringing the world into existence at a specific time is either valid for attachment to bringing the world into existence at another time, or it is not valid for that. If [it is] the first [case], then the will is valid for bringing the world into existence at this time, and valid for attachment to bringing the world into existence at other times, so preferring some attachments over others, if it does not depend on a preponderator, then a possible thing is preponderated without a preponderator, and if it depends on a preponderator, then the discussion about it is as in the first [case], and infinite regress (al-tasalsul) follows. And if the correct [view] is the second division, namely that that will attached to bringing the world into existence at that time was not valid for attachment to bringing the world into existence at another time, then this efficient cause (al-muʾaththir) is not a free-choosing agent (fāʿilan mukhtāran), but rather an efficient cause necessitating by essence (mūjiban bi-l-dhāt), and if it is necessitating, then the failure of the effect to occur from it is impossible, so this necessitates the assertion of the world's eternity, not its temporal origination (ḥudūth). The second aspect: that the attachment of God's will to bringing the world into existence is either not conditioned by a specific time, or it is conditioned by a specific time. If it is not conditioned by a specific time, then its meaning is that He Most High in eternity willed the existence of the world without that will being specific to a specific time, and if the will is in no way restricted to a specific time and was in no way conditional upon a missing condition, then the occurrence of the desired object follows from this will, so priority (al-taqaddum) would be necessary. And if the will is conditioned by a specific time, such as it being said that He Most High willed to bring the world into existence at a specific time, then that time, if it was present in eternity, then the occurrence of that desired object in eternity would be necessary, and if it was not present in eternity, it would be temporally originated (ḥādithan). Then the discussion transfers to how His will causes that time to come to be; if that will is conditioned by another time, then infinite regress follows, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the first theological answer, which claimed that God's will was attached to creating the world at a specific time, and that time was not present in eternity, so creation was delayed. Imam Razi refutes this from two aspects. The first aspect considers whether the eternal will is capable of being attached to different times or only to that specific time. If it is capable of multiple attachments, then preferring one time over others either requires no preponderator (leading to preponderance without a cause) or requires a preponderator, leading to infinite regress. If the will is incapable of attaching to other times, then God is not a free agent but a necessitating cause, implying the world must be eternal, not created in time. The second aspect examines whether God's will is conditioned by a specific time. If it is not conditioned, then the will in eternity should have immediately produced the world, making the world eternal. If it is conditioned by a specific time, then either that time is eternal (making the world eternal) or it is originated, leading to an infinite regress of conditions. Thus, the first answer fails because it either entails an impossibility (regress) or leads to the very conclusion (eternity of the world) that the theologians were trying to refute.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the first answer is a valid explanation for why the world was not created in eternity, i.e., that God's will attached to a future time is sufficient to explain delay.
Correction: Razi shows that this answer either forces an infinite regress of preponderators/conditions or implies that God is a necessitating cause, which actually entails the world's eternity rather than its temporal origination.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, what follows if the eternal will is valid for attachment to creating the world at multiple times?
A) God would have created the world at all times simultaneously.
B) The preference of one time over another requires either a preponderator without a cause or leads to infinite regress.
C) The will would be invalid for attachment to any time.
D) The world would be created at the first possible moment.

Key Takeaway
The first theological answer on the timing of creation fails because it either requires an infinite regress or contradicts the very claim of temporal origination by implying the world's eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's will, do you subconsciously imagine it as a mechanism that acts by necessity, or do you truly believe He chooses freely without any constraint? How does your daily life—especially in moments of frustration or delay—reveal which belief you actually hold? Stop and ask yourself: do I treat my own choices as genuine acts of will, or do I suspect they are predetermined? What one step can you take today to live in light of true tawhid regarding divine and human will?
?
Razi shows that thinking about God's will can lead to either an infinite regress or the conclusion that the world is eternal. Does your understanding of God's decree (qadr) fall into any trap that makes God's will seem conditional or mechanistic? Examine your heart: when you pray for something that does not happen, do you feel that God's will is arbitrary, or do you trust that His wisdom is beyond your comprehension? Write down one area where you need to deepen your acceptance of God's absolute sovereignty.
?
This lesson uses logical arguments to defend the Islamic creed. But is your faith built on intellectual conviction alone, or does it also penetrate your soul? Ask yourself: if you were presented with a logical objection to your belief in God's will, would you be able to respond as clearly as Razi does? Or would you rely on comfortable slogans? Resolve today to study your creed with the same rigor that Razi used, so that your faith is fortified against doubts.

Refutation of the Third Answer: The Expediency Argument Cannot Avoid Regress
Razi refutes the third answer about a time-specific expediency, showing regress or necessitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that a time-specific expediency explains why the world was created at a particular time, by showing that it leads to infinite regress or determinism.

Core Idea
Appealing to a purpose at a specific time merely shifts the question and leads to regress.

Imam Razi continues his critique of the arguments for creation at a specific time. The third answer suggests that God chose that time because it contained a special expediency (maslaha) not present at other times. Razi shows this answer is weak from two angles: first, regarding the nature of the expediency itself; second, regarding the possibility of refraining from creation given knowledge of the expediency. Both angles reveal that this answer cannot escape infinite regress or the denial of free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its full English translation. Note that the passage breaks off mid-sentence at the end, but the main arguments are clear.
The passage is from Imam Razi's al-Arba'in fi Usul al-Din, a work on Islamic theology.
2
Analyze the first argument
Focus on the first argument (الأول). Razi examines the expediency: is it eternal or temporal? If eternal, the world should have been created earlier; if temporal, its occurrence is either necessary or possible. Both lead to absurdities: necessary occurrence implies self-causation (denial of the Maker), possible occurrence requires another specifier, leading to regress.
This argument effectively dismantles the idea that the expediency itself can be the reason for the specific time.
3
Analyze the second argument (incomplete)
Examine the second argument (الثاني). Razi considers whether, given knowledge of the expediency, refraining from creation is possible. If not, God is necessitated (not free). If possible, then both acting and refraining are possible, requiring a preponderator or leading to regress. The argument cuts off, but the pattern shows the same regress problem.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the direction is clear; the full argument would continue along similar lines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب الثالث وهو أن حدوث العالم إنما اختص بذلك الوقت لاختصاص ذلك الوقت بمصلحة لا توجد في سائر الأوقات، فهو أيضًا ضعيف من وجهين : الأول : أن حدوث العالم لما توقف على حضور ذلك الوقت المختص بتلك المصلحة، فحدوث ذلك الوقت إن كان لمحدث فهو نفي الصانع، وإن كان لمحدث فالسؤال عائد في أنه لم لم يحدث قبل ذلك الوقت أو بعده؟ ويلزم التسلسل، وأيضا فالمصلحة الحاصلة في ذلك الوقت إن كانت حاصلة قبل ذلك الوقت فما لأجله حدث العالم كان حاصلا قبل ذلك الوقت فيلزم أن يحدث العالم قبل أن حدث، وإنه محال، وإن قلنا : إن تلك المصلحة ما كانت حاصلة قبل ذلك الوقت، وإنما حصلت في ذلك الوقت فإما أن يقال: إنه حدثت تلك المصلحة في ذلك الوقت، مع وجوب أن تحدث أو مع جواز أن تحدث فإن كان الأول فقد جوزتم أن يحدث الشيء لذاته، وإذا جاز ذلك في بعض الأمور، جاز مثله في كلها، ويلزم نفي الصانع، وهو محال وإن كان الثاني افتقر اختصاص ذلك الوقت بتلك المصلحة إلى مخصص آخر، والكلام فيه كما في الأول، ويلزم التسلسل، وهو محال أيضًا. والثاني : وهو أن مع العلم باشتمال ذلك الوقت على تلك المصلحة إما أن يكون الترك ممكنا أو لا يكون فإن لم يكن ممكنا كان المؤثر موجبا بالذات، لا فاعلا بالاختيار، وإن كان الترك ممكنا فلما كان الترك والفعل ممكنين مع ذلك الداعي، فإن توقف على مرجح آخر كان الكلام فيه كمنا في الأول، ويلزم التسلسل، وإن لم يتوقف كان الممكن واقعا لا عن مرجح. لا يقال : الفعل مع هذا الداعي يصير أولى بالوقوع، ولا تنتهي الأولوية إلى
As for the third answer, which is that the temporal origination of the world was specific to that time because that time is specific to an expediency not found in other times, it is also weak from two aspects: The first: that since the temporal origination of the world depends on the presence of that time specific to that expediency, then the temporal origination of that time: if it has no originator, that is denial of the Maker; and if it has an originator, the question returns as to why it did not occur before that time or after it? And infinite regress follows. Also, the expediency present in that time, if it was present before that time, then the reason for which the world came to be was present before that time, so it would necessitate that the world occurred before it occurred, and that is impossible. And if we say that that expediency was not present before that time, but rather occurred at that time, then either we say that that expediency occurred at that time necessarily or possibly. If the first, then you have allowed that something occurs by itself; and if that is allowed in some things, the like is allowed in everything, and denial of the Maker follows, and that is impossible. If the second, then the specificity of that time to that expediency depends on another specifier, and the discussion regarding it is as in the first, and infinite regress follows, and that is also impossible. The second: that with the knowledge that that time contains that expediency, either refraining [from creation] is possible or not. If it is not possible, then the effecting agent is necessitated by essence, not a free agent. If refraining is possible, since refraining and acting are both possible with that motive, then if it depends on another preponderator, the discussion is as in the first, and infinite regress follows; and if it does not depend, then the possible would occur without a preponderator. It cannot be said: the act with this motive becomes more appropriate for occurrence, and the appropriateness does not end...
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage refutes the third answer, which claimed that the specific time for creation was chosen because it contained a special expediency (maslaha). Razi gives two arguments. First: The expediency itself is either eternal or temporal. If eternal, the world should have been created eternally. If temporal, then either it occurred necessarily (which would mean it exists without cause, leading to denial of the Maker) or possibly, which requires a cause, leading to regress. Second: Given the knowledge of the expediency, either it is possible for God not to create at that time or not. If not possible, God is necessitated. If possible, then both acting and refraining are possible, and a preponderator is needed, leading to regress, or else the act occurs without preponderator (chance). The argument cuts off mid-sentence, but the pattern is clear: the expediency answer cannot avoid infinite regress or determinism.

Key Terms
المصلحة (expediency)
المخصص (specifier)
الداعي (motive)
الأولوية (appropriateness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that appealing to a divine purpose or wisdom (like a special expediency at a specific time) solves the problem of why God created when He did.
Correction: Razi shows that this merely shifts the question: the expediency itself requires explanation, leading to infinite regress or necessitarianism, thus failing to preserve divine free will.

Checkpoint
What follows if the expediency at the specific time is said to have occurred necessarily at that time?
A) It implies the expediency is eternal
B) It leads to infinite regress
C) It allows that something occurs by itself, leading to denial of the Maker
D) It proves the world is temporal

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that the 'time-specific expediency' answer merely postpones the problem and inevitably leads to infinite regress or necessitarianism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that appealing to a 'special purpose' for God's actions leads to regress. In your own life, where do you use vague appeals to 'wisdom' to avoid thinking about hard questions? What would it look like to truly follow Razi's method and demand logical consistency from your own beliefs?
?
This lesson reveals that even the most pious-sounding excuses (like 'Allah knows best') can be logically insufficient if used as a final answer. Are you content with superficial explanations in your faith, or are you willing to dig deeper? Commit to studying one aspect of aqeedah this week that you have been avoiding.
?
Razi forces you to see that unresolved questions about divine action cannot simply be dismissed by saying 'there is a wisdom.' Where in your life have you accepted easy answers instead of pushing for rigorous understanding? How will you change your approach to seeking knowledge after this?

Refutation of Pre-Eternity as a Hindrance to Creation
Al-Razi refutes the fourth answer with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two arguments by Imam al-Razi that refute the claim that pre-eternity prevents God from creating the world temporally.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity cannot be a real hindrance because it is either necessary or contingent, and non-existence is uniform.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi tackles a sophisticated objection from the philosophers: that the pre-eternity of God's existence would prevent Him from creating the world at a specific moment. Al-Razi dismantles this claim with two incisive arguments. The first examines the ontological status of pre-eternity itself, while the second points out the uniformity of pure non-existence. Let's see how he turns the tables.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal translation. Notice how Al-Razi structures his refutation with two distinct points.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Analyze the first argument
Identify the dilemma: pre-eternity (al-azal) is either necessary or contingent. Trace the consequences of each possibility and see how both lead to a contradiction or infinite regress.
This is a classic dialectical argument.
3
Analyze the second argument
Consider the claim that non-existence (al-'adam) is pure negation. Why does this make it impossible for pre-eternity to be a hindrance? Reflect on the uniformity of pure non-existence.
This argument relies on the nature of non-existence.
4
Formulate the overall refutation
Combine both arguments to see how Al-Razi completely undermines the philosopher's fourth answer. Ask yourself: why is this refutation effective?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب الرابع: وهو أن حضور الأزل كان مانعا من الإيجاد، فهو أيضا ضعيف من وجهين : الأول : أن مسمى الأزل الذي جعلتموه مانعا من الإيجاد، إما أن يكون واجبًا لذاته أو ممكنا لذاته فإن كان الأول امتنع زواله وكان يجب أن لا يزول ذلك الامتناع، هذا خلف وإن كان ممكنا لذاته فلا بد لحصوله من سبب والكلام فيه كما في الأول، ولا ينقطع حتى ينتهي إلى الواجب لذاته، وحينئذ يعود المحذور المذكور. والثاني : أن عدم العالم في الأزل إلى وقت حدوثه، نفي محض، والنفي المحض لا يكون فيه اختلاف وامتياز فامتنع القول بأن ذلك العدم في الأزل مانع من الفعل، وفي لا يزال غير مانع منه.
As for the fourth answer — namely that the presence of pre-eternity was a hinderance to bringing into existence — it is also weak in two respects. First: the designation of pre-eternity which you have made a hinderance to bringing into existence is either necessary in itself or possible in itself. If it is the first, then its removal is impossible, and it would be necessary that that impossibility not cease, which is a contradiction. And if it is possible in itself, then its occurrence necessarily requires a cause, and the discussion about it is as in the first, and it does not terminate until it reaches that which is necessary in itself, and at that point the aforementioned difficulty returns. Second: the non-existence of the world in pre-eternity up to the time of its occurrence is pure non-existence, and pure non-existence does not admit of differentiation and distinction; so it is impossible to say that that non-existence in pre-eternity is a hinderance to the act, while in 'not yet' it is not a hinderance.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the fourth answer that the philosopher gives to the problem of creation. The answer claims that the presence of pre-eternity prevents God from creating the world from eternity. Al-Razi shows two weaknesses. First, he considers the ontological status of pre-eternity (al-azal): if it is necessary in itself, then it cannot cease, so the creation would remain impossible forever; if it is contingent, then it requires a cause, leading to an infinite regress. Second, he argues that non-existence is a pure negation and does not have different states; therefore, it cannot be that the same non-existence is a hinderance in pre-eternity but not after. Thus the answer is invalid.

Key Terms
الأزل
وجوب
إمكان
نفي محض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that pre-eternity is a positive attribute that can act as a barrier, but al-Razi shows it is either necessary or contingent, and pure non-existence is uniform.
Correction: Pre-eternity is not a positive entity; it is either a necessary attribute (which cannot be removed) or a contingent concept (which requires a cause). Pure non-existence has no degrees, so it cannot be a variable hindrance.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's first objection to the fourth answer, if pre-eternity (al-azal) is necessary in itself, what follows?
A) It can cease to exist and become contingent.
B) Its removal is impossible, so the impossibility of creation would never cease.
C) It requires a cause to exist.
D) It is a pure negation with no effect.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi shows that pre-eternity cannot be a genuine hindrance because it is not a positive attribute, and non-existence has no degrees.

?

Reflect on a time you thought a particular problem in your life was permanent because it had been there for a long time. How does al-Razi's refutation of pre-eternity as a hindrance inspire you to reconsider? Write down one concrete action you can take now.

The philosophers argued that pre-eternity prevented God from creating earlier. Do you ever delay obeying Allah thinking 'later'? What does this lesson teach you about the fallacy of waiting for the 'right time'? What is one act of obedience you have been putting off that you can start today?

Refuting the Claim that the World Became Possible Only at Creation
Al-Razi refutes the claim that the world was not possible before existence using two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi argues that the world's possibility must be eternal and cannot arise only at the moment of creation.

Core Idea
Possibility is an essential property, not something that changes over time.

In this passage, al-Razi tackles a creative objection: that the world was not possible before it was created. He finds this idea deeply flawed. Using two quick arguments, he shows that it leads to absurd consequences and that possibility must be an eternal feature of a thing's essence.

Guided Path
1
Context of the fifth answer
The fifth answer attempts to avoid the problem of pre-eternal possibility by denying that the world was possible before existence. Recognise this as an attempt to escape the need for a pre-eternal cause.
2
First objection: change of realities
Al-Razi argues that if the world was impossible in itself and then became possible, that would mean its essence changed from impossible to possible, which is a change in the very nature of the thing. Such change of realities is absurd.
This is based on the principle that essential properties cannot change.
3
Second objection: possibility is essential and eternal
The second objection argues that the essence (mahiyya) does not differ over time. If it is receptive to existence at some time, then it is always receptive. Therefore possibility cannot be restricted to a particular time.
This is a strong argument that possibility is immutable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب الخامس: وهو أن العالم ما كان ممكن الوجود قبل أن وجد فهو أيضًا ضعيف من وجهين : الأول : أن هذا يقتضي أن يقال : إنه كان ممتنع الوجود لذاته، ثم انقلب ممكنا لذاته، وهذا يقتضي انقلاب الحقائق، وهو محال. هي لا تختلف، فإن ثبت كونها قابلة للوجود في بعض الأوقات، ثبت أنها من حيث هي هي قابلة للوجود أبداً فكانت ممكنة أبدا فيمتنع أن يكون حصول ذلك الإمكان مخصوصا بوقت دون وقت.
As for the fifth answer — namely that the world was not possible of existence before it existed — it is also weak in two respects. First: this would entail that it be said that it was impossible in itself, then it became possible in itself, and this entails a change of realities, which is absurd. The essence (al-mahiyya) does not differ; for if it is established that it is receptive to existence at some times, it is established that in itself it is receptive to existence forever, so it was possible forever, so it is impossible that the occurrence of that possibility be specific to a time without another time.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage refutes the fifth answer, which claims that the world was not possible before it existed. Al-Razi presents two weaknesses. First, if the world was impossible in itself before existence, and then became possible, that would entail a change in the very essence of the world — impossible things cannot become possible without a change in reality (inqilāb al-ḥaqā'iq), which is absurd. Second, he argues that the nature of a thing (al-māhiyya) does not change; if it is capable of existence at some time, then it is capable of existence at all times, because possibility is an essential property. Therefore, possibility cannot be restricted to a particular moment. Thus the answer is invalid.

Key Terms
ممكن
ممتنع
انقلاب الحقائق
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is a relational attribute that can change over time.
Correction: Possibility is an essential property of the essence itself and does not change; it is eternal.

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi argue about the essence (al-mahiyya) regarding possibility?
A) It can change from impossible to possible over time.
B) It is the same at all times, so if it is possible at one time, it is possible always.
C) It is immaterial and does not affect possibility.
D) It is a creation of God and depends on His will.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of a thing is an essential property of its essence, not a temporal accident, so it cannot come into being at the moment of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where you think something is impossible for you, do you remember that possibility is an essential attribute? How does that change your view of your own limitations? Will you now examine whether your 'impossibility' is based on temporary circumstances or on a true essential impossibility?
?
Al-Razi argues that God's creative act does not alter the essence of the world. Does this challenge your assumption that God's power can change anything arbitrarily? How does this affect your trust in God's unchanging nature?
?
The claim that the world became possible only at creation implies that possibility is time-bound. Do you ever treat your own capabilities as time-bound, assuming you could not have done something before a certain event? Reflect on one past event where you said 'I could not have done that' — is that assessment based on a change in your essence or only on external factors? What does this lesson teach you about the constancy of your inherent capacities?

Refuting Preference Without a Cause
Al-Razi refutes the sixth answer with two arguments and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's two arguments against the claim that a free agent can choose between equally possible actions without any cause or preferrer.

Core Idea
Preference without a cause reduces choice to chance and destroys agency.

Al-Razi now tackles one of the most powerful philosophical doubts: that a free agent can simply prefer one action over another without any reason. He argues that this would make the action coincidental rather than chosen, and would ultimately undermine the very concept of an agent causing an action. In this passage, he gives two clear arguments to expose the flaw, using both logical analysis and everyday examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice that al-Razi presents two distinct 'faces' (wajhayn) of refutation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the first argument: choice vs. coincidence
If both options are equal and one occurs without cause, that is mere chance (ittifaq), not choice (ikhtiyar). If chance is allowed, all events could be independent of causes.
3
Analyze the second argument: the need for a preferrer
Either the agent needs something to tip the scale or not. If yes, that preferrer itself requires explanation (regress). If no, the act occurs without the agent's effect, negating agency.
4
Examine the examples of the fleeing person and thirsty one
Al-Razi uses these to show that in real life, an internal desire (irada) is necessary for preference. Without it, no preference occurs.
5
Grasp the conclusion
Preference without a cause is either impossible or leads to the negation of all causality, including the existence of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب السادس: وهو قولهم: القادر المختار يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر من غير مرجح فهو أيضًا ضعيف من وجهين: الأول: أنه لما استوى الأمران بالنسبة إليه ثم وقع أحدهما دون الآخر كان وقوع ذلك الفعل اتفاقيًا لا اختيارياً، وإن جاز ذلك فليجز مثله في سائر الحوادث، وذلك يقتضي استغناء الحدوث عن المرجح. الثاني: وهو أن القادر على الفعل والترك إذا استوى الفعل والترك بالنسبة إليه فإما أن يقال: إنه لا يرجح أحد الطرفين على الآخر إلا إذا خص ذلك القادر ذلك الطرف بنوع ترجيح أو يقال: إنه يرجح أحد الطرفين على الآخر، وإن لم يخص ذلك القادر ذلك الطرف بنوع ترجيح، فإن كان الأول فحينئذ يتوقف رجحان ذلك الطرف على الطرف الآخر على انضياف ذلك المرجح إليه، وحينئذ يعود الإشكال من الرأس، أن ذلك المرجح هل كان حاصلا في الأزل أو ما كان حاصلا في الأزل؟ فإن كان الحق هو الثاني كان معناه أنه يترجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر، من غير أن يكون ذلك الرجحان لسبب ترجيحه وحينئذ لا يكون ذلك الفعل واقعا بإيقاعه، بل يكون واقعا من غير سبب، وذلك يقتضي استغناء الفعل من الفاعل. وبهذا الحرف يظهر ضعف كلامهم في التمثيل بالهارب من السبع، إذا عَنَّ له طريقان، وبالعطشان إذا خُيّر بين شرب قدحين؛ لأن في هذه الصورة يعلم كل أحد أنه ما لم يحضر في قلبه إرادة الذهاب في أحد الطريقين وإرادة أحد القدحين، فإنه لا يترجح ذلك الطريق على الثاني، ولا يترجح ذلك القدح على الثاني، فظهر بهذا أن حصول الترجيح من غير المرجح إما أن يكون محالاً وإما أن يلزم منه نفي المؤثرات والتأثيرات، وذلك يوجب نفي الصانع بالكلية، وهو باطل. فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذه الشبهة وهي في الحقيقة أعظم شبه الفلاسفة في هذه المسألة.
As for the sixth answer — namely their statement that the free agent can prefer one of his two objects of power over the other without a preferrer — it is also weak in two respects. First: when the two things are equal with respect to him and then one of them occurs without the other, the occurrence of that act is by coincidence not by choice, and if that is permissible, then let the like be permissible in all contingent events, and that would entail that temporal origination is independent of the cause (al-murajjih). Second: it is that the agent capable of action and inaction — if action and inaction are equal with respect to him — then either it is said that he does not prefer one of the two sides over the other unless that agent specifies that side with some kind of preference, or it is said that he does prefer one side over the other even if that agent has not specified that side with some kind of preference. If it is the first, then the preference of that side over the other depends on the addition of that preferrer to it, and then the difficulty returns from the beginning: that preferrer, was it present in pre-eternity or not? If the truth is the second, then its meaning is that one of his two objects of power becomes preferable over the other without that preference being due to a cause for its being preferred, and then that act does not occur by his effecting it, but rather occurs without a cause, and that entails that the act is independent of the agent. And with this point the weakness of their speech becomes clear in the example of the one fleeing from a lion when two roads appear before him, and of the thirsty one when he is given a choice between drinking from two cups; because in this situation everyone knows that unless there arises in his heart an intention to go on one of the two roads and an intention to take one of the two cups, that road is not preferred over the second, nor that cup over the second. So it became clear by this that the occurrence of preference without a preferrer is either impossible or necessitates the negation of all causes and effects, and that necessitates the absolute negation of the Maker, which is false. This completes the discussion in establishing this doubt, which is in reality the greatest doubt of the philosophers in this issue.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the sixth answer, which claims that a free agent can prefer one possible action over another without any cause or reason. Al-Razi shows two weaknesses. First, if both options are equal and one occurs without cause, that is by chance, not choice. If that is allowed, then all occurrences could be without cause, and the need for a cause is eliminated. Second, he analyzes the act of preference: either the agent needs something to tip the scale or not. If he needs a preferrer, then that preferrer itself must be accounted for—either it exists from eternity or not—leading to a regress. If no preferrer is needed, then the act occurs without being effected by the agent, which negates agency altogether. He then gives two examples (a fleeing person choosing a road, a thirsty person choosing a cup) to show that in reality, internal desire is needed for preference. Therefore, preference without a cause is impossible or leads to atheism. This concludes the discussion of this doubt, which is considered the greatest philosophical doubt in this matter.

Key Terms
قادر مختار
ترجيح بلا مرجح
اتفاق
اختيار
مرجح
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A free agent can choose arbitrarily between equally possible actions without any reason or cause.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that such 'free' choice would be coincidental, not volitional, and would ultimately undermine causality and the existence of an agent.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what would happen if a free agent could prefer one action over another without a preferrer (murajjih)?
A) It would prove the agent's perfect freedom.
B) It would lead to the negation of all causes and effects, and ultimately God.
C) It would affirm that the action occurs by the agent's will.
D) It would show that actions can occur without an agent.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi demonstrates that preference without a cause is logically untenable, as it either reduces choice to chance or destroys the very concept of agency, ultimately pointing to the necessity of a cause for all contingent actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent decision you made without much thought. Was it truly a choice, or was it just happening to you? How does al-Razi's argument that preference without a cause reduces to coincidence challenge the way you view your own decisions?
?
When you say 'I just felt like it' as a reason for an action, are you admitting that your actions lack real meaning? In light of this lesson, what would it mean to take responsibility for every preference you act on?
?
If your choices were uncaused, would you still be accountable to Allah? How does the necessity of a cause for every preference affect your understanding of taqwa (consciousness of Allah) in your daily life?

The Trilemma of Origination: Why the World Must be Possible
Imam Razi presents the first part of the third philosophical objection: the trilemma of pre-existence and the positive nature of possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' trilemma that anything originated before its existence must be either necessary, impossible, or possible, and why possibility is a positive attribute.

Core Idea
Before existence, only possibility makes sense; necessity and impossibility are contradictions.

The philosophers argue that if the world is originated (muhdath), then before its existence it must fall into one of three categories: necessary, impossible, or possible. They systematically eliminate the first two, leaving only possibility. Then they probe the nature of possibility itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the trilemma
Read the opening lines where the philosophers propose the three options: necessary, impossible, possible. Note why necessity and impossibility are rejected.
2
Understand the rejection of necessary and impossible
The passage says if the world were necessary before existence, it would already exist. If impossible, it cannot become possible. So only possible remains.
3
Explore the nature of possibility
After establishing that the world was possible, the philosophers ask whether possibility is negative or positive. They argue it is positive.
This is a key move: possibility is not just a lack but a real attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة لهم : قالوا : العالم لو كان محدثًا لكان قبل حدوثه إما أن يكون ممكنا أو واجباً، أو ممتنعاً فإن كان واجبًا كان قبل وجوده موجودًا، وهذا محال، وإن كان ممتنعا ثم انقلب ممكنا لزم أن ينقلب الممتنع لذاته، ممكنا لذاته، وهو محال، ولما بطل القسمان لم يبق إلا الثالث، وهو أن العالم قبل حدوثه كان ممكن الوجود، فنقول : الإمكان إما أن يكون صفة سلبية أو ثبوتية والأول باطل؛ لأن الإمكان نقيض الامتناع والامتناع عدم إذ لو كان موجودًا لكان الممتنع الموصوف به أولى بأن يكون موجوداً ، فثبت أن الإمكان صفة ثابتة. فنقول: هذا الإمكان إما أن يكون عبارة عن كون القادر متمكنا من إيجاده، وإما أن يكون وصفًا راجعا إلى ذات الممكن، والأول باطل ؛ وذلك لأن القادر يمكنه إيجاد الممكنات ولا يمكنه إيجاد المحالات فلولا امتياز الممكن عن المحال بوصف عائد إليه وإلا لم يكن اقتداره على إيجاد الممكنات أولى من اقتداره على إيجاد المحالات فثبت أن كل محدث مسبوق بالإمكان وثبت أن الإمكان صفة موجودة عائدة إلى ذات الممكن. ومن المعلوم بالبداهة أن الموصوف بالصفة الموجودة لا بد وأن يكون موجودًا وذلك الشيء الذي يحصل فيه إمكان حدوث الأشياء هو المسمى بالهيولى، فإذن كل محدث فإنه مسبوق بالهيولى وثبت في أصول الحكمة أن الهيولي إما أن يكون هو
The third objection for them: They said: If the world were originated, then before its origination it must be either possible or necessary or impossible. If it were necessary, it would have been existent before its existence, and that is impossible. And if it were impossible and then became possible, it would necessitate that the impossible in itself changes into possible in itself, and that is impossible. Since the two divisions are invalid, nothing remains except the third, which is that the world before its origination was possible of existence. We say: Possibility (imkan) is either a negative attribute or a positive attribute. The first is false; because possibility is the contrary of impossibility, and impossibility is non-existence; if it were existent, the impossible described by it would be more deserving of existence. So it is established that possibility is a fixed attribute. We say: This possibility is either an expression for the Powerful being able to bring it into existence, or it is an attribute referring back to the essence of the possible. The first is false; because the Powerful can bring possible things into existence and cannot bring impossible things into existence. So were there no distinction of the possible from the impossible by an attribute returning to it, then His power to bring possible things into existence would not be more deserving than His power to bring impossible things into existence. So it is established that every originated thing is preceded by possibility, and it is established that possibility is an existing attribute returning to the essence of the possible. And it is known by intuition that that which is described by an existing attribute must be existent. And that thing in which the possibility of things occurs is called matter (hayula). Therefore, every originated thing is preceded by matter. And it is established in the principles of philosophy that matter is either...
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents the third philosophical objection against the creation of the world. The philosophers argue as follows: If the world is originated (muhdath), before its origination it must be either necessary (wajib), impossible (mumtani'), or possible (mumkin). The first two are impossible: necessary existence before existence is contradictory; impossibility turning into possibility is also impossible. So only possibility remains. Then they argue that possibility (imkan) is a positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya), not a negative one, because its opposite (impossibility) is non-existence; if possibility were negative, the impossible would be more deserving of existence. Thus possibility is a real attribute. Next, they consider whether this possibility refers to the power of the agent or to the essence of the possible itself. The first is false because if possibility were merely the agent's power, then without a distinction in the possible itself, the agent would be equally able to create impossibilities. Therefore, possibility is an attribute inhering in the essence of the possible. Since any attribute requires an existent substrate, the substrate that sustains the possibility of coming-to-be is what the philosophers call 'matter' (hayula). Hence every originated thing is preceded by matter (hayula). The passage cuts off before stating the philosophers' conclusion, but the implication is that matter is pre-eternal, leading to the pre-eternity of the world.

Key Terms
الإمكان
الامتناع
الوجوب

Correction: The philosophers argue possibility is a positive attribute that exists in the essence of the possible, not merely a mental construct.

Checkpoint

C) Because the world is contingent

Key Takeaway
The philosophers use a trilemma to argue that before existence, the world was possible, and they treat possibility as a positive attribute.

Are you treating your possibilities as mere hopes, or as real attributes that demand action?
?

What would it mean if that 'impossibility' were actually possible?

From Possibility to Matter: The Substrate of Potentiality
Imam Razi presents the argument linking possibility as an essential attribute to the existence of matter (hayula).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers argue that possibility is an attribute inhering in the essence of the possible, requiring a pre-eternal matter (hayula) as its substrate.

Core Idea
Possibility is a real attribute that demands an existent bearer, which is matter (hayula).

Having established that possibility is a positive attribute, the philosophers now ask: does this attribute refer to the power of the agent (God) or to the essence of the possible itself? They argue for the latter, and from this deduce that every originated thing is preceded by matter (hayula), a pre-eternal substrate that sustains possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument about the agent's power
Read where the philosophers consider whether possibility is just the agent's ability. They reject this because without a distinction in the possible itself, the agent could equally create impossibilities.
2
Understand the necessity of an attribute in the essence
They conclude that possibility must be an attribute returning to the essence. This is because the agent's power to create possible things is not more deserving than power to create impossible things unless the possible itself has a distinguishing property.
3
Connect to matter (hayula)
Since possibility is an existing attribute, it requires an existent subject. That subject is called matter (hayula), which is the substrate that receives possibility and is pre-eternal.
The passage cuts off but this is the core.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The third objection for them: They said: If the world were originated, then before its origination it must be either possible or necessary or impossible. If it were necessary, it would have been existent before its existence, and that is impossible. And if it were impossible and then became possible, it would necessitate that the impossible in itself changes into possible in itself, and that is impossible. Since the two divisions are invalid, nothing remains except the third, which is that the world before its origination was possible of existence. We say: Possibility (imkan) is either a negative attribute or a positive attribute. The first is false; because possibility is the contrary of impossibility, and impossibility is non-existence; if it were existent, the impossible described by it would be more deserving of existence. So it is established that possibility is a fixed attribute. We say: This possibility is either an expression for the Powerful being able to bring it into existence, or it is an attribute referring back to the essence of the possible. The first is false; because the Powerful can bring possible things into existence and cannot bring impossible things into existence. So were there no distinction of the possible from the impossible by an attribute returning to it, then His power to bring possible things into existence would not be more deserving than His power to bring impossible things into existence. So it is established that every originated thing is preceded by possibility, and it is established that possibility is an existing attribute returning to the essence of the possible. And it is known by intuition that that which is described by an existing attribute must be existent. And that thing in which the possibility of things occurs is called matter (hayula). Therefore, every originated thing is preceded by matter. And it is established in the principles of philosophy that matter is either...
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents the third philosophical objection against the creation of the world. The philosophers argue as follows: If the world is originated (muhdath), before its origination it must be either necessary (wajib), impossible (mumtani'), or possible (mumkin). The first two are impossible: necessary existence before existence is contradictory; impossibility turning into possibility is also impossible. So only possibility remains. Then they argue that possibility (imkan) is a positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya), not a negative one, because its opposite (impossibility) is non-existence; if possibility were negative, the impossible would be more deserving of existence. Thus possibility is a real attribute. Next, they consider whether this possibility refers to the power of the agent or to the essence of the possible itself. The first is false because if possibility were merely the agent's power, then without a distinction in the possible itself, the agent would be equally able to create impossibilities. Therefore, possibility is an attribute inhering in the essence of the possible. Since any attribute requires an existent substrate, the substrate that sustains the possibility of coming-to-be is what the philosophers call 'matter' (hayula). Hence every originated thing is preceded by matter (hayula). The passage cuts off before stating the philosophers' conclusion, but the implication is that matter is pre-eternal, leading to the pre-eternity of the world.

The Threefold Classification and the Positive Nature of Possibility
Enumeration of three modes and refutation of negative possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that the world must be possible before creation, and that possibility is a positive attribute, not merely a negation.

Core Idea
Possibility is not emptiness—it is a real attribute that distinguishes the possible from the impossible.

The philosophers launch their third argument for the eternity of the world by analyzing the state of the world before its existence. They force a choice among necessary, impossible, or possible existence. After eliminating the first two, they focus on possibility and argue that it cannot be a mere negation. This passage lays the groundwork for their conclusion that possibility is a real quality inherent in a pre‑existing subject.

Guided Path
1
Read and understand the trichotomy
Read the first part of the passage where the philosophers divide the world before origination into necessary, impossible, or possible. Notice how they eliminate the first two.
The argument assumes that if the world were necessary, it would have always existed; if impossible, it could never come to be.
2
Identify the two options for possibility
After establishing that the world must have been possible, the passage examines whether possibility is a negative or positive attribute. Read that part carefully.
3
Follow the refutation of negative possibility
The passage argues that possibility cannot be negative because it is the contradictory of impossibility, which is non-existence. Since a negative cannot contradict a negative, possibility must be positive.
This is a logical argument: if impossibility is non-existence, its contradictory must be something existent (positive).
4
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that possibility is a stable attribute. This sets the stage for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة لهم : قالوا : العالم لو كان محدثًا لكان قبل حدوثه إما أن يكون ممكنا أو واجباً، أو ممتنعاً فإن كان واجبًا كان قبل وجوده موجودًا، وهذا محال، وإن كان ممتنعا ثم انقلب ممكنا لزم أن ينقلب الممتنع لذاته، ممكنا لذاته، وهو محال، ولما بطل القسمان لم يبق إلا الثالث، وهو أن العالم قبل حدوثه كان ممكن الوجود، فنقول : الإمكان إما أن يكون صفة سلبية أو ثبوتية والأول باطل؛ لأن الإمكان نقيض الامتناع والامتناع عدم إذ لو كان موجودًا لكان الممتنع الموصوف به أولى بأن يكون موجوداً ، فثبت أن الإمكان صفة ثابتة.
،
فنقول: هذا الإمكان إما أن يكون عبارة عن كون القادر متمكنا من إيجاده، وإما أن يكون وصفًا راجعا إلى ذات الممكن، والأول باطل ؛ وذلك لأن القادر يمكنه إيجاد الممكنات ولا يمكنه إيجاد المحالات فلولا امتياز الممكن عن المحال بوصف عائد إليه وإلا لم يكن اقتداره على إيجاد الممكنات أولى من اقتداره على إيجاد المحالات فثبت أن كل محدث مسبوق بالإمكان وثبت أن الإمكان صفة موجودة عائدة إلى ذات الممكن.
ومن المعلوم بالبداهة أن الموصوف بالصفة الموجودة لا بد وأن يكون موجودًا وذلك الشيء الذي يحصل فيه إمكان حدوث الأشياء هو المسمى بالهيولى، فإذن كل محدث فإنه مسبوق بالهيولى وثبت في أصول الحكمة أن الهيولي إما أن يكون هو الجسم أو يكون أمرًا لا ينفك عن الجسمية، وعلى التقديرين فالقول بقدم الأجسام لازم.
The third doubt of them: They said: If the world were originated, then before its origination it would be either possible, necessary, or impossible. If it were necessary, it would exist before its existence, and this is impossible. If it were impossible and then became possible, the impossible in itself would become possible in itself, which is impossible. Since the two divisions are invalid, only the third remains: that the world before its origination was possible of existence. So we say: Possibility is either a negative or positive attribute. The first is invalid because possibility is the contradictory of impossibility, and impossibility is non-existence, for if it were existent, the impossible described by it would be more worthy of existence. So it is established that possibility is a stable attribute. Then we say: This possibility either denotes the ability of the powerful to bring it into existence, or it is an attribute pertaining to the essence of the possible. The first is invalid because the powerful can bring possible things into existence but cannot bring impossibilities into existence. If there were no distinguishing factor of the possible from the impossible by an attribute belonging to it, then his power to bring about possible things would not be more appropriate than his power to bring about impossibilities. So it is established that every originated thing is preceded by possibility, and that possibility is an existent attribute belonging to the essence of the possible. And it is known by intuition that what is described by an existent attribute must itself exist. That thing in which the possibility of the origination of things inheres is called hyle (الهيولى). Therefore, every originated thing is preceded by hyle. And it is established in the principles of natural philosophy that hyle is either body or something inseparable from corporeality. On both hypotheses, the statement that bodies are eternal is necessary.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents the third doubt (شبهة) of the philosophers for the eternity of the world. It argues that if the world is originated (محدث), before its existence it must have been either necessary, impossible, or possible. The first two are impossible, so it was possible. Then it examines the nature of possibility (إمكان). Possibility cannot be a negative attribute because it is the contradictory of impossibility (امتناع), which is non-existence; its contradictory must be positive. Therefore, possibility is a positive attribute. Next, it asks whether this possibility refers to God's power or to the essence of the possible object. It rejects the first because God's power cannot account for the distinction between the possible and the impossible without an attribute in the object. Thus possibility is an attribute inhering in the essence of the possible. Since an attribute requires a subject that exists, there must be an existent subject that bears the possibility of the world before its origination. This subject is called hyle (الهيولى), which is either body or inseparable from body. Hence bodies are eternal, implying the eternity of the material world.

Correction: The argument shows that possibility must be a real attribute because it is the contradictory of impossibility, which is non-existence. Its contradictory must be existent, i.e., a positive attribute in reality.

Possibility is not a mere negation; it is a real positive attribute that distinguishes possible things from impossible ones.

?

?
The impossibility of something is tied to its non-existence. In your own life, when you face what seems impossible, do you remember that God alone determines what is impossible and what is possible? How can you increase your trust in His absolute power over all things?

Possibility as an Attribute: The Necessity of Pre-Eternal Matter
Argument that possibility inheres in a subject (hyle) implying eternity of bodies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers conclude that possibility must inhere in a pre‑existing subject called hyle, leading to the eternity of bodies.

Core Idea
Every possibility needs a bearer—and that bearer is eternal matter.

Having established that possibility is a positive attribute, the philosophers now ask whether it refers to God's power or to the essence of the possible object. They argue that it must be an attribute of the possible object itself, because otherwise there would be no distinction between possible and impossible for divine power. Since attributes require an existing subject, there must be a pre‑existing entity that bears the possibility of the world's existence. They identify this entity as hyle—prime matter—and conclude that bodies are eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage through
Read the entire passage once to get the flow of the argument. Notice how it builds from the nature of possibility to the necessity of a substrate.
2
Examine the two options for possibility
The passage says possibility either refers to the power of the Agent (God) or to the essence of the possible thing. Identify these two options.
3
Understand why the first option is rejected
The argument says: if possibility were just God's power, then there would be no distinction between possible and impossible for His power; He would be equally able to do all things. But we know He cannot bring about impossibilities. Therefore, the distinction must come from the object itself.
This is a key move: it forces possibility to be an attribute of the object, not just of the agent.
4
Follow the inference to hyle
Since possibility is an attribute in the possible object, and an attribute requires a subject, the subject must exist. This subject is called hyle (prime matter). The passage then notes that in philosophy, hyle is either body or inseparable from body, so bodies must be eternal.
Take note of the final conclusion: the world is eternal because matter is eternal.

The Fourth Doubt: Priority, Time, and Eternity
Philosophers argue from priority to infinite time and eternal bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that priority before existence is an existent property, leading to an infinite regress identified as time, thereby proving the eternity of time, motion, and body.

Core Idea
Priority before existence is an existent attribute, leading to an infinite regress that is time.

This fourth doubt challenges the Islamic view that the world is originated. The philosophers argue that the concept of 'priority' before existence cannot be mere non-existence, because non-existence itself does not have temporal relations. Instead, priority must be an additional existent quality. Since each such priority is itself originated, it requires another priority, and so on infinitely. This infinite series is identified as time, which must therefore be eternal. From there, they infer the eternity of motion and body.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the starting premise
The argument begins with the idea that every originated thing has its non-existence before its existence. Understand that this 'before' implies a temporal priority.
This priority (qabliyya) is not the same as the non-existence (adam) itself.
2
Distinguish priority from non-existence
Non-existence before and after are the same in essence; only priority distinguishes them. Therefore priority is an additional concept that must be something existent.
3
Follow the infinite regress
Since that existent priority is itself originated, it requires another priority before it, leading to an infinite chain. This chain is identified as time.
The argument claims this infinite regress is not vicious but constitutes time itself.
4
Connect time to motion and body
According to natural philosophy, time is an accident of motion, and motion of body. Hence eternity of time implies eternity of motion and body.
This challenges the Islamic doctrine that the universe is created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة : قالوا : كل محدث فإنه لا بد وأن يكون عدمه قبل وجوده، ولا يجوز أن تكون تلك القبلية نفس ذلك العدم ؛ لأن العدم الحاصل قبل والعدم الحاصل بعد يتشاركان في كونهما عدماً، ولا يتشاركان في معنى القبلية والبعدية، فإذن المفهوم من القبلية أمر زائد على ذات العدم، وذلك الزائد لا بد وأن يكون أمراً موجوداً، ثم ذلك الزائد محدث، فيكون مسبوقاً بقبل آخر، فإذن لكل قبل قبل لا إلى نهاية، وما ذاك إلا الزمان، فإذن الزمان قديم، وثبت في أصول الحكمة أن الزمان من لواحق الحركة والحركة من لواحق الجسم، فيلزم من قدم الزمان قدم الحركة، ومن قد الحركة قدم الجسم.
The fourth doubt: They said: Every originated thing must have its non-existence before its existence. And it is not permissible that this priority be identical to that non-existence, because the non-existence that occurs before and the non-existence that occurs after share in being non-existence, but they do not share in the meaning of priority and posteriority. Therefore, the concept of priority is something additional to the essence of non-existence. That additional thing must be something existent. Then that additional thing is originated, so it is preceded by another priority. Thus for every priority there is a prior priority ad infinitum. That is nothing but time. Therefore time is eternal. And it is established in the principles of natural philosophy that time is an accident of motion, and motion is an accident of body. So the eternity of time necessitates the eternity of motion, and the eternity of motion necessitates the eternity of body.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth doubt, arguing for the eternity of time and, by extension, of motion and body. The argument begins with the premise that every originated thing is preceded by its non-existence. However, the priority (قبلية) is not the same as the non-existence itself, because two instances of non-existence (before and after) differ only in priority and posteriority, not in being non-existence. Therefore, priority is an additional concept that must be something existent. Since each priority is originated, it requires a prior priority, leading to an infinite series of priorities. This infinite series is identified as time, which is therefore eternal. From the principles of natural philosophy, time is an accident of motion, and motion an accident of body. Hence, the eternity of time implies the eternity of motion and ultimately the eternity of body (the corporeal world).

Misconception: Priority before existence is just a relation of non-existence and not a real property.
Correction: The argument shows that two instances of non-existence (before and after) are identical in essence, so the distinction must come from an additional existent attribute: priority.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that priority before existence is an existent quality leading to an infinite regress which is time, implying the eternity of the physical world.

How does the Islamic teaching of creation ex nihilo remind you that your past is not an eternal burden but a finite slate that Allah can wipe clean?
?

Razi's Refutation of the No-Change Objection
Razi refutes the claim that his argument would imply no change in the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the objection that his cosmological argument leads to no change in the world by presenting a dilemma about the cause of a specific accident.

Core Idea
Razi turns the objection into a dilemma about the cause of change.

In this passage, Razi addresses a key objection: his argument for a necessary being seems to imply that the world is static. He first presents the objection, then a possible rejoinder from the opponent, and finally his decisive refutation. The logical moves here are subtle but crucial for understanding causal necessity in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read and understand the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Identify the initial objection: that Razi's view implies no change in the world. Notice how the objection is stated as a conditional consequence.
The objection is based on the idea that an eternal cause necessitates eternal effects, leading to a static chain.
2
Follow the opponent's rejoinder
After the objection, Razi presents a possible rejoinder: the Necessary Existence is universally effusive, but effects depend on the preparedness of recipients, which vary due to celestial motions. Understand this attempt to save change.
The opponent invokes an infinite temporal series of celestial motions to explain changing preparedness.
3
Analyze Razi's answer
Razi's answer focuses on a specific accident. He presents a dilemma: either the accident occurs without a cause (impossible) or with a cause. If the cause is temporal, we get an infinite regress at one moment (impossible). If the cause is eternal, then an eternal cause produces a temporal effect, which the opponent already accepted for the first event.
This refutes the opponent's appeal to celestial motions by showing it leads to the same problem.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why Razi's refutation is effective. The opponent cannot avoid the conclusion that an eternal cause can produce a temporal effect, so the objection against Razi's argument falls apart.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : أن نقول : إن صح ما ذكرتم يلزم أن لا يحصل في العالم شيء من التغيرات؛ لأنه يلزم من دوام واجب الوجود أزلا وأبدا دوام المعلول الأول، ومن دوام المعلول الأول دوام المعلول الثاني، وهلم جراء إلى آخر المراتب فيلزم أن لا يحصل في العالم شيء من التغيرات، وإنه خلاف الحس. فإن قال قائل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن واجب الوجود عام الفيض، إلا أن حدوث الأثر يتوقف على استعداد القوابل، وحدوث هذه الاستعدادات المختلفة في القوابل يتوقف على الحركات الفلكية والاتصالات الكوكبية وكل حادث منها مسبوق بآخر لا إلى أول، فلهذا السبب حدث التغير في هذا العالم. فالجواب : أنا بقول : العرض المعين إذا حدث في هذا العالم فإما أن يفتقر في حدوثه إلى سبب أو لا يفتقر ، فإن لم يفتقر فقد حدث الممكن لا عن سبب، وهو باطل بالاتفاق، وإن افتقر إلى سبب فذلك السبب إن كان حادثًا كان الكلام في كيفية حدوثه الأول، فيفضي إلى حدوث أسباب ومسببات لا نهاية لها دفعة واحدة، وهو محال، وإن كان السبب قديمًا فقد أسندتم إلى المؤثر القديم أثرًا محدثاً، وإذا عقلتم ذلك فلم لا يجوز في كل العالم مثل ذلك أيضًا ؟
And the answer to the first doubt: we say: If what you have mentioned is correct, then it would necessitate that no changes occur in the world at all; because from the eternity of the Necessary Existence (wājib al-wujūd) eternally past and future follows the eternity of the first effect (al-maʿlūl al-awwal), and from the eternity of the first effect follows the eternity of the second effect, and so on to the end of the ranks, so it would necessitate that no changes occur in the world at all, and that contradicts sense-perception. If someone says: Why is it not permissible to say that the Necessary Existence is universally effusive (ʿāmm al-fayḍ), except that the occurrence of the effect depends on the preparedness of the recipients (istiʿdād al-qawābil), and the occurrence of these varying preparednesses in the recipients depends on celestial motions and planetary conjunctions, and every temporal occurrence among them is preceded by another without a first, so for this reason change occurs in this world? Then the answer: we say: a specific accident (al-ʿaraḍ al-muʿayyan) when it occurs in this world: either it needs a cause for its occurrence or it does not; if it does not need a cause, then a possible thing has occurred without a cause, which is invalid by consensus; and if it needs a cause, then if that cause is temporal, the discussion about how it first occurred would arise, leading to the occurrence of causes and effects without end at one moment, which is impossible; and if the cause is eternal, then you have ascribed a temporal effect to the eternal agent; and if you understand that, then why is it not permissible for the entire world to be like that as well?
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The author first states the objection: if we accept the opponent's premise (that the Necessary Existence is eternal and necessitates eternal effects), then no change would ever occur in the world, which is false by observation. Then he presents a rejoinder from the opponent, who argues that the Necessary Existence is universally effusive, but the actual occurrence of effects depends on the preparedness of recipients, which in turn depends on celestial motions that form an infinite temporal series. The author then gives his answer: he analyzes a specific accident and poses a dilemma: either it occurs without a cause (impossible) or with a cause. If the cause is temporal, we get an infinite regress of causes and effects at a single moment (impossible). If the cause is eternal, then we have an eternal cause producing a temporal effect, which the opponent already accepted in the case of the first temporal event; so why not allow the same for all events? This refutes the opponent's attempt to avoid the conclusion of no change by appealing to celestial motions.

Key Terms
المعلول الأول
عام الفيض
استعداد القوابل
العرض المعين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Razi denies change in the world.
Correction: Razi does not deny change; he refutes the opponent's argument that his view would imply no change by showing the opponent's alternative leads to the same or worse problems.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's refutation, what happens if the cause of a specific accident is temporal?
A) The accident occurs without a cause, which is impossible.
B) It leads to an infinite regress of causes and effects at one moment, which is impossible.
C) The accident is directly caused by the Necessary Existence.
D) The accident does not occur at all.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the no-change objection by showing that the opponent's alternative leads to either an infinite regress or the very consequence they try to avoid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Razi's argument shows that even the opponent's attempt to explain change through intermediate factors ultimately fails unless they admit the Eternal Cause produces temporal effects. Does your heart actually believe that every single accident, big or small, is directly from God? If you did, how would your reaction to difficulty change right now?
?
The opponent tried to avoid the dilemma by inserting a long chain of temporal causes (celestial motions) to preserve the idea that God only produces eternal effects. Are you tempted to keep God at a distance by thinking He works through impersonal 'systems' or 'laws' rather than direct, moment-by-moment involvement? Razi destroys that comfort zone: either you have an infinite regress (no real explanation) or you accept that the Eternal directly produces the temporal. What is your heart hiding behind when it says 'this happened because of...' rather than 'God willed it'? Will you consciously start replacing that phrase in your daily speech?
?
Razi's dilemma forces a stark choice: either every event is uncaused (which is absurd) or it has a cause that is either part of an infinite regress (impossible) or directly from the Eternal. If you truly accept that the Eternal is the direct cause of every single occurrence, then your entire worldview shifts: there is no room for luck, coincidence, or autonomous nature. What would it mean for your prayer life if you woke up tomorrow convinced that each breath, each thought, each external event is a direct act of God? Would you become more grateful? More humble? More desperate? What is the one thing you can do today to live that conviction?

Refutation of Causal Efficacy as Extra Attribute
Razi proves that the efficacy of the cause is not an extra attribute to avoid regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that the cause's effectiveness cannot be an additional attribute, otherwise an infinite regress would follow.

Core Idea
If causal efficacy were an extra attribute, it would itself need a cause—leading to infinite regress.

Razi now refutes the second doubt raised against his position. The opponent claimed that the effect depends on an additional attribute of the cause. Razi shows this leads to an absurd infinite chain, thus the efficacy must be identical to the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the logical flow: if-then leading to a contradiction.
The key term 'tasalsul' means infinite regress.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on 'mu'aththiriyya' (effectiveness), 'wasf za'id' (extra attribute), and 'tasalsul' (regress). Understand their roles.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Understand the conditional argument
Razi assumes the opposite: if effectiveness were extra, it would depend on the essence, making it possible and in need of a cause. That cause's effectiveness would again be extra, leading to an infinite chain.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
See the conclusion
Since infinite regress is impossible, the original assumption is false: effectiveness is not an extra attribute but identical to the cause's essence.
This preserves divine self-sufficiency.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument supports the idea that God's attributes are not separate from His essence, avoiding multiplicity and dependence.
This is a key point in Ash'ari kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية: أنا نقول : مؤثرية المؤثر في الأثر لا يجوز أن تكون وصفا زائدا على ذات المؤثر؛ لأنه لو كان كذلك لكانت تلك الصفة مفتقرة في تحققها إلى ذات الموصوف فتكون ممكنة في ذاتها مفتقرة إلى المؤثر، فتكون مؤثرية المؤثر في ذات المؤثر زائدة عليها ويلزم التسلسل.
And the answer to the second doubt: we say: the effectiveness (muʾaththiriyya) of the cause (al-muʾaththir) upon the effect (al-athar) is not permissible to be an additional attribute (waṣf zāʾid) over the essence of the cause; because if it were so, then that attribute would depend in its actualization (taḥaqquq) on the essence of the described (dhāt al-mawṣūf), so it would be possible (mumkin) in itself, dependent on a cause (muʾaththir), then the effectiveness of the cause in the essence of the cause would be additional to it, and an infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. The author denies that the causal efficacy (muʾaththiriyya) of an agent is an attribute distinct from its essence. He argues by reductio: if it were an additional attribute, it would itself be a possible entity in need of a cause for its actualization. That cause would then need another attributive efficacy, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). Therefore, the efficacy must be identical with the essence of the cause. This preserves the principle that the cause is self-sufficient and does not depend on an external attribute to be effective.

Key Terms

Correction: Razi affirms effectiveness but as identical to the essence, not as an extra attribute.

Key Takeaway
