The Necessity of a Cause for Change: Razi's Refutation of Infinite Celestial Chains
Razi argues against eternal change and refutes an objection about celestial causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that every temporal event requires a cause and his refutation of the objection that an infinite series of celestial motions can explain change without a first cause.

Core Idea
Change in the world cannot be explained by an infinite causal chain.

Razi presents a fundamental challenge to those who deny a creating cause for each temporal event. He uses the fact of observable change to argue that every new accident must have a cause, and then dismantles the objection that celestial motions and planetary conjunctions could account for change without a first cause. This passage is a masterclass in causal reasoning in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of argument.
Notice that Razi starts with a dilemma: either no change or change must have a cause.
2
Identify the main claim
Razi states that if the Necessary Existence is eternal, its effects must be eternal too, implying no change. But we observe change, so there's a problem.
This sets up the objection.
3
Understand the objection
The objector says change occurs due to the varying preparedness of recipients, caused by celestial motions in an infinite chain without a first cause.
The objector tries to avoid a first cause.
4
Follow Razi's refutation
Razi analyzes a specific accident and considers three possibilities: no cause (invalid), temporal cause (leads to infinite regress), eternal cause (then why not all events?).
Each option is shown to be problematic.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that if an eternal cause can produce a temporal effect in one case, it can in all cases, so the objection fails.
This forces the objector to accept that change requires a temporal cause or that the eternal cause directly acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة في العالم شيء من التغيرات؛ لأنه يلزم من دوام واجب الوجود أزلا وأبدا دوام المعلول الأول، ومن دوام المعلول الأول دوام المعلول الثاني، وهلم جراء إلى آخر المراتب فيلزم أن لا يحصل في العالم شيء من التغيرات، وإنه خلاف الحس. فإن قال قائل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن واجب الوجود عام الفيض، إلا أن حدوث الأثر يتوقف على استعداد القوابل، وحدوث هذه الاستعدادات المختلفة في القوابل يتوقف على الحركات الفلكية والاتصالات الكوكبية وكل حادث منها مسبوق بآخر لا إلى أول، فلهذا السبب حدث التغير في هذا العالم. فالجواب : أنا بقول : العرض المعين إذا حدث في هذا العالم فإما أن يفتقر في حدوثه إلى سبب أو لا يفتقر ، فإن لم يفتقر فقد حدث الممكن لا عن سبب، وهو باطل بالاتفاق، وإن افتقر إلى سبب فذلك السبب إن كان حادثًا كان الكلام في كيفية حدوثه الأول، فيفضي إلى حدوث أسباب ومسببات لا نهاية لها دفعة واحدة، وهو محال، وإن كان السبب قديمًا فقد أسندتم إلى المؤثر القديم أثرًا محدثاً، وإذا عقلتم ذلك فلم لا يجوز في كل العالم مثل ذلك أيضًا؟
Third preliminary: In the world there are some changes because the perpetuity of the Necessary Existence (Wajib al-Wujud) eternally necessitates the perpetuity of the first effect, and the perpetuity of the first effect necessitates the perpetuity of the second effect, and so on to the last ranks, so it would follow that no change occurs in the world, which is contrary to sense. If someone says: Why is it not permissible to say that the Necessary Existence is of universal effusion, except that the occurrence of the effect depends on the preparedness of the recipients, and the occurrence of these different preparednesses in the recipients depends on the celestial motions and planetary conjunctions, and each incident from them is preceded by another without a first, and because of this change occurs in this world? The answer is: We say: The specific accident (al-'arad al-mu'ayyan) when it occurs in this world either needs a cause for its occurrence or does not need. If it does not need, then a possible thing occurred without a cause, which is invalid by agreement. If it needs a cause, then if that cause is temporal, the discussion about how it first occurred leads to the occurrence of causes and effects without end all at once, which is impossible. If the cause is eternal, then you have attributed a temporal effect to the eternal agent. And if you understand that, then why is it not permissible regarding the entire world likewise?
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a third preliminary and a refutation of an objection concerning change in the world. The argument begins with the premise that if the Necessary Being is eternal and perpetual, then the first effect must be perpetual, and so on, so no change should occur. This contradicts observable change. The objector suggests that change arises from the varying preparedness of recipients, caused by celestial motions and planetary conjunctions in an infinite chain. Razi refutes this by analyzing a specific accident: if it occurs without a cause, that is impossible; if with a temporal cause, there is an infinite regress; if with an eternal cause, then a temporal effect is directly from the eternal, which should apply to all things. The refutation forces the objector to accept that if an eternal cause can produce a temporal effect in one case, it could do so generally, collapsing the distinction.

Key Terms

معلول
عرض
سبب
قديم
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite chain of temporal causes (e.g., celestial motions) can explain the occurrence of temporal events without needing a first cause or an eternal cause directly producing temporal effects.
Correction: Razi argues that such an infinite regress is impossible because each cause requires a cause, leading to an actual infinite simultaneous series. Moreover, if an eternal cause directly produces a temporal effect is accepted in one case, it should be accepted for all events, eliminating the need for an infinite chain.

A) It assumes an infinite regress without a first cause
B) It denies the existence of change
C) It relies on the idea that the agent's efficacy is an added attribute
D) It claims that possibility is an existent attribute

Key Takeaway
Every temporal event must have a cause, and appeals to infinite causal chains fail because they either require an uncaused event or imply an eternal cause directly producing temporal effects.

Fourth Proof: Possible Things Need a Substrate
Razi gives the fourth proof that the possible requires matter and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's fourth proof that possible things require a substrate and how he refutes the objection that eternal possibles are self-subsisting.

Core Idea
Even eternal possibles need a substrate; otherwise possibility becomes a necessary attribute.

This lesson presents one of Razi's arguments for the existence of a substrate. He argues that if active intellects, human souls, and prime matter are all possible, they would need another matter to subsist in, leading to an infinite regress. However, an objection claims that eternal possibles are self-subsisting. Razi refutes this by showing that treating possibility as a real attribute leads to an absurd conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overview of the argument.
Notice the structure: proof, objection, refutation.
2
Identify the components
Identify the three parts: the initial proof, the objection, and Razi's refutation. Mark where each begins.
3
Understand key terms
Familiarize yourself with the terms 'active intellects', 'human souls', 'prime matter', 'possibility', and 'essential necessity' as used in the passage.
These are central to the argument.
4
Follow the logical chain in the refutation
Trace Razi's refutation step by step: how he shows that if possibility is a real attribute, it would necessitate the possible being, which is absurd.
This is the heart of the lesson.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument matters: it demonstrates that possibles require a substrate, supporting the existence of matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها : أن العقول الفعالة والأرواح البشرية والهيولى كلها ممكنة، فيلزم افتقارها إلى هيولى أخرى، وهو باطل. لا يقال : الممكن إنما يفتقر إلى الهيولى إذا كان محدثًا لأنه قبل حدوثه غير موجود، فلا بد له من شيء آخر يكون موجودًا قبل حدوثه، حتى يكون ذلك الشيء محلا لذلك الإمكان، أما العقل الفعال والهيولى، لما كانا موجودين أزلا ولأبدًا كان إمكانه قائما به فلا يفتقر إلى هيولى أخرى، وهو باطل. لأنا نقول : ثبوت الإمكان للماهية الممكنة يكون على سبيل الوجوب الذاتي، فإذا كان شرط قيام الإمكان به كونه في نفسه موجودًا وثبوت الإمكان له أمر واجب لذاته، وما كان شرطًا لما كان واجبًا لذاته أولى بأن يكون واجبًا لذاته، فيلزم أن يكون ثبوت الوجوب لهذه الأشياء واجبًا بالذات فيلزم أن يكون الممكن لذاته واجبًا لذاته، وهو محال.
Fourth: That the active intellects, human souls, and matter are all possible, so they must depend on another matter, which is false. It cannot be said: The possible only depends on matter if it is originated because before its origination it is non-existent, so it needs something else that exists before its origination so that that thing is a locus for that possibility. As for the active intellect and matter, since they exist eternally and forever, their possibility subsists in them and they do not need another matter, which is false. For we say: The subsistence of possibility for the possible quiddity is in the mode of essential necessity. So since the condition for the subsistence of possibility in it is that it is in itself existent, and the subsistence of possibility for it is a thing necessary in itself, and whatever is a condition for what is necessary in itself is more worthy of being necessary in itself, then it follows that the subsistence of necessity for these things is necessary in itself, so it follows that the possible in itself is necessary in itself, which is absurd.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. It presents the fourth argument that if active intellects, human souls, and prime matter are all possible, they would each need another matter to subsist in, leading to an infinite regress. The objector claims that only originated things need a substrate, but eternal possibles like intellect and matter have their possibility self-subsisting. The author refutes this by arguing that the possibility of a quiddity is an essential necessity; if the condition for that possibility is that the thing is existent in itself, then the possibility itself is necessary, and the condition for a necessary thing is even more necessary. This leads to the absurd conclusion that the possible is necessary. Therefore, the original claim that these possibles need no substrate is false.

Key Terms
العقول الفعالة
الأرواح البشرية
الهيولى
الإمكان
الوجوب الذاتي
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal possible beings do not need a substrate because their possibility is self-subsisting.
Correction: Razi shows that if possibility were a real attribute, it would lead to the possible being necessary, which is absurd. Therefore, even eternal possibles must have a substrate.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the fourth proof?
A) Eternal possibles are self-sufficient.
B) The possible would become necessary if possibility were a real attribute.
C) Matter is eternal.
D) Active intellects are necessary beings.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof demonstrates that even eternal possible beings require a substrate, as treating possibility as a real attribute leads to the absurd conclusion that the possible is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that even the active intellects and human souls are possible and need a substrate. Think of the most lofty spiritual concept you hold—do you ever treat it as if it were self-subsisting, independent of Allah? What would Razi say to that?
?
The objection claimed that eternal possibles don't need a substrate because their possibility is self-subsisting. Razi refuted it by showing that leads to the possible being necessary. Is there any created thing you consider so permanent or 'eternal' in your life that you have implicitly granted it necessity? How can the refutation help you break that illusion?
?
The argument ultimately shows that only the Necessary Being can be without a substrate. In your daily life, what is one thing you rely on as if it were a necessary foundation? How can you remind yourself that it is in fact dependent and that only Allah is the true Foundation?

Razi's Refutation of the Fourth Argument on Quiddity
Razi refutes the fourth argument by parity of reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the claim that quiddity cannot be caused by an agent, showing that the same reasoning would make existence uncaused and ultimately negate the Creator.

Core Idea
If existence cannot be caused, nothing can be caused.

In this passage, Razi tackles a sophisticated objection: that quiddity cannot be originated because that would require an agent, which is deemed impossible. His response is both clever and devastating: he turns the argument on its head, showing that the same logic would prove existence itself cannot be caused, leading to absurd consequences. By tracing through the argument and its possible countermoves, you will see how Razi defends the doctrine of creation and the role of the divine agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation once to get the overall flow. Pay attention to the structure: opponent's argument, Razi's first response, opponent's likely rejoinder, and Razi's final rebuttal.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent claims that if the quiddity were originated (mujaddada), it would have to occur by an agent. They then assert that this is impossible. Understand the reasoning behind their claim.
The opponent's premise is that occurrence by an agent is impossible for the quiddity.
3
Analyze Razi's first refutation
Razi argues that the same reasoning would apply to existence: if existence occurs by the agent, then without the agent existence would cease to be, which is absurd. Therefore the premise must be rejected.
This is a parity argument: what applies to quiddity applies to existence.
4
Consider the opponent's possible escape
The opponent might concede that existence does not occur by the agent, but claim that only the quiddity's being qualified by existence occurs by the agent. Razi then applies the same reasoning to that qualification.
Notice how Razi anticipates and blocks this move.
5
Understand the conclusion
Razi shows that if nothing—neither quiddity, nor existence, nor their qualification—occurs by the agent, then the agent has no effect, which denies the Creator. Thus the argument is unsound.
This reductio ad absurdum proves the opponent's argument fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الرابعة وهي قولهم : لو كانت الماهية متجددة لكان وقوعها بالفاعل ممكناً، لكن وقوعها بالفاعل محال. فجوابها : إن القادر كما يجعل الماهية موجودة فهو يجعل الماهية ماهية والحجة التي تمسكتم بها في امتناع وقوع الماهية بالفاعل فهي بعينها تقتضي امتناع وقوع الوجود بالفاعل، فإنه لو وقع الوجود بالفاعل لزم عند تقدير عدم ذلك الفاعل يخرج الوجود عن كونه وجودًا. وهذا محال.
فإن التزموا أن الوجود لا يقع بالفاعل وزعموا أن الواقع بالفاعل هو موصوفية الماهية بالوجود أوردنا عليهم ذلك الكلام في نفس تلك الموصوفية، وذلك يقتضي أن يقع بالفاعل لا الماهية ولا الوجود ولا موصوفية الماهية بالوجود، وذلك يوجب أن لا يكون للمؤثر أثر البتة، وهو يوجب نفي الصانع، فثبت أن الحجة ساقطة.
As for the fourth argument—namely their statement: 'If the quiddity were originated (mujaddada), then its occurrence by the agent would be possible; but its occurrence by the agent is impossible.' The answer: The powerful agent, just as He makes the quiddity existent, He also makes the quiddity [be a] quiddity. The argument you adhered to for the impossibility of the quiddity's occurrence by the agent itself necessitates the impossibility of existence's occurrence by the agent. For if existence occurs by the agent, then on the supposition of the non-existence of that agent, existence would cease being existence—and that is impossible. If they commit to [the claim] that existence does not occur by the agent, and they claim that what occurs by the agent is the quiddity's being qualified by existence, we would adduce that very speech against that being qualified itself. That entails that neither the quiddity, nor existence, nor the quiddity's being qualified by existence occurs by the agent, which entails that the effective cause has no effect at all, and that entails the denial of the Creator. So it is established that the argument is unsound.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage refutes the argument that the quiddity cannot be caused (tajaddud) because that would require it to be produced by an agent, which the opponent deems impossible. Razi's response is clever: the same reasoning would make existence impossible to be caused, because if existence depends on the agent, then if the agent were absent, existence would not be existence. But this is absurd, so the premise that occurrence by an agent is impossible must be false. Razi then considers a possible counter: the opponent might say existence is not caused, only the quiddity's being qualified by existence is caused. Razi then applies the same argument to that 'being qualified' (mawṣūfiyya), leading to an infinite regress or a denial that any effect comes from the agent—which would ultimately negate the Creator. Thus the argument is self-defeating.

Key Terms
قادر (qādir)
ماهية (māhiyya)
حدوث (ḥudūth)
فاعل (fā‘il)
وجود (wujūd)
موصوفية (mawṣūfiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent believes that the quiddity cannot be caused by an agent because that would be impossible.
Correction: Razi shows that the same reasoning would make existence itself uncaused, leading to the denial of any effect from the Creator, so the opponent's argument is self-defeating.

Checkpoint
What does Razi claim would result if the opponent insists that neither quiddity nor existence is caused by the agent?
A) The agent would have infinite power.
B) The agent would have no effect at all, negating the Creator.
C) The world would be eternal.
D) The argument would be proven correct.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the fourth argument by demonstrating that the same reasoning would render existence uncaused, leading to the denial of any effect from the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is the agent who makes you who you are—not only your existence but your very essence—are you living as if your identity and existence are sustained by Him every moment? What would change if you truly believed that without His ongoing act, you would cease to be you?
?
Razi shows that denying God's effect leads to absurdity. In what areas of your life do you subtly act as if your own actions, choices, or existence are independent of God? Confess a specific area where you need to acknowledge His agency more deeply.
?
The opponent's argument failed because it tried to limit God's power over quiddity. Do you ever place limits on what God can do in your life—perhaps thinking some part of your character or situation is beyond His reach? How would you respond to the truth that He is the agent of both your existence and your essence?

Razi’s Refutation of the Fourth Argument
Razi refutes the fourth argument by showing its logic leads to atheism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the argument that quiddity cannot originate from an agent, and why that argument ultimately leads to atheism.

Core Idea
If quiddity cannot be created, then neither can existence—denying divine action.

In this passage, Razi takes on the fourth argument of his opponents, who claim that the quiddity (essence) of things cannot be temporally generated from an agent because that would entail an impossibility. Razi turns their own reasoning against them, showing that the same logic would make existence itself uncreatable, and if they try to escape by talking about 'qualification' of quiddity by existence, they fall into a regress that negates any divine effect. This is a masterful reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi’s opening response
Notice how Razi directly answers the fourth proof by stating that the agent makes the quiddity both existent and a quiddity. Understand the parallel he draws.
2
Follow the logical trap
Razi argues that the opponents’ proof applies equally to existence. If they claim quiddity cannot come from an agent because its nature would depend, the same applies to existence. See why this is a problem.
3
See the escape and its failure
The opponents might say that what comes from the agent is the ‘qualification’ of quiddity by existence. Razi shows the same issue recurs, leading to no effect for the agent—atheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الرابعة وهي قولهم : لو كانت الماهية متجددة لكان وقوعها بالفاعل ممكناً، لكن وقوعها بالفاعل محال. فجوابها : إن القادر كما يجعل الماهية موجودة فهو يجعل الماهية ماهية والحجة التي تمسكتم بها في امتناع وقوع الماهية بالفاعل فهي بعينها تقتضي امتناع وقوع الوجود بالفاعل، فإنه لو وقع الوجود بالفاعل لزم عند تقدير عدم ذلك الفاعل يخرج الوجود عن كونه وجودًا. وهذا محال. أن لا فإن التزموا أن الوجود لا يقع بالفاعل وزعموا أن الواقع بالفاعل هو موصوفية الماهية بالوجود أوردنا عليهم ذلك الكلام في نفس تلك الموصوفية، وذلك يقتضي أن يقع بالفاعل لا الماهية ولا الوجود ولا موصوفية الماهية بالوجود، وذلك يوجب أن لا يكون للمؤثر أثر البتة، وهو يوجب نفي الصانع، فثبت أن الحجة ساقطة.
As for the fourth proof, which is their statement: if the quiddity were temporally generated, then its occurrence from the agent would be possible, but its occurrence from the agent is impossible. Its answer: the powerful (agent) just as He makes the quiddity existent, He makes the quiddity a quiddity; and the proof you have clung to regarding the impossibility of the quiddity occurring from the agent is the very same that entails the impossibility of existence occurring from the agent. For if existence occurred from the agent, it would be necessary, upon the supposition of the non-existence of that agent, that existence would cease to be existence. This is impossible. But if they commit to the view that existence does not occur from the agent, and they claim that what occurs from the agent is the qualification of the quiddity by existence, we would then bring up the same discussion regarding that qualification itself. That would entail that neither the quiddity, nor existence, nor the qualification of the quiddity by existence occurs from the agent. That would necessitate that there is no effect for the agent at all, and that necessitates the negation of the Creator. So it is established that the proof is invalid.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage refutes the fourth argument. The opponents argue that if the quiddity (essence) were temporally generated, it would have to be produced by an agent, but they claim that the quiddity's occurrence from an agent is impossible (since the quiddity is supposed to be self-subsistent). Razi responds by arguing that the same reasoning would also make existence's occurrence from an agent impossible, because if existence came from an agent, then if the agent were nonexistent, existence would cease to be existence—a contradiction. If the opponents try to avoid this by saying that existence does not come from the agent, but rather the qualification of the quiddity by existence does, then the same problem arises for that qualification. This leads to a regress and ultimately negates any effect of the agent, which amounts to atheism. Therefore, the opponents' argument is invalid.

Key Terms
ماهية
فاعل
وجود
موصوفية
صانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think quiddity cannot be created because it is an unchanging essence.
Correction: Razi shows that this logic would also prevent existence from being created, which is absurd. The opponents' argument ultimately denies God's creative action entirely.

Checkpoint
What is the ultimate consequence Razi draws from the opponents' argument if taken to its logical end?
A) Quiddities are eternal.
B) The agent has no effect, negating the Creator.
C) Existence is not created.
D) The quiddity is uncreated.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the argument that quiddity cannot originate from an agent by showing it would equally make existence uncreatable, ultimately denying God's creative power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If you believe that some aspect of your being (such as your essence or quiddity) is independent of God's creative act, then what part of your existence do you think is truly dependent on Him? How does that affect your daily reliance on God?

Razi shows that denying God's creation of quiddity leads to denying any divine effect—ultimately atheism. Is there any area of your life where you subtly attribute the 'whatness' of things—like your intelligence or achievements—to yourself rather than God? How would you realign that with this lesson?
?
When you sin, do you ever excuse yourself by thinking 'that's just the way I am' as if your quiddity is fixed and not created by God? How does Razi's argument challenge that excuse and call you to repent and seek transformation from the Creator of your very essence?

Temporal Origination is Not a Cause of the Need for a Determiner
Razi refutes views that include temporal origination as part or condition of the need for a determiner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical argument that temporal origination (huduth) cannot be a cause, part, or condition of the need for a determiner (mu'aththir).

Core Idea
Temporal origination is an effect, not a cause of the need.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi addresses a fundamental question in theology: what exactly causes something to need a determiner (creator)? He reports three different views from scholars, then rejects all those that include temporal origination as a factor. His proof is a powerful logical argument based on the impossibility of self-reference. Read carefully to see how he uses this to exclude temporal origination from the causal story.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Let the structure of the argument become clear before you analyze it.
Notice how Razi lists three views before giving his own.
2
Identify the three views
The passage begins by describing three competing views on the cause of the need for a determiner: pure possibility, possibility plus origination (as part), and possibility conditioned by origination (as condition). Identify each clearly.
3
Understand Razi's rejection
Razi states that in his view, temporal origination is not considered at all—not as full cause, not as part, not as condition. See how he categorically excludes it.
This rejection is absolute.
4
Grasp the logical proof
The proof is the core: origination is a property of existence, which is itself posterior to the need. If origination were the cause of the need, then the need would be prior to itself, which is impossible. Work through this chain of reasoning step by step.
This is an argument by self-reference (takhalluf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية : زعم كثير من مشايخ علم الأصول أن علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر هي الإمكان، ومنهم من زعم أن علة الحاجة هي مجموع الإمكان والحدوث، فيكون الحدوث على هذا القول شطر العلة، ومنهم من زعم أن الحاجة هي الإمكان بشرط الحدوث، فيكون الحدوث على هذا القول شرط العلة. وعندنا أن الحدوث غير معتبر في تحقق الحاجة، لا بأن يكون تمام العلة ولا بأن يكون شطر العلة ولا بأن يكون شرط العلة. والدليل عليه : أن الحدوث عبارة عن كون الشيء مسبوقا بالعدم صفة للوجود الذي هو متأخر عن تأثير القادر فيه، والذي هو متأخر عن احتياجه إلى القادر الذي هو علة تلك الحاجة، وعن جزء تلك العلة، وعن شرط تلك العلة، فلو جعلنا الحدوث علة للحاجة أو جزءاً منها أو شرطا لهذه العلة لزم تأخر الشيء عن نفسه بمراتب، وهو محال.
The Second Premise: Many of the masters of the science of principles claimed that the cause of the need for the determiner (mu'aththir) is possibility (imkan). Among them, some claimed that the cause of the need is the combination of possibility and temporal origination (huduth), so on this view temporal origination is part of the cause. Among them, some claimed that the need is possibility conditioned by temporal origination, so on this view temporal origination is a condition of the cause. In our view, temporal origination is not considered in the realization of the need, neither as the full cause, nor as part of the cause, nor as a condition of the cause. The proof of that: temporal origination is a description of existence (wujud) that is preceded by non-existence, and existence is posterior to the effect of the determiner in it, and it is posterior to its need for the determiner, which need is the cause of that need, and part of that cause, and condition of that cause. So if we made temporal origination the cause of the need, or part of it, or a condition of this cause, then a thing would be posterior to itself by degrees, which is impossible (muhal).
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the cause of the need for a determiner (علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر). Razi reports three views: (1) The cause is solely possibility (الإمكان). (2) The cause is the combination of possibility and temporal origination (الحدوث), making origination a part (شطر) of the cause. (3) The need is possibility conditioned by origination, making origination a condition (شرط). Razi rejects all inclusion of origination, stating it is not a factor at all. His proof is logical: origination is a property of existence—it is a description of existence as preceded by non-existence. But existence itself is posterior to the determiner's effect and posterior to the need for the determiner. Therefore, if origination were the cause, part, or condition of that need, then the need would be prior to itself, leading to self-reference (تأخر الشيء عن نفسه), which is impossible. This argument shows the importance of not confusing the temporality of existence with the modal property of possibility.

Key Terms
علة الحاجة
مؤثر
إمكان
حدوث
شطر
شرط
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because originated things need a cause, origination itself is the cause of that need.
Correction: Razi shows this leads to circularity: if origination were the cause of the need, then the need would be prior to itself, which is impossible. Origination is an effect of the need, not its cause.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's main proof that temporal origination (huduth) cannot be a cause of the need for a determiner?
A) Origination is not a real attribute of existence.
B) It would entail a thing being posterior to itself.
C) Possibility alone is sufficient for the need.
D) The determiner does not affect originated things.

Key Takeaway
Razi proves that temporal origination cannot be a cause, part, or condition of the need for a determiner because it would entail a logical self-reference (posteriority of a thing to itself).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your need for Allah is because you were created at a specific point in time, rather than because you are inherently contingent in every moment? How does Razi’s reframing challenge you to see your dependence as constant and essential, not tied to time? What change in your awareness does this demand?
?
Consider the impossibility of a thing being posterior to itself—Razi uses this to demolish any view that makes origination a cause of need. In your own life, are there ways you treat temporary attributes (like your age, health, or achievements) as conditions for your reliance on Allah, as if you only need Him when you are weak or originated? How can you remove these self-imposed conditions and live in constant recognition of your need?
?
Razi insists that the need is solely due to possibility (imkan), which is intrinsic. Do you truly believe that without any external condition, your very existence demands a Determiner? Or do you find yourself assuming that once you exist, you are self-sufficient? How can you internalize this truth so that your heart never forgets its absolute need for Allah?

6 min
Huduth Is Not a Cause of Need
Razi refutes the view that huduth is part of the cause of need for an effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that huduth (temporal origination) is not a cause of the need for an agent, and how he uses the impossibility of circular priority as proof.

Core Idea
Huduth cannot cause need—it comes after need, so circularity would result.

In this passage, Razi addresses a key debate in Islamic theology: what exactly makes a created thing need a creator? Some scholars argued that both contingency and temporal origination are needed. Razi disagrees and presents a clear argument that huduth is not a factor at all. Let's follow his reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its English translation. Pay attention to how Razi structures his argument: he first reports opposing views, then states his own, then gives a proof.
Notice the key terms: 'imkan', 'huduth', 'illa', 'shart'.
2
Identify the two opposing views
Razi mentions two views held by 'many of the leading scholars of usul'. Identify what each view says about the role of huduth in the need for an effect.
One view makes huduth 'shart al-illa' (part of the cause), the other makes it 'shart al-illa' (condition of the cause).
3
Understand Razi's proof
Razi argues that huduth cannot be a cause, part, or condition of the need because doing so would lead to a logical absurdity. Trace the steps of his proof to see why.
The key: huduth is an attribute of existence that comes after the agent's effect, so it cannot be prior to the need itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية : زعم كثير من مشايخ علم الأصول أن علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر هي من زعم أن علة الحاجة هي مجموع الإمكان والحدوث، فيكون الحدوث على هذا القول شطر العلة، ومنهم من زعم أن الحاجة هي الإمكان بشرط الحدوث، فيكون الحدوث على هذا القول شرط العلة وعندنا أن الحدوث غير معتبر في تحقق الحاجة، لا بأن يكون تمام العلة ولا بأن يكون شطر العلة ولا بأن يكون شرط العلة. والدليل عليه : أن الحدوث عبارة عن كون الشيء مسبوقا بالعدم صفة للوجود الذي هو متأخر عن تأثير القادر فيه والذي هو متأخر عن احتياجه إلى القادر الذي هو علة تلك الحاجة، وعن جزء تلك العلة، وعن شرط تلك العلة، فلو جعلنا الحدوث علة للحاجة أو جزءا من هذه العلة أو شرطا لهذه العلة لزم تأخر الشيء عن نفسه بمراتب، وهو محال.
The Second Preliminary: Many of the leading scholars of usul claimed that the cause of need for the effect is [something]. Some claimed that the cause of need is the combination of imkan and huduth, so huduth on this view is part of the cause. And some claimed that the need is imkan with the condition of huduth, so huduth on this view is a condition of the cause. And in our view, huduth is not considered in the realization of the need, neither as the complete cause, nor as part of the cause, nor as a condition of the cause. The proof for this is that huduth is the state of a thing being preceded by non-existence, an attribute of existence which is posterior to the effect of the powerful agent (al-qadir), and which is posterior to its need for the agent that is the cause of that need, and posterior to the part of that cause, and posterior to the condition of that cause. So if we made huduth the cause of the need, or part of that cause, or a condition of that cause, it would necessitate the thing being posterior to itself by degrees, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents the second preliminary, where Razi discusses the cause of the need for an effect (the agent). He reports two opposing views: some say the cause is the combination of imkan and huduth, making huduth a part (shart) of the cause; others say the cause is imkan with the condition of huduth, making huduth a condition (shart) of the cause. Razi then states his own position: huduth is not considered in the need at all—neither as the complete cause, nor as part, nor as condition. He provides a proof based on temporal priority: huduth is an attribute of existence that comes after the agent's effect and after the need itself. If huduth were the cause or part or condition of the need, then it would be prior to itself, which is absurd. Thus huduth cannot be a factor in the need for an agent.

Key Terms
العلة (cause)
الحاجة (need)
المؤثر (effect/agent)
الإمكان (imkan)
الحدوث (huduth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Huduth (temporal origination) must be a cause, part, or condition of the need for an agent.
Correction: Razi argues that huduth is not a factor at all; need is due solely to imkan (contingency).

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the correct position regarding huduth in relation to the need for an effect?
A) Huduth is the complete cause of need
B) Huduth is part of the cause of need
C) Huduth is a condition of the cause of need
D) Huduth is not considered in the need at all

Key Takeaway
Huduth is not a factor in the need for a cause; only imkan (contingency) is.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that your need for Allah is not because you are 'new' (huduth) but because you are contingent (imkan). Does your daily prayer reflect this deeper dependence, or are you relying on the fact that you were born recently? How can you cultivate a constant awareness of your contingency?
?
If huduth were a cause of need, then something that comes after your need (your temporal existence) would be the reason for that need—a contradiction. Are there aspects of your life where you mistakenly think that certain 'new' circumstances change your need for Allah? How can you align your thinking with the truth that your need is rooted in your very essence, not in time?
?
The proof shows that huduth cannot even be a condition for needing Allah. How does this free you from the false assumption that only when something is 'new' does it need Allah? Does this help you see that every moment, your existence depends on Him, even if you have been around for years? Commit to a specific action today that reminds you of your continuous need.

The Agent's Influence Extends to Essence and Existence
Razi presents the main claim and the first proof against the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the agent influences both essence and existence, and his refutation of the first objection using the infinite regress argument.

Core Idea
If the agent cannot affect essence, he cannot affect existence either.

Razi begins by rejecting the common claim that an agent only affects existence, not the essence of a thing. He points out that existence itself has an essence, so the same logic applies. Then he considers a possible response: perhaps the agent affects the 'being described' of essence by existence. He refutes this with three proofs, starting with the infinite regress argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the opening claim and the first proof.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the claim Razi refutes: that agent influences existence only. Then his counter-argument: existence has a quiddity, so if agent can't affect quiddity, can't affect existence.
3
Understand the objection
The objection: agent influences the quiddity being described by existence. Razi then begins his proof that this 'being described' cannot be a fixed entity.
Focus on the first proof about infinite regress.
4
Reflect on the first proof
The first proof shows that if 'being described' were a separate entity, it would lead to an infinite regress of qualifications.
This is a standard kalam argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة : زعم الجمهور من الفلاسفة والمعتزلة أن تأثير المؤثر إنما يكون في وجود لا في ماهيته، وهذا القول عندنا باطل؛ لأن الوجود له ماهية، فلو امتنع أن يكون للقادر تأثير في الماهية لامتنع أن يكون له تأثير في الوجود. فإن قيل : تأثير القادر في كون الماهية موصوفة بالوجود. قلنا : موصوفية الماهية بالوجود يمتنع أن يكون أمراً ثابتاً، ويدل عليه وجوه. أحدها: أن اتصاف الماهية بالوجود لو كان أمرًا ثابتا مغايرا للماهية والوجود لما كان جوهرًا مستقلا بنفسه قائما بذاته، بل كان صفة من صفات الماهية، وحينئذ يكون اتصاف تلك الماهية بتلك الصفة زائدًا على الماهية وعلى تلك الصفة، ويلزم منه التسلسل. وثانيها : أن تلك الموصوفية تكون مساوية في الوجود لسائر الموجودات ومخالفة لها في الماهية فيلزم أن يكون لاتصاف الماهية بالوجود ماهية ووجود آخر، فيكون اتصاف ماهيتها بوجودها زائدا عليه، ويعود الكلام الأول فيه، ويلزم التسلسل، وحينئذ لا يكون الموجود الواحد موجودًا واحدا ، بل يكون موجودات غير متناهية، وذلك محال. وثالثها وهو أن موصوفية الماهية بالوجود بتقدير أن يكون أمرًا مغايرا للماهية والوجود، فلا بد أن يكون له ماهية فإذا امتنع أن يكون للقادر في الماهيات امتنع أن يكون له تأثير في هذه الموصوفية، وعلى هذا التقدير لا يكون للمؤثر أثر، لا في الماهية ولا في الوجود ولا في موصوفية الماهية بالوجود وهذا نفي للتأثير والمؤثر بالكلية، وهو باطل قطعاً، بل القول الصحيح ان المؤثر كما يؤثر في جعل الماهية موجودة فكذلك يؤثر في جعل الماهية ماهية.
The Third Preliminary: The majority of the philosophers and the Mu'tazila claimed that the influence of the agent is only on existence, not on the quiddity (mahiyya). This statement is false in our view, because existence has a quiddity. So if it were impossible for the powerful agent to have influence on the quiddity, it would be impossible for him to have influence on existence. If it is said: 'The influence of the agent is on the quiddity being described by existence.' We say: The quiddity's being described by existence is impossible to be a fixed matter. Several proofs indicate this. First: If the quiddity's being qualified by existence were a fixed matter distinct from both quiddity and existence, then it would not be an independent substance subsisting by itself, but rather an attribute of the quiddity. Then the quiddity's being qualified by that attribute would be additional to both the quiddity and that attribute, leading to infinite regress. Second: That 'being described' would be equal in existence to other existents but different in quiddity; then the quiddity's being described by existence would require a quiddity and another existence, so the quiddity's being described by its existence would be additional to it, and the same argument recurs, leading to infinite regress; then one existent would not be one existent but infinite existents, which is impossible. Third: The quiddity's being described by existence, assuming it is something distinct from quiddity and existence, must have a quiddity. So if it is impossible for the agent to have influence on quiddities, it would be impossible for him to have influence on this 'being described'. On this assumption, the agent would have no effect—neither on the quiddity, nor on existence, nor on the quiddity's being described by existence. This is a complete negation of effect and agent, which is absolutely false. Rather, the correct statement is that the agent influences both in making the quiddity existent and likewise influences in making the quiddity a quiddity.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents the third preliminary, which refutes a claim by most philosophers and Mu'tazila that the agent's influence is only on the existence of a thing, not on its essence (mahiyya). Razi declares this false because existence itself has a mahiyya; if the agent cannot affect the mahiyya, then he cannot affect existence either. Then Razi considers a possible objection: perhaps the agent's influence is on the quiddity being described by existence (i.e., the state of the quiddity's having existence). He refutes this by three proofs. First, if this description (al-mawsufiyya) were a fixed entity distinct from both quiddity and existence, it would be an attribute of the quiddity, and then the quiddity's being qualified by that attribute would be an additional entity, leading to infinite regress. Second, if this description is considered as an existent, it would have its own quiddity and existence, leading to an infinite chain of descriptions, making a single existent into infinite existents. Third, this description, if distinct, would itself have a quiddity, and if the agent cannot influence quiddities, then he cannot influence this description either, resulting in no effect at all—an absurdity. Therefore, Razi concludes that the agent's influence extends both to making the quiddity existent and to making the quiddity itself as a quiddity. This affirms that the agent creates the essence as well as the existence.

Key Terms
الماهية (quiddity)
الوجود (existence)
المؤثر (agent)
الموصوفية (being described)
التسلسل (infinite regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the agent only creates the existence of a thing, while its essence is uncreated or pre-existing.
Correction: Razi argues that if the agent cannot affect the essence, it cannot affect existence either, because existence itself has a quiddity. Therefore, the agent must create both.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is the claim that the agent influences only existence false?
A) Because existence is identical to essence
B) Because existence has a quiddity, so if the agent cannot affect quiddity, it cannot affect existence
C) Because the agent's power is limited to accidents
D) Because the objection proves the opposite

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that the agent creates both the essence and existence of a thing, refuting the view that only existence is created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that the agent creates both your essence and your existence. That means your very nature—your personality, your inclinations, your deepest desires—is entirely dependent on Allah's creative act. Are you living as if your character is fixed and unchangeable, or do you truly believe that Allah can transform your essence? What specific aspect of your character will you plead with Him to change today?
?
Razi's proof shows that even the 'being described by existence' is not a separate thing—there is no intermediate layer between Allah's creation and you. Does this confront your tendency to think you have some independent 'self' apart from Allah's constant creation? In what moment today will you remind yourself that 'I am not self-made; I am being made every instant'?
?
If the agent cannot affect the quiddity, then he cannot affect existence—meaning your actions (existence) flow from your essence. So if you find yourself sinning, it points to an issue in your very essence. What sin have you been comfortable with, and what does it reveal about your essence that you need to submit to Allah for change?

Two More Proofs Against the Agent's Limited Influence
Razi provides the second and third proofs and concludes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second and third proofs that the 'quiddity being described by existence' cannot be a real entity, leading to the conclusion that the agent influences both essence and existence.

Core Idea
If the description were real, it would cause infinite existents or negate agency entirely.

Razi continues his refutation of the objection by presenting two more proofs. The second proof shows that if the description were an existent, it would require its own description, leading to infinite regress and turning one existent into infinite. The third proof argues that the description itself would have a quiddity, so the same problem arises, ultimately negating all agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the second proof carefully. It involves the description being equal in existence but different in quiddity.
Note the argument that this leads to infinite regress.
2
Understand the third proof
The third proof: the description must have a quiddity, so it cannot be affected by the agent, leading to no effect at all.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Connect to the conclusion
After refuting the objection, Razi concludes that the agent influences both existence and quiddity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Philosophical Argument for Contingency from Time
Imam Razi presents a philosophical argument from time for the second premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of time having a beginning or end leads to the conclusion that time is contingent and thus requires an external cause.

Core Idea
Time's composite nature forces it to be contingent, not necessary.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument used by philosophers to establish that every possible being needs a cause. By examining the nature of time, Imam Razi shows that time cannot have a beginning or end, yet it is composed of successive instants, proving it is contingent. The argument is a key link in the larger proof for God's existence, though it cuts off mid-sentence in our source.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the flow of the argument. Note that the text cuts off at the end.
The passage is incomplete; the conclusion is missing, but the structure is clear.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage has two clear parts: first, demonstrating that time has no beginning or end; second, classifying time as either necessary or possible, and ruling out necessity due to composition.
Pay attention to the logical steps: if time had a beginning, a contradiction arises; similarly for an end.
3
Ask: why does composition rule out necessity?
Consider why something composed of parts cannot be necessary in itself. This is a key principle in kalam: the necessary being must be simple and without composition.
This connects to the broader argument for a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويمكن تقرير هذه المقدمة بناء على قول الفلاسفة من وجه آخر : وذلك أنهم قالوا : الزمان يمتنع أن يكون له مبدأ ومنتهى لأنا لو فرضنا للزمان أولا لكان عدمه سابقاً على وجوده بالزمان والسبق بالزمان لا يحصل إلا مع وجود الزمان فيلزم أن يقال : الزمان كان موجودًا قبل أن كان موجوداً، وذلك محال.
،
وهكذا لو فرضنا للزمان منتهى لكان عدمه حاصلا بعد وجوده، بعدية بالزمان والبعدية بالزمان لا تحصل إلا مع وجود الزمان فيلزم أن يقال: الزمان موجود بعد أن لم يكن موجوداً، وذلك محال، فثبت أن عدم الزمان محال.
وإذا ثبت هذا قالوا : الزمان إما أن يكون واجبًا لذاته وإما أن يكون ممكنا لذاته، والأول باطل ؛ لأنه مركب من آنات متوالية منقضية وكل ما كان كذلك لم يكن واجبًا لذاته، فهو إذن ممكن لذاته والممكن إما أن يقع لغيره أو لذاته، أو لا لغيره ولا لذاته،
And it is possible to establish this premise based on the philosophers' statement from another aspect: for they said: Time is impossible to have a beginning and an end, because if we suppose for time a first, then its non-existence would be prior to its existence in time, and temporal priority occurs only with the existence of time, so it would be necessary to say: time existed before it existed, which is impossible. Similarly, if we suppose for time an end, then its non-existence would occur after its existence, a temporal posteriority, and temporal posteriority occurs only with the existence of time, so it would be necessary to say: time exists after it did not exist, which is impossible. So it is established that the non-existence of time is impossible. When this is established, they said: Time is either necessary in essence or possible in essence. The first is false because it is composed of successive, passing instants, and everything that is so is not necessary in essence. So it is therefore possible in essence. The possible either occurs by another or by itself, or by neither other nor itself—
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative way to establish the second premise (that every possible needs a cause) using a philosophical argument about time. The argument begins by showing that time cannot have a beginning or end: if time had a first moment, then before that moment time would have been non-existent, but that 'before' would itself be a temporal relation requiring time, creating a contradiction. Similarly for an end. Therefore, time's non-existence is impossible. Then the argument proceeds: since time is not necessary (because it is composed of successive instants), it must be possible. But if it is possible, then something must influence it. The text cuts off at the classification of possible causes (by another, by itself, or neither). The argument likely concludes that time requires an external effecter, thus supporting the second premise that every possible needs a preponderator. However, the lesson is incomplete as presented.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time could be self-subsistent and necessary.
Correction: This argument shows time is composed of successive instants, so it cannot be necessary; it must be contingent and dependent on an external cause.

Checkpoint

B) Time is impossible.
C) The non-existence of time is impossible, so time must be possible or necessary.
D) Time is an illusion.

Key Takeaway
The philosophical argument from time demonstrates that time is contingent and therefore requires an external cause, supporting the principle that every contingent being needs a preponderator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The argument shows that something seemingly fundamental like time cannot be self-sufficient. In what areas of your life do you act as if things are independent of Allah? What would change if you truly internalized that everything—including time—depends on Him?
?
If time had a beginning and end as the argument shows impossible, yet the universe had a beginning, how does that reshape your understanding of Allah's relationship to time? Does your prayer life reflect that He is beyond time and yet intimately involved in your moments?

The Impossibility of Causal Influence on Essence, Existence, or Attribution
Razi systematically eliminates all possible loci of causal influence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that no form of causal influence (ta'thir) can affect essence, existence, or the attribution of existence to essence, thereby proving that the very concept of external causation in this sense is logically impossible.

Core Idea
All possible loci of causal influence are impossible, so causality itself is impossible.

Imam al-Razi presents a rigorous logical proof that the concept of 'influence' (ta'thir) cannot be coherently applied to any aspect of a thing: neither its essence, its existence, nor the connection between them. By systematically eliminating each candidate, he concludes that the very notion of external causation is untenable. This argument is crucial for understanding the Ash'ari critique of causal necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possible loci of influence
Read the passage and note the three candidates: essence (mahiyya), existence (wujud), and the attribution of essence by existence (ittisaf al-mahiyya bil-wujud).
The passage explicitly enumerates these three.
2
Understand why influence on essence is already ruled out
Razi references a previous argument that influence on essence is impossible. We assume that conclusion; focus on the other two.
This is given without proof here.
3
Examine the argument against influence on existence
Read Razi's brief argument: if influence were on existence, then assuming the agent's absence would make existence cease to be existence, which is absurd.
This shows existence cannot be independently affected.
4
Analyze the two refutations of influence on attribution
Razi gives two reasons why influence on the connection between essence and existence is impossible. The first leads to regress (tasalsul); the second reapplies the same tripartite division to the supposed additional entity, also leading to impossibility.
These arguments show the third option is also incoherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة: يكون كون السواد سوادا واقعا بالفعل، وأما أنه لا يجوز أن يكون تأثير المؤثر في الوجود لأن عند تقدير عدم المؤثر يلزم أن يخرج الوجود عن كونه وجوداً، وذلك أيضًا محالا قررناه في الماهية. وأما أنه لا يجوز أن يكون تأثير المؤثر في اتصاف الماهية بالوجود فذلك لوجهين: الأول: أن اتصاف الماهية بالوجود ليس أمرًا زائدا على الماهية، وعلى الوجود، إذ لو كان زائدا لكانت له ماهية وله وجود، وكان اتصاف تلك الماهية بذلك الوجود زائدا عليه، ولزم التسلسل. الثاني: أن بتقدير أن تكون الموصوفية أمرًا زائدا على الماهية وعلى الوجود حصلت لها ماهية ووجود فحينئذ يعود التقسيم المذكور، وهو أن المؤثر إما أن يؤثر في ماهيته أو في وجوده أو في موصوفية ماهيته بوجوده والكل محال، فثبت أن المؤثر لو أثر لكان إما أن يؤثر في الماهية أو في الوجود أو في موصوفية الماهية بالوجود، والكل محال، فكان القول بالتأثير محالا.
The Third Issue: That blackness is blackness is actual. As for that it is not permissible that the influence of the agent is upon existence, because if the agent is assumed absent, existence would cease to be existence, and that is also impossible as we established regarding the essence. As for that it is not permissible that the influence of the agent is upon the attribution of the essence by existence, that is due to two ways: First: That the attribution of the essence by existence is not an additional thing beyond the essence and beyond existence, since if it were additional, it would have an essence and an existence, and the attribution of that essence by that existence would be additional to it, and regress would follow. Second: That assuming the state of being qualified is an additional thing beyond the essence and beyond existence, then the essence would have an essence and existence; then the aforementioned division returns, namely that the agent either influences its essence, or its existence, or the qualification of its essence by its existence, and all are impossible. So it is established that if the agent were to influence, it would either influence the essence, or the existence, or the qualification of the essence by existence, and all are impossible. Thus the statement that there is influence is impossible.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage contains a proof against the possibility of causal influence (ta'thir) by examining what influence could possibly affect. The author, Imam al-Razi, considers three candidates: the essence (mahiyya), existence (wujud), and the attribution (ittisaf) of essence by existence. He argues that influence cannot be upon existence because if the agent were removed, existence would cease, which is not self-evident. Influence cannot be upon essence because that was already ruled out in a previous discussion. Then he focuses on the third candidate: the attribution of essence to existence. He gives two reasons against it. First, this attribution is not a separate thing from essence and existence; if it were, it would lead to regress because each attribution would itself require another attribution. Second, if it were separate, then the same partition would apply again: the influence would have to be on the essence of that attribution, or its existence, or its attribution—all impossible. Hence all three loci of influence are impossible, so the very notion of external influence (ta'thir) is impossible.

Key Terms
تأثير
ماهية
وجود
اتصاف
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Razi's argument denies all forms of causation, including concurrent causation or divine action.
Correction: Razi's argument specifically targets a philosophical notion of influence (ta'thir) where the agent's effect is upon the essence, existence, or their attribution. It does not directly deny that God creates things or that events have causes in a different sense.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's argument, why is it impossible for influence to be upon the attribution of essence by existence?
A) Because attribution is identical to existence
B) Because if attribution were a separate thing, it would lead to regress
C) Because the agent cannot affect something non-existent
D) Because existence is eternal

Key Takeaway
Razi's proof shows that the idea of an external agent 'influencing' a thing's essence, existence, or their connection is logically incoherent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something you worked hard for, do you ever feel that your effort or the means you used had real causal influence? How does Razi’s proof that no created thing can truly influence anything challenge that feeling? Will you consciously remind yourself that only Allah is the true agent?
?
Think of a recent situation where you prayed for something to change, but also relied strongly on a person, a medicine, or a plan. Did your heart truly believe that those means have no independent effect? What does it cost you to surrender that sense of control?
?
If it is impossible for any creature to have any real influence, then what does that mean for how you view your own actions and responsibilities? Are you willing to live as if you are only a locus of Allah’s power, with no independent causality—and still act with full effort? What would change in your daily life if you truly internalized this?

Razi's First Two Proofs That Influence Is Not Identical to Cause or Effect
Razi provides two arguments that the act of causing is not the same as the cause or effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first two arguments demonstrating that the act of causing (ta'thir) is not identical to the essence of either the cause or the effect.

Core Idea
Causation is not the same as the cause or the effect.

Razi begins the Third Question by framing a logical division: if influence exists, it must be either identical to the cause, the effect, or a third thing. He then sets out to refute the first two options. This lesson covers the first two proofs, which rely on conceivability and the nature of relations. These arguments are foundational for his broader critique of causal necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: a division followed by two proofs.
Pay attention to the terms 'مؤثر' and 'أثر'.
2
Understand the logical division
Identify the three possibilities Razi lists: influence is identical to the cause, identical to the effect, or a third thing. He will disprove the first two.
This division sets up the rest of the question.
3
Examine Proof One: Conceivability
Razi argues we can conceive the cause and effect while doubting whether the cause actually caused the effect. This shows that the causal relation is additional and not identical to either essence.
The example uses the Necessary Existent and the world.
4
Examine Proof Two: Influence as a Relation
Influence is a relation between cause and effect. A relation is posterior to its relata, and what is posterior is different. Therefore, influence cannot be identical to cause or effect.
This argument relies on the nature of relations as dependent and posterior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث: لو أثر شيء في شيء لكان تأثير المؤثر في الأثر إما أن يكون عين ذات المؤثر أو ذات الأثر أو يكون أمرًا ثالثًا مغايرا لهما والقسمان باطلان، فكان القول بالتأثير باطلا. وإنما قلنا إن كونه مؤثرًا يمتنع أن يكون عين ذات المؤثر، وذات الأثر بوجوه: الأول: أنه يمكننا أن نعقل ذات المؤثر وذات الأثر، مع الشك في كون ذلك المؤثر مؤثرًا في هذا الأثر مثلا: نعقل الموجود الذي هو واجب الوجود لذاته، ونعقل هذا العالم، ثم نشك في أن المؤثر في وجود هذا العالم هو ذلك الواجب أم واجب آخر أو شيء من معلولات واجب الوجود؟ والمعلوم غير ما هو غير معلوم فهذه المؤثرية التي هي غير معلومة مغايرة لذات المؤثر، ولذات الأثر، الذي هو معلوم. الثاني: إن تأثير المؤثر في الأثر نسبة بين ذات الأثر وذات المؤثر، والنسبة بين شيئين متأخرة عن تينك الذاتين، والمتأخر عن الشيء مغاير لذلك الشيء.
The Third Question: If something influenced something, the influence of the agent upon the effect would either be identical to the essence of the agent or the essence of the effect, or be a third thing different from both, and the two divisions are false, so the statement that there is influence is false. We state that it is impossible for the agent to be identical to the essence of the agent and the essence of the effect, through proofs: First: That we can conceive the essence of the agent and the essence of the effect, while doubting whether that agent is an agent upon this effect, for example: we conceive the existent that is necessary of existence by itself, and we conceive this world, then we doubt whether the agent upon the existence of this world is that necessary existent or another necessary existent or something from the effects of the necessary existent? The known is not what is unknown; so this agency which is unknown is different from the essence of the agent and from the essence of the effect, which is known. Second: That the influence of the agent upon the effect is a relation between the essence of the effect and the essence of the agent, and the relation between two things is posterior to those two essences, and that which is posterior to a thing is different from that thing.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage opens the Third Question (al-su'al al-thalith) which examines the nature of influence (ta'thir) if it were to exist. Razi first poses a division: if something influences something else, the influence must either be identical to the essence of the cause or the effect, or be a third thing distinct from both. He then sets out to disprove the first two options (that it is identical). He provides several proofs (wujuh). This lesson covers the first two proofs. The first proof is based on conceivability: we can conceive the cause (e.g., the Necessary Existent) and the effect (the world) while doubting whether the cause actually caused this effect. That doubt shows that the causal relation (agency) is not known by simply knowing the essences of cause and effect; hence agency is additional and distinct from both. The second proof argues that influence is a relation (nisba) between cause and effect. A relation is temporally posterior to the two relata, and what is posterior to something is different from that thing. So influence cannot be identical to either the cause or the effect.

Key Terms
تأثير
مؤثر
أثر
مغايرة
نسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the act of causing is just a property of the cause that is identical to its essence.
Correction: Razi shows we can conceive the cause without knowing its causal relation, and relations are posterior, so causation is distinct from the cause's essence.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason Razi gives to show that influence is not identical to the cause?
A) The cause and effect are identical in essence
B) We can conceive the cause and effect while doubting whether causation occurred
C) Influence is a substance
D) The effect is eternal

Key Takeaway
Razi's first two proofs establish that influence (ta'thir) is not identical to the essence of the cause or effect, using conceivability and the posterior nature of relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that we can conceive the Necessary Existent (God) and the world while doubting whether God is the agent of this world. When you look at the world around you, do you take for granted that God is the direct cause of every event? Would your faith be shaken if you couldn't immediately see the causal link? What does this lesson demand from you regarding how you attribute events to God?
?
If causation is not part of God's essence but a relation posterior to His being, then God's act of creation is not necessary but voluntary. Does this comfort you—knowing that God created out of free will—or does it unsettle you to think that God's essence does not necessitate creation? How does this reshape your gratitude towards Him?
?
The second proof says that influence is a relation posterior to the relata. In your daily life, do you sometimes confuse your actions (causes) with their outcomes (effects), thinking that you own the results? How can this lesson free you from anxiety about outcomes, knowing that the causal link is not inherent in you but is a separate relation from God?

Two Proofs That Agency Is Not the Cause or Effect
Razi gives two more proofs that agency is distinct from cause and effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two additional proofs from Imam Razi that the act of causing (influence) is a real attribute distinct from the essence of the cause and the effect.

Core Idea
Agency is an attribute distinct from the cause's essence, proven by logic and explanatory practice.

Imam Razi continues his third question on causality with two new proofs. The third proof uses the logical distinction between a subject and its attribute. The fourth proof examines how we actually explain effects—by citing agency, not mere existence. Together, they show that influencing is something real beyond the cause and effect themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the two numbered proofs (الثالث and الرابع) and the concluding statement.
The passage is dense; take your time to let the structure sink in.
2
Examine the third proof (subject-attribute distinction)
Identify how Razi uses the fact that we describe the cause as an 'agent'—the described (ذات المؤثر) is different from the attribute (كونه مؤثرًا). This shows agency is not identical to the cause's essence.
Think of any description: the describer and the attribute are logically distinct.
3
Analyze the fourth proof (explanatory practice)
Notice that we explain an effect by saying 'Allah created it' (by His agency), not 'Allah exists'. The truth of one explanation and falsehood of the other proves that agency is a distinct explanatory factor.
If agency were just existence, both explanations would be equally valid—but they aren't.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Read the concluding sentence: the four proofs together refute the claim that being an agent is merely a verbal expression for the essences of cause and effect. The distinctness of agency is established.
This sets up the next section that will ask whether agency is an additional attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: وهو أنا نصف ذات المؤثر بكونه مؤثرًا في ذلك الأثر، والموصوف مغاير للصفة. الرابع: وهو أنا نعلل الأثر بذات المؤثر، بل بكونه مؤثراً، فإنا نقول: إنما وجد هذا الأثر لأن الباري تعالى أوجده وأبدعه وخلقه، ولا نقول: إنما وجد هذا الأثر لأن الباري تعالى موجود وواجب الوجود، وصدق النفي والإثبات يوجب التغاير. فبطل بهذه الوجوه أنم يقال كون المؤثر مؤثرًا في الشيء عبارة عن ذات المؤثر، وذات الأثر.
Third: That we describe the essence of the agent as being an agent in that effect, and the described is different from the attribute. Fourth: That we explain the effect by the essence of the agent, but rather by its being an agent, for we say: This effect only came to exist because Allah the Exalted brought it into being, originated it, and created it, and we do not say: This effect only came to exist because Allah the Exalted exists and is necessary of existence. The truth of negation and affirmation necessitates difference. So it is proven by these proofs that saying 'the agent is an agent in something' is an expression for the essence of the agent and the essence of the effect.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage continues the Third Question with two further proofs that influence is not identical to the cause or effect. The third proof uses the logical distinction between the subject (al-mawsuf) and the attribute (al-sifa): we describe the essence of the cause as being an agent (mu'aththir), and that description is an attribute distinct from the subject; therefore the agency is not identical to the essence. The fourth proof appeals to explanatory practice: we explain the existence of the effect by saying 'Allah created it' (i.e., by His agency), not by saying 'Allah exists'. The difference between a true explanation and a false one shows that agency is a distinct factor. The passage then concludes that the four proofs together establish that the statement 'the agent is an agent in something' does not reduce to the mere essences of cause and effect; rather, agency is something additional (but this conclusion is preliminary, as the next section will examine whether it can be a separate attribute).

Key Terms
مؤثر
أثر
مغايرة
تعليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that calling something an 'agent' is just another way of referring to its essence, with no extra meaning.
Correction: Razi shows through logical distinction (described vs. attribute) and explanatory practice (we explain by agency, not by existence) that agency is a real attribute distinct from the essence.

Checkpoint

C) The fact that we describe the cause as an agent
D) The necessity of existence for the cause

Key Takeaway
Influence is a real attribute distinct from the cause and effect, proven by the subject-attribute distinction and by explanatory necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
If agency is a real attribute added to your essence, then every action you take is a genuine expression of that attribute. Are you living as if your choices are trivial labels or as real attributes that define your eternal state? What one decision today must reflect that truth?
