Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Celestial bodies are immutable because they are perfect and unchanging.
Correction: Razi argues that celestial bodies are contingent like earthly elements, so they can undergo perforation, mending, and transformation.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion about celestial bodies derived from the argument about equal qualities?
A) Celestial bodies are eternal and unchanging.
B) Celestial bodies are composed of the same elements as earthly bodies.
C) Celestial bodies can undergo perforation, mending, and transformation.
D) Celestial bodies have their own unique essence different from earthly bodies.

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument establishes that celestial bodies are fundamentally similar to earthly bodies in their potential for change, challenging the view of their immutability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that celestial spheres, despite their apparent permanence, are as changeable as earth, water, air, and fire. What in your life do you treat as permanent and unchangeable, even though it is as contingent as the celestial spheres? Are you willing to accept that your own identity, circumstances, or even the world around you could be completely overturned by God's power?
?
The objection in this passage rests on the idea that each individual's 'particularization' makes them unique and unrepeatable. Yet Razi shows that particularization is itself an accident, not essential. How does your own sense of uniqueness and personal identity stand before God? If everything about you that you consider 'you' is ultimately accidental and perishable, what remains that is truly essential and enduring?
?
Razi's conclusion has profound implications for the Day of Resurrection: the same power that can transform earth into sky can transform your heart. What in your character or habits do you assume is 'fixed' or 'natural' that you have never truly surrendered to God's power to change? What would it look like to genuinely believe that God can remake you completely?

Bodies Require a Particularizer for Their Specific Qualities
Razi establishes that bodies' specific qualities are contingent and need a particularizer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why every body's specific place, shape, and state are contingent and thus require a particularizer.

Core Idea
All specific properties of bodies are contingent and need a cause.

Razi begins by stating that every body has a definite place, shape, and hardness or softness. These specific attributes could have been otherwise; they are contingent. In Islamic theology, contingent things require a particularizer (murajjiḥ) to determine which possibility is actual. This lesson establishes that foundation before examining what that particularizer could be.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the three necessary qualities of bodies
The passage states that every body has a specific place, shape, and state (hard or soft). Find these in the text and understand why they are necessary.
3
Understand why these qualities are contingent
Razi argues that the specificity of each body's place, shape, and state could have been different; therefore they are contingent (jāʾizāt). Contingent things need a particularizer.
This connects to the broader argument for God's existence.
4
See the initial conclusion
The passage concludes that all bodies are in need of a determiner and particularizer for their places and qualities. This is the premise being established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة : وهي أن كل جسم يوجد فلا بد له من حيز معين، ولا بد له من شكل معين ولا بد وأن يكون صلبًا أو رخوا، وقد دللنا على أن اختصاص كل واحد من الأجسام بصفته المعينة لا بد وأن يكون من الجائزات ولا بد للجائز من مرجح ومخصص فثبت بهذا افتقار جميع الأجسام في أحيازها وصفاتها إلى مخصص ومرجح، ثم ذلك المرجح إن كان جسما أيضًا في حيزه المعين وشكله المعين وصفته المعينة إلى مخصص ومرجح، ثم ذلك المرجح إلى مخصص ومرجح، وذلك يفضي إلى التسلسل وهو محال كما سبق، فثبت افتقار جميع الأجسام في صفاتها إلى موجود ليس بجسم ولا جسماني، وإذا أردنا أن نبين أن ذلك الموجود واجب الوجود لذاته رجعنا إلى بعض ما ذكرناه في البرهان الأول.
The third premise: That every body that exists necessarily has a specific place (ḥayyiz), necessarily has a specific shape (shakl), and necessarily is either hard or soft. And we have demonstrated that the specificity of each body with its particular quality must be among the contingent matters (al-jāʾizāt), and the contingent necessarily has a particularizer (murajjiḥ) and determiner (mukhaṣṣiṣ). So by this it is established that all bodies, in their places and qualities, are in need of a determiner and particularizer. Then that particularizer, if it is also a body, in its particular place, shape, and quality, is in need of a determiner and particularizer. Then that particularizer [is in need of] a determiner and particularizer, and this leads to infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible as previously established. So it is established that all bodies, in their qualities, are in need of an existent that is neither a body nor a corporeal being (jismānī). And if we wish to demonstrate that that existent is necessary in itself (wājib al-wujūd li-dhātihi), we refer to some of what we mentioned in the first proof.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a premise (muqaddima) in a proof. It begins by stating a universal: every body necessarily has a specific location, shape, and state (hard or soft). These specific attributes are contingent (jāʾizāt)—they could have been otherwise. The contingent requires a particularizer (murajjiḥ) that selects one possibility over others. This particularizer, if it itself is a body, would in turn need its own particularizer for its location, shape, and qualities. This sets up a chain of particularizers. If this chain continues indefinitely, it leads to infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. Therefore, the chain must terminate at an existent that is not a body nor a corporeal being (jismānī). This establishes the existence of a non-corporeal entity. The passage notes that proving this entity is necessary in itself (wājib al-wujūd) can be done by referring back to the first proof.

Key Terms
حيز
شكل
صلب
رخو
جائز
مرجح
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say bodies need a particularizer?
A) Because bodies can change over time.
B) Because their specific properties are contingent and could have been otherwise.
C) Because all bodies are made of the same matter.
D) Because bodies are eternal.

Key Takeaway
Every body's specific place, shape, and state are contingent, so they require an external cause to determine them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body has a specific shape, place, and state. Do you live as though these are accidental or as though they were deliberately chosen for you by a wise Determiner? What would change in your daily attitude if you truly internalized that every detail of your physical existence was specifically particularized?
?
If the particularizer of all bodies is not the bodies themselves, then who or what is it? Have you ever honestly confronted the implication that the universe's very specificity demands a cause beyond it—and that you are accountable to that cause?
?
Think of a specific quality of your body that you dislike or take for granted. Can you see it as a contingent gift that could have been otherwise, and that was deliberately chosen for you? How does that reframe your gratitude or patience?

The Particularizer Must Be Non-Corporeal to Avoid Infinite Regress
Razi argues that the particularizer cannot be a body to avoid infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the chain of particularizers for bodies cannot continue infinitely and must end with a non-corporeal being.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is impossible; the ultimate particularizer must be non-corporeal.

Having established that bodies need a particularizer, Razi now considers what that particularizer could be. If it is itself a body, it would in turn require its own particularizer for its specific place, shape, and state. This would lead to an infinite chain of particularizers, which is logically impossible. Therefore, the original particularizer must be something that is not a body or a corporeal being—a non-corporeal entity.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Start from 'ثم ذلك المرجح' to the end. Notice the conditional structure: if the particularizer is a body, then it also needs a particularizer.
2
Understand the infinite regress argument
Razi shows that if each particularizer is a body, the chain goes on forever. He invokes the impossibility of infinite regress (tasalsul) which was proved earlier.
Infinite regress is a common argument against an endless chain of causes.
3
Draw the conclusion
Since infinite regress is impossible, the chain must end with a particularizer that is not a body nor a corporeal being. Thus, a non-corporeal existence is required.
4
Connect to the next step (optional)
Razi mentions that proving this existent is necessary in itself can be done by referring back to the first proof. This shows the interconnectedness of the arguments.
This is a bridge to the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة : وهي أن كل جسم يوجد فلا بد له من حيز معين، ولا بد له من شكل معين ولا بد وأن يكون صلبًا أو رخوا، وقد دللنا على أن اختصاص كل واحد من الأجسام بصفته المعينة لا بد وأن يكون من الجائزات ولا بد للجائز من مرجح ومخصص فثبت بهذا افتقار جميع الأجسام في أحيازها وصفاتها إلى مخصص ومرجح، ثم ذلك المرجح إن كان جسما أيضًا في حيزه المعين وشكله المعين وصفته المعينة إلى مخصص ومرجح، ثم ذلك المرجح إلى مخصص ومرجح، وذلك يفضي إلى التسلسل وهو محال كما سبق، فثبت افتقار جميع الأجسام في صفاتها إلى موجود ليس بجسم ولا جسماني، وإذا أردنا أن نبين أن ذلك الموجود واجب الوجود لذاته رجعنا إلى بعض ما ذكرناه في البرهان الأول.
The third premise: That every body that exists necessarily has a specific place (ḥayyiz), necessarily has a specific shape (shakl), and necessarily is either hard or soft. And we have demonstrated that the specificity of each body with its particular quality must be among the contingent matters (al-jāʾizāt), and the contingent necessarily has a particularizer (murajjiḥ) and determiner (mukhaṣṣiṣ). So by this it is established that all bodies, in their places and qualities, are in need of a determiner and particularizer. Then that particularizer, if it is also a body, in its particular place, shape, and quality, is in need of a determiner and particularizer. Then that particularizer [is in need of] a determiner and particularizer, and this leads to infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible as previously established. So it is established that all bodies, in their qualities, are in need of an existent that is neither a body nor a corporeal being (jismānī). And if we wish to demonstrate that that existent is necessary in itself (wājib al-wujūd li-dhātihi), we refer to some of what we mentioned in the first proof.

Commentary
This passage is a premise (muqaddima) in a proof. It begins by stating a universal: every body necessarily has a specific location, shape, and state (hard or soft). These specific attributes are contingent (jāʾizāt)—they could have been otherwise. The contingent requires a particularizer (murajjiḥ) that selects one possibility over others. This particularizer, if it itself is a body, would in turn need its own particularizer for its location, shape, and qualities. This sets up a chain of particularizers. Second Proof: The Present Moment as an Example
Imam Razi uses the present moment to argue for the conceivability of possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the nature of the present moment to argue for the conceivability of possibility.

Core Idea
The present moment's fleeting nature proves that possibility is conceivable.

This lesson presents Razi's second argument for the conceivability of possibility. He focuses on the present instant (al-an), which is the boundary between past and future. By showing that each 'now' must occur and then cease, Razi argues that such a pattern is rationally conceivable for all contingent events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow from the 'now' to the conclusion about all events.
The Arabic may have a typo: 'المستقيل' likely means 'المستقبل' (future).
2
Identify the argument's structure
Razi makes two claims about the present moment: it must be renewed (wajib al-tajaddud) when it occurs, and it must perish (wajib al-fana') thereafter. He then asks: if this is conceivable for parts of time, why not for all events?
The argument is analogical: from the pattern of the 'now' to all contingent things.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Consider why Razi believes the pattern of the 'now' can be extended. What property does the 'now' share with other events that makes the analogy valid?
The key is that both are contingent; the 'now' is a clear example of something that comes to be and passes away.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: وهو أن هذا الآن الذي هو آخر الماضي وأول المستقيل كما وجد يمتنع بقاؤه ; لأن الحاضر لا يعقل أن يكون هو عين المستقبل، فإذن هذا الآن واجب التجدد حين حدث، وواجب الفناء بعد ذلك، وإذا عقل هذا في الآنات التي هي أجزاء الزمان فلم لا يعقل مثله في جميع الحوادث ؟
The second: This ‘now’ which is the end of the past and the beginning of the future, as it occurs, its persistence is impossible; because the present cannot be conceived to be identical with the future. Therefore, this ‘now’ is necessarily renewed when it occurs, and necessarily perishes thereafter. And if this is conceivable in the ‘nows’ which are parts of time, then why is it not conceivable similarly in all events?
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument for the conceivability of possibility. Razi focuses on the ‘now’ (al-an), the present instant which is the boundary between past and future. He observes that each ‘now’ must occur and then immediately cease, and it cannot persist into the future. The present moment is thus necessarily renewed and then perishes. If we can conceive of this pattern in these temporal parts, then we should also be able to conceive of the same pattern (coming into existence and then passing away) in all contingent events. This shows that possibility, i.e., the ability to be and then not be, is rationally intelligible.

Key Terms
آن (an)
واجب التجدد (wajib al-tajaddud)
واجب الفناء (wajib al-fana')
حوادث (hawadith)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi is claiming that all events must follow the exact pattern of the present moment.
Correction: Rather, he is using the present moment as an analogy to show that such a pattern is conceivable, not that it is necessary for every event.

Checkpoint
What does the second argument use to demonstrate the conceivability of possibility?
A) The concept of necessary existence
B) The nature of the present moment
C) The impossibility of the world being eternal
D) The principle of non-contradiction

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's second proof demonstrates that the temporal nature of the present moment makes the concept of possibility rationally conceivable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The present moment slips away as soon as it arrives. What have you invested your heart in that is as fleeting as a 'now'? Will you keep clinging to it or release it to the One who never passes away?
?
Razi uses the impossibility of the present moment persisting to show that possibility is conceivable. If you truly believed that every moment of your life is a fresh creation that perishes instantly, how would your priorities change right now?
?
You cannot hold on to the present moment. Are you living as if you could? What specific attachment do you need to let go of today to live in light of this truth?

Razi's Argument for Inherent Possibility
Imam Razi argues that the possibility of the world arises from itself, not from an agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's third argument that the possibility of the world is intrinsic and not dependent on an agent.

Core Idea
The world's possibility arises from itself, not from an agent.

Imam Razi presents a crisp argument showing that the possibility of the world's existence at a given time cannot be caused by an agent, because it would be present even if the agent is assumed absent. This establishes that the concept of a thing coming into existence by itself is rationally conceivable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let the logical flow sink in.
Notice how Razi uses conditional reasoning.
2
Identify key terms
Look up the terms 'imkan', 'sabaq bi-l-‘adam', 'la yazal', and 'fa‘il'. These are central to the argument.
Understanding these terms is crucial for grasping the proof.
3
Map the logical steps
Break down the argument: first, the world cannot be eternal due to temporal priority of non-existence. Then, its possibility arises at a later time. This possibility is not from an agent because it obtains regardless of the agent's existence.
The key insight is that the possibility is independent of the agent.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding: Razi is not saying things create themselves. He is only arguing that the concept of possibility (imkan) is intrinsic and not caused by an agent.
This is a logical argument about possibility, not about actual creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : العالم بشرط كونه مسبوقا بالعدم سبقا زمانيًا يمتنع حصوله في الأزل ; لأن الجمع بين النقيضين محال، ثم في ما لا يزال حدث ذلك الإمكان وحدوث ذلك الإمكان ليس بالفاعل ; لأن كل ما كان حصوله بالفاعل كان ممتنع الحصول عند فرض عدم الفاعل وصحة وجود العالم في لا يزال حاصلة سواء حصل الفاعل أو لم يحصل، فعلمنا أنه حدثت هذه الصحة لا بالفاعل بل لذاته، فثبت أن حدوث الشيء لذاته معقول.
The third: The world, on the condition of being preceded by non-existence in a temporal priority, its occurrence in eternity is impossible; because combining two opposites is impossible. Then at the non-eternal [future endless duration], that possibility occurred, and the occurrence of that possibility is not from an agent; because everything that occurs from an agent is impossible to obtain upon the assumption of the absence of the agent, while the soundness of the existence of the world in the non-eternal is obtained whether the agent obtains or not. Thus we know that this soundness occurred not from an agent but from itself. So it is established that the coming into existence of a thing from itself is conceivable.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This is the third argument. Razi considers the world as temporally preceded by non-existence. He states that the world cannot exist in eternity (because that would imply combining existence and non-existence simultaneously). Then, at a later time (fi ma la yazal), the world's possibility arises. This possibility is not caused by an agent, because if it were, it would disappear if the agent were absent. But the possibility of the world's existence at that later time is present regardless of whether the agent exists or not (since the agent is eternal and always present). Therefore, the possibility occurs from the thing itself, not from an agent. This proves that the concept of a thing arising intrinsically (i.e., having possibility) is rational.

Key Terms
إمكان (imkan)
سبق بالعدم (sabaq bi-l-‘adam)
لا يزال (la yazal)
فاعل (fa‘il)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi is saying that things create themselves.
Correction: Razi is not arguing for self-creation; he is arguing that the possibility (imkan) of the world is inherent and not caused by an agent. The 'coming into existence from itself' refers to the possibility, not the actual existence.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, why is the possibility of the world not from an agent?
A) Because the world is eternal
B) Because the agent does not exist
C) Because the possibility occurs regardless of whether the agent exists
D) Because the agent is not powerful enough

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that the world's possibility is not caused by an agent, because it is present regardless of the agent's existence, and thus the concept of intrinsic possibility is rationally viable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that the possibility of the world arises from itself, not from an agent. Think about a personal weakness or sin you believe is impossible to overcome because of your circumstances. If the possibility of change is intrinsic to you as a creature of Allah, does that make you more responsible for seeking that change? What step will you take today to act on that possibility?
?
The passage shows that possibility remains even when the agent is hypothetically absent. In your life, do you often attribute your spiritual possibilities (like repentance or growth) to external factors like a good environment or teacher? Examine your reliance: could the possibility still be there even if those were taken away? How does this affect your trust in Allah's power to change you directly?

First Objection: The Problem of Human Agency
Razi disputes the premise of human origination of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first objection: the premise that humans originate their own actions is not granted, and actions could occur spontaneously without a cause.

Core Idea
If human actions can occur by coincidence, the inferential argument collapses.

Razi begins his critique by challenging the foundation of the inferential approach. The Mu'tazila assume that humans produce their own actions. Razi questions this, suggesting that actions may arise spontaneously without a cause, and challenges opponents to apply their own reasoning directly to the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection
Read the Arabic and translation of the first objection. Notice how Razi denies the premise and proposes an alternative explanation.
The alternative is 'by way of coincidence' (ittifaq).
2
Analyze the logical move
Razi does not prove his alternative; he merely argues that if the opponent claims coincidence is impossible, they should apply that directly to the world.
This shifts the burden of proof.
3
Connect to the larger argument
Reflect on how this objection undermines the analogy between human actions and the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى تمسكهم بهذا القياس سؤالات صعبة : أولها : أنا لا نسلم أن الواحد منا مُحْدِثٌ لأفعال نفسه، وسيأتي تقريره إن شاء الله تعالى في مسألة خلق الأفعال، ولم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن أفعالنا تحدث عند قصودنا ودواعينا لا بقدرتنا وداعيتنا، بل على سبيل الاتفاق من غير مؤثر ؟ فإن قالوا : الحدوث على سبيل الاتفاق محال فليذكروا ذلك ابتداء في حدوث العالم حتى يدل حدوث العالم على وجود الفاعل من غير حاجة إلى ذكر هذا القياس. وثانيهما هب أن أفعالنا واقعة بقدرتنا ولكن لا نسلم أن علة الحاجة هي الحدوث، ودليله الوجوه الكثيرة التي قدمناها في أن الحدوث لا يمكن أن يكون علة للحاجة، ولا جزءًا لهذه العلة ولا شرطا لها . ثالثها هب أن حدوثها علة لحاجتها إلينا لكن لم لا يجوز أن تكون العلة ذلك الحدوث، أعني : ماهية الحدوث بشرط إضافتها إلى تلك الأفعال الخاصة - والعالم وإن حصل فيه الحدوث لكن لم يحصل فيه ذلك الشرط الخاص، فلا يلزم أن يحصل فيه الحاجة إلى الفاعل.
And upon their adherence to this analogy there are difficult objections: First: We do not grant that any one of us is an originator (muhdith) of his own actions, and its confirmation will come, God willing, in the issue of the creation of actions. Why may it not be said that our actions occur at the time of our intentions and motives, not by our power and motive, but rather by way of coincidence (ittifaq) without any agent? If they say: occurrence by way of coincidence is impossible, then let them mention that from the outset concerning the occurrence of the world, so that the occurrence of the world indicates the existence of an agent without needing to mention this analogy. Second: Granted that our actions occur by our power, but we do not grant that the cause of the need is origination (huduth). Its proof is the many aspects we have presented that origination cannot be a cause of need, nor a part of this cause, nor a condition for it. Third: Granted that their origination is a cause for their need to us, but why may it not be that the cause is that origination, meaning the essence of origination with the condition of its addition to those particular actions? The world, even though origination occurs in it, yet that particular condition does not occur in it, so it does not follow that the need for an agent occurs in it.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents three objections (sualat) raised by Razi against the inferential argument of the Mu'tazila. The inferential argument uses human actions as a model: since humans originate their actions, and the world is similar in being originated, the world must need a cause. Razi objects: (1) The premise that humans originate their own actions is disputed; actions may occur spontaneously without a cause. If the opponent claims such spontaneity is impossible, they should apply that directly to the world rather than rely on the problematic analogy. (2) Even if humans originate actions, it does not follow that the cause of need is origination (huduth) itself; Razi refers to earlier arguments that huduth is not a cause, part, or condition of need. (3) Even if huduth is the cause, its effect may be specific to actions with a particular condition (e.g., being human actions); the world's huduth lacks that condition, so the inference fails. These objections target the core of the inferential method.

Key Terms
سؤال
حدوث
اتفاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans are the sole originators of their own actions.
Correction: Razi argues that this premise is not granted; actions could occur by coincidence without a cause.

Checkpoint
What alternative does Razi propose for how human actions occur?
A) By divine intervention
B) By way of coincidence without an agent
C) By human power alone
D) By predetermined fate

Key Takeaway
Razi's first objection undermines the inferential argument by casting doubt on the premise that humans cause their own actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action—like making a decision or moving your hand—do you feel certain that you are the originator of that act? What if that feeling is merely an illusion? How would that change your sense of responsibility for your deeds?
?
Razi questions the inferential argument by doubting a premise about human agency. Is there any premise in your own belief in God that you have never critically examined? Could a single doubt shake your entire conviction?
?
If actions truly occur by coincidence, then what meaning does your struggle to obey God have? But if God creates your actions (as Ash'aris hold), then how can you sincerely repent for sins you did not originate? Let these questions unsettle you until you seek a deeper understanding.

Second Objection: Is Origination Really the Cause of Need?
Razi denies that huduth is the cause of need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second objection: even if humans originate actions, origination (huduth) is not necessarily the cause of need for an agent.

Core Idea
Huduth is not proven to be the cause, part, or condition of need.

Granting for argument's sake that humans cause their actions, Razi now attacks the central link: the claim that origination (huduth) is the reason why something needs a cause. He refers to earlier arguments that huduth cannot be the cause, part, or condition of need.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
Read the Arabic and translation of the second objection. Notice the concessive 'huba' (granted) and the denial that huduth is the cause of need.
Razi is not accepting the premise, but showing that even if it were true, the argument fails.
2
Understand the technical terms
Identify the three roles Razi denies to huduth: cause (illa), part (juz'), and condition (shart). These are key concepts in kalam.
3
Reflect on the implication
If huduth is not the cause of need, then the world's huduth does not imply it needs a creator. This removes the basis for the analogy.

What does Razi claim about huduth (origination) in the second objection?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If huduth (origination) is not the necessary cause of your need for Allah, then what is the real basis for that need? Have you ever truly examined why you depend on Him? Does your worship reflect a continuous reliance or just a response to having been created?
?
This lesson separates being created from being needy. In your daily life, do you only turn to Allah when you remember He created you, or do you feel a constant, moment-by-moment need for Him? How might Razi's objection reshape your understanding of what it means to be in need of Allah?
?
Consider: if huduth does not automatically create need, then your very existence is not enough to explain your dependence on Allah. What does this say about your connection to Him? Are you willing to admit that your need is deeper and more intimate than you usually acknowledge? How will you respond to that reality today?

Third Objection: The Specific Condition of Origination
Razi points out a missing condition in the world's origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third objection: even if huduth causes need, it does so only with a specific condition that the world lacks.

Core Idea
The world's origination lacks the particular condition that makes need apply.

Razi's final objection accepts for argument's sake that huduth is the cause of need, but only when combined with a specific condition (e.g., being a human action). The world's origination lacks that condition, so the analogy fails. This is a powerful refutation of the inferential method.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the phrase 'with the condition of its addition to those particular actions.'
This condition is key.
2
Analyze the structure
Razi distinguishes between huduth in itself and huduth with a specific condition. The world has huduth but not the condition, so the inference doesn't apply.
3
Consider the impact
This objection shows that analogies require similarity in all relevant aspects; here a crucial aspect is missing.

Source Text

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the world is originated, it must need an agent just as human actions do.
Correction: Razi shows that the condition attaching huduth to human actions may be absent in the world, breaking the analogy.

What condition does Razi say is missing in the world's origination?
A) The condition of being created by God
B) The condition of being a particular action like human actions
C) The condition of being temporal
D) The condition of being observable

The third objection shows that analogies fail if a necessary condition is not shared.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the arguments for God's existence, are you aware of the hidden conditions that might affect whether an argument applies to God versus created things? Have you ever accepted an analogy without checking if the conditions are the same? Resolve to examine at least one argument you have used with a critical eye for conditions.
?
In your own life, do you assume that because something is true in one context, it must be true in another? Reflect on a recent situation where you made such an assumption. Will you commit to testing your assumptions by looking for missing conditions?
?
The objection shows that even if a cause seems universal, it may be conditioned. How does this challenge your confidence in your own reasoning about God? Are you willing to humbly admit that your understanding may have overlooked crucial conditions? Pray for guidance to see clearly.

Razi’s Refutation of Christ’s Creative Power
Razi briefly refutes the claim of Christ having creative power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi’s brief refutation of the claim that God gave Christ creative power and the principle that creation belongs solely to God.

Core Idea
Only God creates—no one else shares this power.

In this short passage, Imam al-Razi quickly dismisses a Christian theological claim. He asserts that the notion that God bestowed upon Jesus the power to create bodies is false. The reason is the fundamental Islamic principle that creation (ījād) is an exclusive attribute of God. Razi announces that he will later provide a detailed proof for this exclusivity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Note the brevity and directness of Razi’s style.
This is a single sentence refutation with a forward reference.
2
Identify the claim and response
The passage mentions a specific claim about Christ. Identify what the claim is and how Razi responds to it. Pay attention to the word 'also' (أيضًا) which connects to previous refutations.
3
Understand the exclusive creation principle
Razi bases his refutation on the principle that only God can create. Consider why this is a foundational doctrine in Sunni theology. He promises a future proof, so this is just a preview.
Creation (ījād) is an exclusive attribute of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القول بأنه تعالى خلق فيه القدرة على خلق الجسم فهذا أيضًا باطل لأنا سنبين أنه لا قدرة لأحد على الإيجاد إلا الله تعالى.
As for the claim that the Most High created in him the power to create bodies, this is also false, because we will show that no one has the power to bring into existence except God Most High.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This is a brief refutation of the Christian claim that God bestowed upon Jesus the power to create bodies. Imam al-Razi dismisses it as false, noting that he will later demonstrate (in a subsequent section) that no being has the power to create except God alone. This argument relies on a broader theological principle: creation (ījād) is exclusive to God. Thus, the claim that Jesus had such power is rejected.

Key Terms
القدرة
الإيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi provides a full proof for the exclusivity of creation in this passage.
Correction: Razi only states the refutation and says he will prove it later ('سنبين'). The detailed proof is given in a subsequent section.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi consider this view false?
A) Because Christ was a prophet
B) Because only God can create
C) Because he will prove it later
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi refutes the claim of Christ's creative power by asserting that creation belongs exclusively to God, and he promises a future proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if you have ultimate control over outcomes in your life, as if you are the creator of your own success? Reflect on an area where you need to relinquish that pretense and acknowledge God as the only Creator.
?
Razi refutes the claim that anyone else has power to create. In your daily life, what 'creations' do you attribute to yourself or others that belong solely to Allah? How can you realign your heart to recognize His exclusive power?
?
The passage calls out a specific heretical claim. Are there subtle beliefs in your own heart—perhaps about your own ability to bring about change—that contradict this core truth? Write down one specific thought pattern you need to correct.
